Abstract (Summary) 
In the present MSc thesis, a techno-economic assessment of different configurations of 
Concentrated Solar Power (CSP) systems integrated with Pumped Thermal Electricity 
Storage (PTES) is conducted. Such systems are capable of storing electrical energy from 
intermittent renewable sources, like wind and solar Photovoltaics (PV) in man-made 
tanks that feature a fluid or solid storage medium. In the same tanks, thermal energy 
produced by the Solar Field component of the CSP plants may also be stored. Tanks with 
a sufficient thermal capacity can then power a discharging cycle that provides electricity 
to the grid. The discharging power cycle may also use thermal energy from the Solar Field 
directly as input. The nominal electrical output from the discharging cycle was taken to 
be equal to 50 MWel, as this is a common capacity for the majority of operational CSP 
plants worldwide. 
In the first combined system, System 1, a closed-Brayton discharging cycle and a reverse-
Brayton heat pump for charging were examined. The two cycles were linked through 
energy balances in the Cold and the Hot Storage. In the Cold Storage, the same amount of 
heat charged by the heat pump was then discharged through the power cycle. In the Hot 
Storage, additional thermal energy input from the Solar Field was considered in the 
energy balances. The obtained results showed that the operation of this combined system 
is only reasonable for low Solar Field thermal inputs, and that an increment in this input 
can cause significant irreversibilities and exergy destruction in the thermodynamic 
cycles.  
In the second combined system, System 2, the two cycles were assumed to be independent 
from each other, since they were not linked in the Cold Storage. The Cold Storage tanks 
were replaced by cooling water at constant temperature in the low-temperature parts of 
the cycles, nonetheless the Hot storage tanks were assumed to be identical to those from 
System 1 and at the same temperature levels as them. A Rankine power cycle for 
discharging and a two-stage interheated reverse-Brayton heat pump cycle for charging 
were assumed. Moreover, a renewable Solar PV farm was considered to be part of the 
system, responsible for providing electricity directly to the grid and for charging the heat 
pump with the excess electrical load. This is why the PV farm was designed to have a 
higher electrical output than 50 MWel. The component interactions were described in 
detail in an Operational strategy scheme which was the basis for the Off-design model of 
the system. This Off-design model was used to describe the performance of the System 
under different Solar Field thermal outputs and different PV electrical outputs for a whole 
year. The annual Capacity Factor was estimated, and it was found to be as high as 86.3% 
in the base case. In the base case, the goal was to over-dimension the components in order 
to maximize the attained Capacity Factor, which resulted in a particularly high Levelized 
Cost of Electricity (LCOE) of 188.29 $/MWh. Lastly, a sensitivity analysis was conducted 
in order to estimate those PV, Heat pump and Solar Field capacities that bring about the 
lowest LCOE for the system. The results suggested that intermediate Solar Field sizes and 
smaller PV and Heat pump sizes produce the ideal combination in terms of LCOE. It was 
observed that having PV system with a higher capacity made the Capacity factor more 
dependent on the heat pump, since a lot of excess electricity was charged through it. The 
opposite was observed for smaller PV sizes. The LCOE is essentially a ratio of the incurred 
(annualized) cost over the electrical output of a plant. In the case of System 2 it was 
concluded that the decrease in cost was more crucial for reaching the optimal LCOE than 
the respective decrease in the electrical output. This explains why smaller capacity 
components (PV, Heat pump) produced the lowest LCOE. This behavior can be attributed 
to the fact that the Investment costs of the system components (especially the heat pump 
and Solar Field) were particularly high.

1 Introduction - Background 
The energy landscape globally is currently undertaking major changes. To name a few, 
electrification is pushed more and more as a cost-competitive and climate friendly 
alternative for a wide variety of industries and more energy efficient buildings are 
promoted with the aim of cutting down energy consumption. Moreover, electric and fuel-
cell vehicles are being developed as a viable alternative to petrol and diesel for ground 
transport, and state-of-the-art electro- and biofuels are under research and development, 
mostly for the shipping and aviation sectors. All of the above share the need for electricity 
-preferably “green”. Consequently, it can be argued that providing reliable electrical 
energy when needed is a necessity. There are countless research topics (and opinions) 
only on this field, however it can be agreed that merging different technologies and 
focusing the effort on trying to avoid wasting the energy that is produced is of the utmost 
importance. This chapter will investigate why these combined systems could be relevant 
and will give a brief description of their components. Finally, the objective of the MSc 
thesis will be given in the last section.  
 
1.1 Emissions reduction and Policies 
During the last decades, in spite of the fact that Renewable electricity generation is 
experiencing a rapid increment globally, the emission of greenhouse gases appears to 
continue growing as well. Among the pollutants, Carbon Dioxide (CO2), Methane (CH4) 
and Nitrous oxide (N2O) are the most prominent one [1]. And while the last two are mainly 
emitted as a result of agricultural and industrial activity, the situation with Carbon 
Dioxide, which accounts 76% of the air pollutants emitted [1], appears to be more 
alarming. Figure 1-1 below gives an illustration of the breakdown of the global CO2 
emissions:  
 
 
Figure 1-1: The main sources of Carbon Dioxide emissions worldwide. Source: [2] 
Figure 1-1 shows that energy related emissions are particularly important, followed by 
those coming from the Manufacturing Industry, Households and Transportation. 
Moreover, it appears that a significant portion of the energy related emissions is the 
byproduct of electricity and heat generation activities [3]. 
Figure 1-2 below shows the major pollutants worldwide, as well as a time series of CO2 
emission data: 
 
==PAGE:19==
15 
 
 
 
Figure 1-2: Emission time series data for selected emerging economies (left) and for selected advanced 
economies (right). Source: [4] 
From Figure 1-2 it is observed that China is undoubtedly and by far the country that 
contributes the most to global CO2 emissions, surpassing the combined emissions of the 
most advanced economies (USA, European Union and Japan) during the latest years. 
China also happens to be the sole country that significantly expanded its coal– fired power 
fleet in 2020, during the peak of the COVID-19 pandemic [5]. The emissions of India 
appear to rise too, nonetheless the slope of the curve is not as steep -at least for now.  
On the contrary, the strongest economies in the developed world, which have historically 
contributed heavily to global pollution, are trying to stop their streak by implementing 
various measures, such as quotas, taxes and by placing the “Green” Growth on top of their 
agendas. However, at post-pandemic times even the emissions from the Advanced 
countries are experiencing a steep rise. According to recent studies [5], this rebound effect 
can be attributed mostly to electricity generation related activities. The study shows that 
both the initial drop (2019) and the subsequent sharp rise (2021) in emissions that 
occurred before and after the COVID pandemic respectively were driven by the electricity 
demand itself, and by the fact that fossil-based generators became the main means to 
restore the production levels back to normal after the Pandemic worldwide. 
Unfortunately, it was coal, namely the most prominent polluter, that replaced gas in many 
occasions, owing to a rise in prices of the latter worldwide [5]. And this despite the fact 
that Renewable investments accounted for 83% of the energy projects on a global scale. 
More specifically, the total amount of produced energy from Renewables was as high as 
700 TWh in 2020, as opposed to 381 in 2010 and 186 in 2000 [6]. Nevertheless, it is now 
uncertain whether the emissions trend of 2021 will continue during the coming years, 
and the outcome will also depend on the rate at which the global energy demand will rise 
during the following years. The current status shows a 30% increase in the last 20 years 
[7]. This leads to the direct conclusion that the deployment of low-carbon generation and 
energy storage technologies needs to speed up. Simultaneously, the implementation of 
Climate Change mitigation policies at United Nations level has to be enforced swiftly. 
With regards to the latter, the Kyoto Protocol, signed in 1997, was one major policy act 
that was initially agreed between 37 countries, obliging them to limit their emissions by 
5%, compared to the 1990 levels, between the years 2008-2012 (first amendment period) 
and 2013-2020 (second amendment period) [8]. The second and most important Act was 
the Paris Agreement, that was signed in 2015 and is agreed upon among 193 parties (as 
for the latest additions). The Paris Agreement has at its core the ambition to limit the 
world average temperature to 2 oC, ideally at 1.5 oC when compared to pre-industrial 
==PAGE:20==
16 
 
levels [9]. It also urges the parties to ensure that the global peak in temperature will be 
reached as soon as possible, to collaborate with each other for the common goal and to 
publish data on their performance and set specific targets (transparency) [9]. Lastly, the 
Agreement recognizes the fact that developing countries will need more time than the 
developed ones in balancing social growth with Climate Change mitigation targets. In the 
latest Glasgow Climate Pact, signed in 2021, the party-countries agreed that their 
individual targets for 2030 should be revisited with the aim of them being in alignment 
with the Paris Agreement targets [10]. The real impact of these policies has yet to be fully 
understood, even so it is undoubtedly a fact that more and more countries -mostly 
developed ones- are now deciding to push forward with new Renewable energy projects 
in the core of their agendas. 
As for the current status of deployment of Renewable energy sources by type, Figure 1-3 
gives an insight: 
 
 
Figure 1-3: The share of Renewable energy technologies in the global electricity generation mix. Source: [11]. 
The bar-chart illustrates that hydropower currently dominates the market, nevertheless 
more and more projects are now focused on wind turbines and mainly solar photovoltaics 
(PV), as these technologies are not limited to specific physical characteristics of a place, 
such as elevation and water abundance. In spite of the fact that solar PV and wind turbines 
are becoming more and more cost-attractive and therefore appealing, they do not come 
without their own problems. Most notably, intermittency and grid stability issues are 
relevant for these technologies [12]. And while interconnections, proper management of 
load usage from the demand side (e.g., electric vehicles charging) and peaking plants 
based on gas or biogas for sudden load changes have all been proposed as potential 
solutions to some extent, energy storage could also be a key to unlocking the full potential 
of intermittent renewable energy sources. An introduction to the role that Storage and 
Concentrated Solar Power systems (also shown in Figure 1-3) could play in the future 
either individually or combined is examined in the following three sections of the 
Introduction.   
==PAGE:21==
17 
 
1.2 Energy storage – Pumped Thermal Energy Storage systems 
The gradual replacement of traditional fossil-fueled, central power stations with smaller 
and geographically scattered Renewable Energy systems, like wind turbines and solar PV 
systems is definitely a positive change, however it does not come without some crucial 
disadvantages. The most characteristic among them is the intermittency that naturally 
accompanies the installation of these energy sources. Wind turbine operation relies on 
wind forecasts. Wind loads typically change within the time span of weeks, but also show 
seasonal variations, while solar PV systems feature daily operational patterns and tend to 
be susceptible to seasonal variations as well.  
Intermittency tends to cause a mismatch between generation and consumption of 
electricity, which poses a threat to the delicate balance between them and jeopardizes the 
frequency quality [13]. Maintaining this balance is the cornerstone of the majority of 
contemporary grids around the world. This problem could be solved by decoupling 
generation and consumption, using storage. The idea is that electricity can be charged into 
the Storage system at times when there is excess energy production, and be discharged 
during peak demand times [13], when generation is not sufficient. This way undesired 
situations like load shedding, blackouts and negative electricity prices could be avoided, 
effectively turning the intermittent sources into load following ones. 
When it comes to different Storage technologies, these may well be placed in categories 
based on the basic principle (Mechanical/Thermal, Electrochemical, Electromagnetic 
Chemical), based on the capacity of the system (large, small) or based on the maturity and 
availability of each technology. Chemical storage technologies use the process of 
electrolysis to store electricity in the form of secondary carriers, like hydrogen or 
synthetic gas [14]. These carriers are then able to release this energy in fuel cells or even 
in modified gas turbines. Such systems allow for sector coupling and long-term storage, 
but are still under development and suffer from low electricity-to-electricity conversion 
efficiencies (as low as 40-50%) and high costs [14]. Then, electromagnetic methods that 
rely on supercapacitors or superconductors are promising, nonetheless they are still 
under demonstration and thus have prohibitively high costs [14]. Electrochemical storage 
systems include all rechargeable batteries (secondary batteries) and have the conversion 
of chemical into electric energy through reversible electrochemical oxidation-reduction 
reactions [15] as their basic operating principle. They include all battery systems, like the 
Li-ion battery, the Lead-Acid battery, as well as Flow-batteries. Unlike the first two battery 
systems, which are very popular when it comes to short-term, small scale storage 
applications (e.g., cars, laptops), Flow batteries are suitable for large scale applications, 
nevertheless they suffer from poor lifetime (5-15 years) and high investment costs [16].  
Mechanical and Thermal methods are mostly suitable for large scale systems and involve 
the conversion of electrical energy into another stable and storable energy form, like 
potential or thermal energy [16]. Here, one may find more mature technologies such as 
Pumped Hydro Storage (PHS) and Compressed Air Energy Storage (CAES) that have 
already been installed, and others that are yet to be fully commercialized, like Gravity 
Energy Storage (GES), Liquid Air Energy Storage (LAES), and Pumped Thermal Electricity 
Storage (PTES) systems. PHS is the most adopted storage technology by a large margin, 
with 178 GW of readily installed capacity, owing to its simple design [16]. It uses a 
reversible hydraulic turbomachine, the pump-turbine that transfers water from a low-
level to a high-level reservoir during charging, and allows it to flow the opposite way 
during discharge to produce electricity. The requirement of a proper physical morphology 
and water availability are considered to be the most significant drawbacks of PHS, 
prohibiting its usage in certain locations [16]. 
As for CAES, the technology relies on pressurizing storing and depressurizing a stream of 
air. In a Diabatic-CAES the air is pressurized and stored inside natural cavities (e.g., salt 
==PAGE:22==
18 
 
caverns) during charging. Then it heats up using a fossil, backup stream (typically natural 
gas) and expands through a gas turbine to produce electricity during discharge [16]. 
Having the requirement for fossil fuel support, and specific physical ground morphology 
are considered to be the most serious drawbacks of CAES. A new type of CAES, named 
Adiabatic-CAES promises to sort out these issues to some extent, but it is still under 
Research and Development [16]. With regards to GES, at least three versions have been 
proposed with the aim of solving the topological needs of PHS, with limited success to this 
day [16]. Then, LAES suggests another interesting way to store energy by compressing 
and liquifying air during charge, and by heating it up and pressurizing it during discharge. 
This technology is regarded by some authors as a member of the PTES family [17],[18] 
which is debatable. Irrespective of that though, LAES is still impeded by high costs and 
slow response times [16].  
Before moving to PTES, which is the storage technology of interest in this MSc thesis, a 
comparison between the large-scale storage technologies mentioned above is established 
in Table 1 below:

Table 1 illustrates that Hydrogen and PTES storage offer energy densities that are 
substantially higher compared to the other technologies. The maturity of PHS and CAES 
technologies is reflected on their relatively low price, however Hydrogen and PTES also 
appear to be competitive in this field. When it comes to Round-trip efficiency and lifetime 
though, the advantage of PTES systems becomes clear, in spite of the fact that the round-
trip efficiency range of the PTES system shown in Table 1 appears to be slightly 
overestimated [17], [19]. An overview of the technology, the alternative systems that have 
been proposed and their components will be presented next. 
According to the definition of PTES, all systems that utilize a heat pump, run by electrical 
energy, to charge a hot storage by extracting heat from a cold storage and perform the 
reverse process to produce electricity in a heat engine, belong to this category [12]. Here, 
it has to be noted that the hot and cold storage reservoirs are typically man-made (e.g., 
insulated tanks). Under specific occasions ambient air may serve as the cold storage (heat 
source). All of the PTES alternative systems use a heat transfer medium that is subject to 
temperature changes and in some cases even phase cases. If a phase change occurs and a 
phase change material (PCM) is used [16], the PTES system is termed a latent heat PTES, 
whereas if only sensible heat transfer occurs, the system is called a sensible PTES. Latent 
heat PTES materials usually move from the solid to the liquid phase and vice-versa, and 
==PAGE:23==
19 
 
in case chemical reactions are also involved, the Storage type is termed Thermochemical 
Energy Storage (TCES) [16]. Latent heat PTES and TCES are more appropriate 
alternatives for smaller system capacities, up to 1 MW [16]. On the other hand, Sensible 
PTES systems may be scaled up further and thus offer an advantage in this case [16].  
Further, PTES systems can be categorized on the basis of the type of thermodynamic cycle 
(or cycles) that dominates the system. If Liquid Air cycles are excluded, PTES systems can 
be classified as Brayton PTES, Rankine (and Organic Rankine) PTES and Trans-critical 
PTES systems [17].   
Brayton PTES systems consist of two separate cycles, one for charging the two storages 
and one for discharging them. Figure 1-4 and Figure 1-5 below show the cycle 
configurations and the corresponding T-S (temperature-entropy) diagrams: 
 
 
Figure 1-4: The charging (1-4) and discharging (5-8) cycles in Brayton based PTES systems. Source: [18] 
 
Figure 1-5: The T-S diagrams of the charging (1-4) and discharging (5-8) cycle in Brayton PTES systems. 
Source: [18]. 
During the charging (heat pump mode) process, electricity is used to move heat from the 
low to the high temperature reservoir. To do so the gaseous working fluid receives heat 
from the Low temperature storage (1-2), before being compressed (2-3). Subsequently, it 
transfers the heat to the High temperature reservoir (3-4) and expands (4-1) back to its 
initial state to let the process to start again. During discharge (heat engine mode), a closed 
Brayton cycle is used to discharge the heat from the charged High temperature reservoir 
to the Low temperature reservoir. The working fluid accepts the heat from the Hot storage 
(5-6), expands through the turbine to produce mechanical and electrical work in the 
generator (6-7), transfers energy to the Cold storage (7-8) and is then recompressed back 
to its initial state. Figure 1-5 shows the irreversibilities that take place in the processes of 
compression and expansion, which are not isentropic. In an irreversible process the 
system and its surroundings cannot return exactly to their initial states if the process is 
==PAGE:24==
20 
 
reversed [20] and extra entropy is generated. Figure 1-5 also depicts the finite 
temperature differences between the points 1 and 8 for the cold storage and 3 and 6 for 
the hot storage. A balance needs to be maintained at all times between these temperature 
differences, as minimizing them would result in heat exchangers with large surface areas, 
which are uneconomical and cause substantial pressure drops in the working fluid. On 
the other hand, maximizing these differences would result in more compact, but 
inefficient heat exchangers and undesired exergy destruction.  
In terms of the Storage material, packed beds made of gravel (solid) or similar materials 
have been proposed, mostly owing to their availability and low-cost. However, the 
pressurized operation could add extra costs to the system, and the extreme thermal 
gradients that are formed through the material have to be constantly monitored with the 
aim of avoiding harming the medium [12]. The above issues can be tackled by choosing 
Liquid state Storage instead. In this case synthetic oils and molten salts are considered to 
be the best candidates [12], the former having an operational range from 12 to 400 oC 
(Therminol vp-1 [21]) and the latter from 250 to 565 oC (Nitrate molten salts, [12]). Liquid 
Storage Brayton based PTES systems can use one or two tanks for the hot and the cold 
storage respectively, although the two-tank approach is more popular. Finally, when it 
comes to choosing a working fluid for the Brayton and reverse Brayton cycles of the PTES, 
Nitrogen, Air and Argon are usually considered [17], [19], [12] in Literature. These gases 
behave like ideal gases at high temperatures and relatively high pressures, and thus the 
cycles are termed ideal Brayton cycles. Air and Nitrogen (N2) are very similar in most of 
their properties and Nitrogen is actually preferred in PTES applications, as it is free of 
pollutants and moisture [17]. Choosing between Nitrogen and Argon becomes more 
challenging however, as the former has superior heat capacity, Cp [22] and therefore 
improved performance in exchanging heat, while the latter has a superior specific heat 
ratio γ [22] (k in  [22]), which allows it to achieve greater efficiencies in Brayton cycles 
under smaller pressure ratios [23]. In liquid Storage Brayton based PTES, high pressure 
operation is adopted in many cases, with pressures up to 100 bar [17], in order to take 
advantage of the high energy density of the working fluid in this region and make the use 
of more compact turbomachines possible [17]. Operating under high pressures and 
relatively small pressure ratios makes the use of recuperation (internal heat exchange) 
attractive, as high-temperature heat at the outlet of the turbine can be utilized to preheat 
other streams, effectively reducing the irreversibilities [17]. An illustration of such a 
system will be given in 1.4. 
Moving to trans-critical, or supercritical cycles, these have been promoted as a favorable 
way to couple the advantages of steam Rankine and gas Brayton cycles [24]. Carbon 
dioxide (CO2) is the working fluid of choice here, as it starts behaving as a supercritical 
fluid from 7.38 MPa (73.8 bar) and 30.98 oC. These conditions are similar to the desired 
ones for the low temperature part of the cycle. Nonetheless the pressure is higher than 
that of a steam Rankine or Brayton cycle and may reach up to 250 bar or more, depending 
on the configuration of the cycle [17] in the high pressure part. This is done in order to 
keep the cycle above the critical point. The advantage of the trans-critical design stems 
from the fact that supercritical CO2 (s-CO2) behaves like a gas, albeit it features the density 
of a liquid [24]. This renders it almost incompressible. Incompressibility and high 
temperature operation results in high density and thus in a compact and efficient system, 
with smaller turbomachinery and heat exchanger components [19]. When a suitable 
working fluid is chosen, the combined system may reach considerably high round-trip 
efficiencies, up to 78%, according to [19]. The outlet stream of the turbine in discharge 
mode must be handled with care in trans-critical CO2 cycles. As explained above for the 
Liquid Storage Brayton based PTES systems, supercritical cycles ought to be recuperated 
to avoid wasting the heat carried by that stream. Furthermore, the thermal load carried 
by it tends to be greater than the load of the respective hot stream exiting the compressor 
during discharge [24]. This mismatch can be counteracted either by splitting the turbine 
==PAGE:25==
21 
 
outlet stream and recompressing part of it [24] or by storing the excess thermal energy 
in the hot or in the cold storage by applying a technique known as time shifted 
recompression [19]. Figure 1-6 below shows the discharge cycle of a s-CO2 system with 
recompression and the charge cycle of a s-CO2 system with time shifted recompression:  
 
 
Figure 1-6: The discharge cycle of a transcritical Carbon Dioxide cycle with flow split and recompression (left) 
and the charging cycle of a Carbon Dioxide cycle with time shifted recompression (right). Source: [24], [19] 
From Figure 1-6 it can be seen that PTES Storage systems can be combined with other 
renewable technologies, like Concentrated Solar Power (CSP) to enhance their 
performance from a technical and economical perspective. An introduction to CSP 
technologies will be presented in 1.3, while an analysis of the potential of combined 
systems will be given in 1.4. The most common storage media used in s-CO2 PTES are 
molten salts.  
Lastly, a short reference is made here to Rankine PTES systems. The basic principle is to 
utilize the ambient air as the cold storage and a latent heat storage system for the hot 
storage [18]. During charging, heat from the ambient air is used to evaporate water at 
below atmospheric pressure to saturation conditions. Subsequently, the saturated steam 
is compressed and de-superheated before transferring its latent heat to the Hot thermal 
storage medium. The charging cycle is completed with the processes of subcooling and 
throttling through a valve. In order to reduce the irrevirsibilities that result from the 
significant temperature differences between charging and discharging cycle, when one-
stage compression is applied, it has been proposed that the steam be compressed in 
several stages [18].  Sub-critical Rankine cycles with limited super-heating, like those met 
in existing CSP or nuclear power plants may well be modified to host a Rankine PTES 
system. An illustration of the basic charging and discharging cycles of a Rankine PTES 
system are shown in Figure 1-7 below: 
 
Figure 1-7: The charging (1-5) and discharging (6-10) cycles of a Rankine based PTES system. Source: [18] 
==PAGE:26==
22 
 
A modified version of the Rankine PTES system described above incorporates a low-
temperature ammonia (NH3) heat pump cycle coupled with the steam cycle (cascade 
system) [18]. This approach has been followed to alleviate the issue that is created by the 
fact that water at low pressure has a substantial specific volume, which renders its 
compression a challenging task [18]. If a low temperature heat source other than the 
environment is available, offering heat at a higher temperature than ambient air, the 
system efficiency can be enhanced further.  
1.3 CSP plants for sustainable power generation 
Similar to Solar PV, Concentrated Solar Power technologies rely on solar radiation to 
produce power. Nonetheless, rather than having as their basis the Photoelectric Effect, 
they concentrate solar radiation through collectors to heat up a medium in a receiver. This 
high-temperature medium may then be used in a heat engine (e.g., a Rankine cycle) to 
generate electricity [17]. Two main categories of CSP include technologies that are either 
commercially available or under development worldwide. The basis for this first 
categorization is the type of Solar Field that is used in the CSP system. The solar field is 
the heart of every CSP system, as it is the subsystem that converts the solar irradiance 
into thermal energy in a heat transfer fluid (HTF).  
The first category includes the so-called Line-focus technologies, in which a linear 
receiver is utilized, and the medium inside is typically a thermal oil (like Therminol vp-1 
mentioned above) [17]. Line-focus technologies are well-established, have lower costs, 
nevertheless their limited solar concentration ratio can harm their efficiency. The 
operational limits of most thermal oils and the relatively low concentration ratios limit 
the maximum temperature at the outlet of the receiver to approximately 390 oC. The 
receiver is typically a steel tube surrounded by a glass cover that offers insulation to the 
HTF medium inside [25]. The most prominent Line-focus technologies are the Parabolic 
Trough Collectors (PTC) and the Linear Fresnel reflectors (LFR). The former is currently 
the most attractive CSP alternative when it comes to investments, with 30 plants of a total 
of 2681 MWh,el capacity under construction worldwide [25]. The latter on the other hand 
has not been commercialized so far [25]. In PTC, the direct sunlight (DNI) is reflected onto 
a parabolic shaped Concentrator and is redirected to the receiver, heating the medium 
inside it. Like in the case of Solar PV, the Concentrators (and receivers) may face towards 
either the East-West axis or the North-South axis. The North-South orientation is 
preferred and a single-axis tracking system is also applied with the aim of allowing the 
Concentrator mirrors to track the sun during daytime from East to West [26].  
The second category comprises the Point-focus technologies. In Point-focus systems the 
Direct Normal Irradiation (DNI) of the sun is reflected on flat mirrors called heliostats 
(Solar Power Tower systems) or on a parabolic dish (Parabolic Dish systems), and is 
concentrated on a single point where the receiver is located. The concentration ratios and 
temperature elevations in the heated medium that are achieved by point-focus systems 
are inherently higher, in comparison to Line-focus systems [17]. Consequently, molten 
salts are typically the HTF of choice for Point-focus systems. Solar Power Tower (SPT) 
and Parabolic Dish systems are the two most important Point-focus solar field CSP 
technologies today. In the former, the receiver is situated on a tower surrounded by flat 
mirrors (heliostats-concentrators). High temperatures, up to 565 oC for nitrate molten 
salts [17] have been recorded and 5383 MWh,el of new capacity [25] are to be installed 
globally. These systems tend to benefit more from economies of scale and therefore their 
average nominal capacity is estimated at 207 MWh,el , as opposed to 89 [25] for PTC 
systems. 
Here it has to be clarified that capacity in this case refers to the maximum amount of 
energy that can be stored in the system [25], and this explains why it is expressed in 
energy and not in power units. As it will be discussed later, modern CSP systems tend to 
==PAGE:27==
23 
 
be integrated with thermal storage systems, in order to increase their flexibility and to 
decouple their production of electricity from sunny times. SPT systems have increased 
costs, as a result of their large heliostat field and sophisticated multiple- axis tracking 
system. This explains why they are typically more competitive if their capacities are 
increased. Parabolic Dish systems have been proposed, although no commercial projects 
based on them have been planned. Figure 1-8 below gives an illustration of the four types 
of CSP systems mentioned above: 
 
Figure 1-8: The four main types of CSP systems based on their solar field. Source: [25] 
 The energy produced by any given CSP system is a function of the DNI coming from the 
sun, in [W/m2], the area of the heliostat field, A, in [m2] and the product of the optical (sun 
concentration), receiver (sun thermal energy to HTF thermal energy) and power cycle 
efficiency [25]. With the exception of DNI that depends on the location, all the remaining 
parameters can be optimized to maximize the CSP efficiency. Most of them are related to 
the solar field. 
The majority of modern CSP systems are integrated with some form of thermal storage 
with one (stratified) or two tanks (constant temperature) containing the storage medium. 
This enables them to produce energy during peak demand times, which do not necessarily 
coincide with the hours of sunshine [27]. Thermal storage systems can be distinguished 
into Active and Passive Storage systems, while Active storage systems can be further 
classified as Direct or Indirect [25]. In passive storage systems, the Storage medium is in 
the solid phase (e.g., concrete) and thus not capable of moving between the tanks, 
whereas active storage systems apply storage media that are in the liquid phase and can 
move between the tanks [25]. Active Direct systems apply the same medium in their solar 
field and in the storage tanks, while in indirect systems, the storage medium can be 
different than the solar field medium. Indirect Active storage systems are usually found 
in PTC plants, however both Direct and Indirect storage type SPT plants can be found. 
Molten salts are the most common media used in Active storage systems and are 
encountered in 80% of the new SPT projects currently under construction [25]. Figure 
1-9 below shows the configurations of a Direct and an Indirect type SPT CSP system. The 
respective configuration in a PTC system would be the same, and the Power Tower would 
be replaced by series of Parabolic Troughs.  
 
==PAGE:28==
24 
 
 
Figure 1-9: The components of an Indirect (left) and a Direct (right) Active SPT CSP system. Source: [28] 
By observing Figure 1-9 one can note that an Indirect system requires the addition of a 
Heat exchanger between the TES system and the Solar Field which adds to the Investment 
costs [25].  
The last part of a typical CSP system, apart from the Solar Field and the Thermal Energy 
Storage (TES) system is a power block that exploits the temperature elevation that occurs 
in the Solar Field to produce power. PTC systems were the first to be developed and their 
limited maximum temperature (up to 400 oC) dictated that the Steam or Organic Rankine 
cycle should be used for power production [25].  Rankine based systems can also be 
coupled with SPT systems, with efficiencies up to 42% [17]. Modifications in current 
nitrate molten salts, like the addition of alkali or alkali-earth nitrate components such as 
LiNO3, as well as oxides in nanoparticle form, such as SiO2 and Al2O3 [25] can enhance 
thermal conductivity and heat transfer properties of salts. Moreover, research is being 
carried out in the field of novel High-temperature molten salts. The goal is to render them 
capable of reaching temperatures from 550 to 750 oC without incurring a phase change 
[25]. Several carbonate and/or chloride molten salts and binary or tertiary mixtures of 
them, such as Na2CO3/K2CO3/Li2CO3 that ensures safe operation up to 800 oC, have been 
proposed [25]. Although corrosion and high costs currently hinder their introduction to 
the market, it is likely that this will happen soon. Nitrate and High-temperature molten 
salts will make it possible to merge SPT systems with ideal-gas Brayton and even s-CO2 
cycles. The configuration of these cycles can be manipulated by numerous ways to 
increase their efficiency and limit the exergy destruction and irrversibilities in their 
components. The addition of a recuperator, interheating between turbine stages, 
intercooling between compression stages and applying flow splits are just some of them. 
Targets of 50% efficiency at temperatures over 700 oC [29] have been set for Brayton 
based CSP power systems. Finally, Combined cycle (top Brayton, bottom Rankine) CSP 
systems are under discussion [26]. PTC systems would require fossil support to allow for 
that [26], but in SPT CSP such a hybridization would not be necessary.   
1.4 A combined system approach  
Following the discussion in 1.2 and 1.3, it becomes clear that CSP and PTES systems can 
co-exist. Combined systems have started being the objective of intensive research efforts 
worldwide. Not only do the operational conditions (temperature, pressure) and the media 
used (molten salts) seem to allow for this match, but also the novel energy landscape, 
dominated by intermittent renewable sources, is favorable for such a coupling.  
More specifically, CSP plants, that already have a TES system attached to them, are 
designed to handle and store the maximum thermal energy obtained from their solar field 
and typically follow daily cycles. Nevertheless, even TES systems that are designed to 
handle only a few hours of storage cannot reach full charge state during the colder and 
==PAGE:29==
25 
 
less sunny days of the year, when the thermal energy coming from the solar field is not 
sufficient. This results in storage capacity not being used [17].  
Simultaneously, intermittent renewable sources like Solar PV and wind turbines do not 
produce power when needed, and their power production is often curtailed as discussed 
above. If this curtailed power could be charged through a charging PTES cycle in a TES 
system, then both electricity curtailment and part-load operation of PTES could be evaded 
[17]. The three more promising combined system configurations that have been proposed 
will be analyzed below. 
In the first approach the coupling of an existing CSP with a Rankine power block and a 
heat pump is recommended. This approach could be the most beneficial from an economic 
point of view, as solely the heat pump component would need to be added to the existing 
CSP system [17]. The addition of the heat pump could enable these plants to operate as 
baseload units and produce power at all times [30]. Most commercial heat pumps are 
based on the vapor-compression cycle and utilize low temperature heat sources, like 
ambient air or seawater and ground water. One-stage, multiple-stage and even cascaded 
and supercritical heat pumps have been successful in providing heat for domestic 
applications (space heating and hot water), up to 75 oC or low-grade heat for industrial 
applications, up to 100 oC [30]. These temperatures are well beyond the desired ones for 
integration with a CSP system though. That necessitates the use of alternative cycles, that 
are capable of achieving such high temperature lifts. A recuperated reverse Brayton cycle 
could be an attractive option, as it combines low pressure ratios (as a result of 
recuperation) with the desired temperature elevation, up to 500 to 700 oC [30]. Ideal 
gases like Argon and Nitrogen are suitable candidates for working fluids in Brayton heat 
pumps and their performance is similar to that of s-CO2 for high temperature operation 
[30]. The heat pump could use the environment as heat source [30]. A schematic 
illustrating how such a system could look is shown in Figure 1-10 below: 
 
Figure 1-10: A Rankine- CSP system reftrofitted with a recuperated Brayton heat pump (charging cycle). 
Source: [17] 
In the system depicted in Figure 1-10 the charging and discharging cycles are not coupled 
and are of different type, which could result in a potential mismatch between their 
operating conditions [17]. On the contrary, a system that applies a Brayton cycle for 
discharge and a reverse Brayton cycle for charge would not suffer from such issues. This 
system could have a common cold storage composed of two tanks at two distinct 
temperature levels for the two cycles, preferably below ambient temperature, and the 
direction of flow between them could change during charge and discharge. A gradient 
would be needed between the lowest temperature point of the charging and discharging 
cycle [17] and the storage temperature should be between these two. The addition of 
==PAGE:30==
26 
 
more than one interheated expansion stages would bring the storage temperature closer 
to ambient. While the cold storage would be charged and discharged only by means of the 
PTES system, the hot storage, which needs to match the operational temperatures of the 
solar field, should be capable of receiving the thermal load from the solar field and the 
PTES system [17]. This thermal load would then be used in the discharge PTES cycle and 
the direction of flow would be altered. All of the components with the exception of 
turbomachines could be shared among the charging and discharging cycles of the PTES 
[17], which could result in significant cost reductions. Nitrogen and Argon are considered 
to be the best candidates for working fluids in this case. Round-trip efficiency (ηRT) of up 
to 61% is claimed for this approach, which is slightly higher than the 55% claimed by the 
Retrofitted CSP-Brayton heat pump module [17]. According to [17], this difference should 
be mainly attributed to the lower cold storage temperature in the Brayton- reverse 
Brayton PTES approach [17]. Round-trip efficiency is by definition the ratio of the 
discharged over the charged electricity [12] in the PTES system and it is used to evaluate 
its performance. In practice, it can also be estimated as the product of the COP (Coefficient 
of performance) of a heat pump and the ηth (thermal efficiency) of the power block [17].  
A proposed design for this approach is given in Figure 1-11 below: 
 
 
Figure 1-11: A Brayton/reverse Brayton PTES system coupled with a CSP solar field (charging cycle). Source: 
[17] 
Finally, the third approach is to use s-CO2 as working fluid and apply split flow and 
recompression or time-shifted recompression as explained in 1.2. Time shifted 
recompression is considered to be the most promising among them (see Figure 1.6) as it 
claims Round-trip efficiencies as high as 70.6% if both a hot and a cold storage is used 
[19]. Additionally, time-shifted recompression decouples the maximum temperature of 
charging (heat pump) from the maximum temperature of discharging (heat engine) as the 
Solar field input entering the heat engine is at a higher temperature when compared with 
the compression heat that is stored in the Hot tanks [19]. Consequently, the efficiency of 
the heat engine becomes particularly high (53.8% at 650 oC [19]), the compression work 
is diminished and an extremely high round-trip efficiency of around 70.6% is achieved 
[19]. However, in this approach, the heat from the solar field is not stored but rather used 
directly when available. Furthermore, the temperature of 650 oC makes the use of more 
advanced salts than the nitrate (e.g., Solar Salt) ones necessary. The high costs of 
carbonate/chloride molten salts could potentially offset the economic advantage that 
stems from the usage of smaller and more compact turbomachines in the s-CO2 cycles. 
 
==PAGE:31==
27 
 
1.5 Scope of the thesis 
The scope of this MSc thesis is to screen out different alternatives of combined CSP and 
PTES systems similar to those presented in part 1.4 and investigate their technical and 
economic viability. The main thesis objective is expressed below:  
“To demonstrate and compare the technical and economic performance of two different 
configurations of Pumped Thermal Energy Storage systems integrated with Concentrated 
Solar power for a reference 50MWel net output. Identify the Key Performance (KPI) and 
Economic Indicators (EI) of the system and develop a Design, Off-design and Economic 
model for each of them during charge and discharge (if applicable).” 
 
• 
System 1: Nitrogen/Argon based Joule – Brayton cycle for both charging and 
discharging of the PTES and a heliostat field/solar tower (SPT) with direct TES for 
the CSP part. The basic layout is displayed in Figure 1-11. A more detailed 
description will be given in the Methods section. 
• 
System 2: A direct CSP plant, based on SPT technology with a steam Rankine power 
cycle for discharge and a Nitrogen reverse Joule-Brayton cycle for charge. The basic 
layout is shown in Figure 1-10. A more detailed description will be given in the 
Methods section.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:32==
28 
 
2 Methods 
In this Chapter, the configuration of the PTES thermodynamic cycles and other 
components used in Systems 1 and 2 will be presented. The mathematical formulation 
(equations) and all the relevant assumptions, tools (software) and methods applied will 
also be included here. In the case of System 1, the PTES Charging and Discharging cycle 
models will be described first, and an analysis of the combined PTES system will follow. 
The challenges that were introduced by the adoption of a common Hot and Cold storage 
for both the charging and discharging cycles will be explained. Moreover, a proposed 
scheme with all the relevant energy flows, which serves as the basis for the calculation of 
the PTES Round-trip efficiency, will also be given. Thus, the emphasis in System 1 is put 
on modelling the PTES system and in determining its feasibility from a technical 
perspective. As for System 2, it comprises two separate thermodynamic cycles, namely a 
discharging Rankine power cycle and a charging reverse Brayton cycle. For each of those 
cycles, a different modelling approach was followed and more details will be provided in 
two separate parts. The Solar Field component of System 2 will be analyzed next, and the 
modelling procedure and inputs applied in the System Advisor Model (SAM) software for 
the extraction of the outputs will be stated. Subsequently, a short part will be dedicated 
to the Solar PV system and the corresponding SAM model. The molten salt, two-tank TES 
system that links all of the system components will be described, together with the 
assumptions and simplifications that were taken. Additionally, the System Operational 
Strategy Scheme that shows the yearly operation of the System under part-load 
conditions and its component interactions will be introduced. Lastly, the economic model, 
the calculation of the system Levelized Cost of Electricity (LCOE) and the multi-parameter 
optimization of subsystem capacities will be the subject of the last part of the present 
section. 
 
2.1 System 1: Thermodynamic Cycles configuration, assumptions, 
description and relevant software 
The configuration proposed by [17], shown in Figure 1-11 was the basis for System 1. 
Apart from turbines, compressors, and heat exchangers found in all Brayton and reverse 
Brayton cycles, it features a regenerator for heat recovery, air precoolers for bringing the 
temperature of the working fluid closer to the Cold Storage temperature and four storage 
tanks, two of which belong to the Hot storage and two to the Cold Storage. The cycle 
models were created in Engineering Equation Solver (EES), a software that has libraries 
of thermophysical properties for a wide variety of fluids, as well as heat transfer functions 
built-in it. Coolprop libraries are also compatible with EES, providing it with additional 
fluid libraries (e.g. for incompressible fluids). EES is particularly helpful when modelling 
thermodynamic systems at steady state. It is capable of executing multi-parameter 
optimization and create parametric tables, useful for studying the influence of certain 
parameters on others.  
The PTES system capacity of choice is 50 MWel, which means that the discharging cycle 
work output was fixed to that value. With regards to the other inputs, they will be given 
in detail in part 2.1.1 below. Subsequently, a multi-parametric optimization was 
performed with the aim of maximizing the thermal efficiency, ηth of the Brayton cycle. An 
illustration of the discharging cycle will also be shown in part 2.1.1. The discharging cycle 
is practically independent from the Solar field and only utilizes the heat stored in the Hot 
storage to produce mechanical and electrical work. The two hot tanks include the hot Heat 
Transfer Medium at two distinct temperature levels. During discharge, fluid from the 
hotter tank flows through the heat exchanger, dissipates heat to the working fluid, and 
==PAGE:33==
29 
 
enters the colder (Hot storage) tank. Similarly, heat is transferred to the Cold storage, 
where the cold storage medium flows from the colder to the hotter tank (Cold Storage).  
On the contrary, the Hot Storage charging process involves both the PTES charging cycle 
and the Solar field, as it accepts heat from both of them. The PTES charging cycle features 
a similar configuration to the discharging cycle. Like in the discharging cycle, assumptions 
were taken regarding the inputs (mainly from [17]). Nonetheless, unlike in the 
discharging cycle, the electricity charged in the compressor of the system was not fixed to 
a value. 
Owing to the fact that they are part of the same PTES system, the two cycles must be tied 
in terms of energy (through energy balance equations), thus the charging cycle uses input 
from the discharging cycle model. The relevant equations will be given in 2.1.3. The 
charging PTES cycle was also optimized with respect to its COP (Coefficient of 
Performance) under varying Solar Field input. For each case, the Round-trip efficiency 
(ηRT) was estimated and a threshold of 0.5 (50%) as an indicator the feasibility of the 
PTES. In the Hot and Cold storage, the flows are reversed (compared to the discharging 
case).  
2.1.1 Discharging cycle 
In connection to the description given above, a schematic of the discharging Brayton cycle 
is depicted in Figure 2-1 below: 
 
Figure 2-1: The Brayton discharging cycle of the PTES of System 1 and its components. 
As it can be seen in Figure 2-1, the discharging cycle is a regenerated closed Brayton cycle. 
It features four heat exchangers (CSP/main hex, Regenerator, Cold hex, Precooler) and 
two turbomachines (Compressor, Turbine) connected through a shaft. The state points 
shown in Figure 2-1 correspond to the ones given in the Results part. All the inputs used 
in the discharging cycle EES model are tabulated in Table 2 below:

From Table 2 it can be seen that Polytropic efficiencies were defined instead of Isentropic, 
as they allow for comparison between turbomachines that operate at different pressure 
ratios [17],[31]. The value of 90% was adopted from [17]. The compressibility of gases 
necessitates the introduction of a pressure loss factor in all heat exchangers. This is 
defined as the difference in pressures between the inlet and outlet divided by the inlet 
pressure. The highest temperature point in the discharging cycle is 1 and the temperature 
assumed was 540 oC. The high temperature limit of solar salt (which is the HTF in the Hot 
Storage tanks) is 565 oC [32] and the existence of unavoidable temperature gradients in 
the main/CSP heat exchanger dictate that a lower temperature should be adopted for the 
working fluid in the discharging cycle. The high pressure of 100 bar was also taken from 
[17], the reason being that it ensures compactness in turbomachines and cost reductions. 
The generator efficiency was assumed to be 98%, as according to [33] it can be as high as 
99% for large machines at design conditions and nominal load. Argon and Nitrogen were 
both tested as working fluids and were treated as ideal gases [34]. Their specific heat, Cp 
was found to be almost constant throughout the temperature range of the cycle, as they 
are both light gases at high temperatures (thus ideal gases). On the other hand, a real gas 
behavior was assumed for the auxiliary air stream in the preheater.  
Moving on to the analysis of the cycle and component equations, the State points of Figure 
2-1 are revisited below: 
• 
1: CSP/main heat exchanger outlet – turbine inlet 
• 
2: Turbine outlet – Regenerator inlet 
• 
3: Regenerator Outlet – Precooler inlet 
• 
4: Precooler outlet – Cold heat exchanger inlet 
• 
5: Cold heat exchanger outlet - Compressor inlet 
• 
6: Compressor outlet – Regenerator inlet 
• 
7: Regenerator outlet – CSP/main heat exchanger inlet 
The equations applied in modelling the expansion of the working fluid through the 
turbine (1→2) are listed below:

in [kJ/kg] and ηs_t is the isentropic efficiency of the turbine (dimensionless).  
Then, internal heat exchange takes place in the regenerator, and more specifically heat 
flows from the hot streams 2 and 3 to the cold streams 6 and 7. The equations that 
describe this process (2→3) and (6→7) are shown below: 
 
ℎ[7] − ℎ[6] =  ℎ[2] − ℎ[3] 
2.4 
 
𝑒 = (ℎ[7] −ℎ[6])/(ℎ[2] −ℎ[6]) 
2.5 
Where h[2], h[3], h[6], h[7]  are the specific enthalpies of the working fluid in [kJ/kg] at 
state points 2,3,6,7 respectively and e is the dimensionless effectiveness of the heat 
exchanger that expresses the ratio of the heat transferred between two streams at the 
same side of heat exchanger and the maximum heat that could be exchanged. This is when 
the process is considered reversible. Parameter e is usually used as an alternative to pinch 
point ΔΤ in internal heat exchangers. In the EES model, it is also a parameter that is 
optimized. In general, high values of e are translated into more efficient heat exchange 
and thus a lower limit of 0.7 and an upper limit of 0.95 were set for e in the model. The 
limit of 0.95 was set to show that a fully reversible heat exchange process with negligible 
temperature gradients is not feasible.  
Subsequently, the working fluid at state 3 enters the precooler, where its temperature is 
reduced by exchanging heat with ambient air. The equations that describe the cooling 
process (3→4) are depicted below: 
 
𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡∗(ℎ[3] − ℎ[4]) = 𝑚𝑐𝑜𝑙𝑑𝑎𝑖𝑟∗(ℎ𝑐𝑜𝑙𝑑𝑎𝑖𝑟𝑜𝑢𝑡 − ℎ𝑐𝑜𝑙𝑑_𝑎𝑖𝑟_𝑖𝑛)

2.7 
Where mdot and mcold_air  are the mass flows of the working fluid and the auxiliary air 
stream respectively in [kg/s], hcold_air_out and hcold_air_in are the enthalpies of the air stream 
in the outlet and inlet of the precooler in [kJ/kg], T[4] is the temperature of the working 
==PAGE:36==
32 
 
fluid in state point 4 in [oC]. Additionally, Tcold_air_in is the temperature of the air at the inlet 
in [oC], which coincides with the ambient temperature in Table 2. Equation 2.6 is actually 
the energy balance in the adiabatic heat exchange process, and Equation 2.7 connects two 
temperatures at the two sides of the heat exchanger by defining a new parameter, the 
pinch point difference (DTpinch_prec, in [oC]). This parameter was optimized in the model 
and it was defined on the side of the heat exchanger, where it is believed that that the 
minimum temperature difference was present. It was constrained between 5 and 15 oC.  
In this case, since the outlet air temperature was also optimized (constrained between 30 
and 80 oC), the pinch was set on the other side.  
With the temperature at point 4 defined through the optimization of DTpinch_prec, the lowest 
temperature in the cycle, at point 5, was then optimized too. It was necessary to apply 
bounds on T[5]. Due to the fact that the cycle only has one intercooled compression stage, 
the minimum temperature had to be sufficiently low to allow for acceptable ηth values and 
therefore T[5] was constrained between -40 and 20 oC. The choice of the minimum limit 
was based on the operational range of several commercial cold storage mediums from 
[17]. More specifically, MEG-H2O (60-40 wt%) mixture was found to be an attractive 
choice owing to its large operational range (from -50 oC up to >100 oC) and the lower 
bound for T[5] was decided to be slightly higher than -50 oC due to the existence of 
thermal gradients in the heat exchangers.

follows:

similar to those used for the turbine (Equation 2.1, 2.2, 2.3). After solving this system of 
equations, h[6] and as a result T[6] was estimated. Here it has to be mentioned that for 
the ideal gas cycle approximation, temperature and specific enthalpy are interrelated. 
This effectively means that if one of them is known the value of the other can be 
determined directly by means of an equation of state (built-in in EES). 
Finally, h[7] (enthalpy at state point 7) and T[7] (temperature at state point 7) can both 
be determined from Equations  2.4 and 2.5. The final parameter that was optimized was 
the dimensionless pressure ratio (Prat) in the turbine. It was constrained between 2.7 and 
4 for Argon and 4.2 and 7 for Nitrogen. The difference in these ranges was caused by the 
different specific heat, γ ratios of the two gases.

2.1.2 Charging cycle 
Following the description provided in part 2.1, an illustration of the charging PTES 
reverse Brayton cycle is given in Figure 2-2: 
 
Figure 2-2: The Brayton charging cycle of the PTES of System 1 and its components. 
The cycle shares many similarities with the one from Figure 2-1, in that they are both 
based on a closed Brayton cycle. Specifically, they share the same components, as well as 
the Hot and the Cold Storage, at the same temperature levels. The inputs of the charging 
PTES cycle are effectively the same as those already mentioned in Table 2, with the 
exception of T[1], which was higher in this case since the Hot Storage was charged. 
Additionally, the Electricity out net parameter was not relevant to the charging cycle, 
since electricity flows in and not out of the PTES during charging. The efficiency of the 
motor (ηmotor) attached to the compressor was assumed to be identical to the generator 
efficiency (ηgener) from the discharging cycle and equal to 0.98 (98%). It has to be noted 
here that Q, solar field is the thermal power contribution of the solar field to the Hot 
Storage.  
The charging cycle state points shown in Figure 2-2 are also mentioned below: 
• 
1: Compressor outlet - CSP/main heat exchanger inlet 
• 
2: CSP/main heat exchanger outlet - Regenerator inlet 
• 
3: Regenerator outlet - Precooler inlet 
• 
4: Precooler outlet - Turbine inlet 
• 
5: Turbine outlet - Cold heat exchanger inlet 
• 
6: Cold heat exchanger outlet - Regenerator inlet 
• 
7: Regenerator outlet - Compressor inlet 
In the process (1→2) the working fluid dissipates its heat to the Hot storage. The 
temperature of the fluid at state point 1 was not defined in the charging cycle directly. The 
pinch temperature difference in the CSP/main heat exchanger (DTpinch_CSP) between the 
==PAGE:39==
35 
 
temperature of the working fluid during charging and discharging was optimized instead, 
and the respective temperature of the fluid in the discharging cycle (540 oC) was also used.

between the charging and discharging cycle temperatures of the working fluid, both in 
the Hot and in the Cold Storage. The specific enthalpy h[2] was then estimated through  a 
system of equations involving the regenerator effectiveness, echarg and the energy balance, 
as well as some auxiliary equations that link the charging to the discharging cycle and will 
be presented in part 2.1.3. The equations that describe the internal heat exchange process

𝜂𝑠_𝑐_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔 = 𝑤𝑐𝑖𝑑𝑒𝑎𝑙𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔/𝑤𝑐_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔 
2.30 
Where h[1], h[7] and h1_s are specific enthalpies in [kJ/kg],  wc_charg and wc_ideal_charg 
indicate specific work (real and isentropic), also in [kJ/kg], and  ηs_c_charg is the isentropic 
efficiency of the compressor.  
Some other relations that were relevant for the charging reverse Brayton PTES cycle are 
given below. It has to be noted here that those relations that characterize the combined 
PTES system and are used for information exchange between the two cycles are not 
presented here, but in the following part 2.1.3: 
 
𝑞𝑖𝑛_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔 = 𝑞𝑐𝑜𝑙𝑑_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔

2.39 
Where qin_charg and qout_charg represent the total specific heat streams that flow in and out of 
the charging cycle, in [kJ/kg]. Then, qCSP_charg and qcold_charg represent the specific heat 
streams that are charged to the Hot and the Cold storage respectively, also in [kJ/kg]. 
Moreover, qprec_charg indicates the specific heat exchanged in the precooler in [kJ/kg] and 
COP and COPel_to_thermal are indicators that represent the thermal performance of the heat 
pump charging system. The sole difference between them is that the former does not take 
the motor efficiency (ηmotor) factor into account, whereas the latter does. Lastly, Wnet, 
Wel_net, Wturb and Wcomp are the work flows of the net mechanical work charged into the 
cycle, the net electrical work added to the cycle, the turbine work output and the 
compressor work input respectively. They are all expressed in [kW]. All of the absolute 
work (W) and heat (Q) values in [kW] are extracted by the respective specific values when 
the latter are multiplied with the working fluid mass flow, mdot. The pressure ratio Prat_charg 
was another optimization variable in the charging cycle. A list with all the bounds applied 
to variables in the EES model of the charging cycle is given in Appendix 6.1.2, while a more 
detailed description of the EES model is also given in Appendix 6.1.2. It is reminded here 
that the charging cycle model was more complicated than the discharging cycle model, 
due to the fact that all the connecting equations between them were included in the 
charging cycle model. Having the discharging model results fixed (e.g., ηth), a series of 
simulations were performed for the charging cycle under several solar field thermal loads. 
The COP indicator was maximized in each case and the Round-trip efficiency (ηRT) was 
estimated. The procedure is described in detail in part 2.1.3. 
2.1.3 Combined PTES  
The main principle behind merging the charging and discharging PTES cycles is that the 
thermal energy, in [kJ], that is charged to and discharged from the Hot and Cold storage 
must be the same. Given the number of hours that the Storage system is discharged (td) 
and charged (tc) assuming daily cycles, the energy balances that involve the thermal 
energy flows mentioned above are as follows: 
𝑄ℎ𝑜𝑡_𝑜𝑢𝑡_𝑃𝑇𝐸𝑆= 𝑡𝑑∗(𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡)𝑑∗(ℎ[1] −ℎ[7])𝑑 
2.40 
 
𝑄ℎ𝑜𝑡_𝑖𝑛_𝑃𝑇𝐸𝑆= 𝑡𝑐∗(𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡)𝑐∗(ℎ[1] −ℎ[2])𝑐 
2.41

𝑄ℎ𝑜𝑡_𝑖𝑛_𝑠𝑓= 𝑡𝑠𝑓∗(𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡)𝑠𝑓∗(ℎ𝑠𝑎𝑙𝑡ℎ𝑜𝑡𝑡𝑒𝑟−ℎ𝑠𝑎𝑙𝑡𝑐𝑜𝑙𝑑𝑒𝑟)𝑠𝑓 
2.42

Where Qhot_out_PTES, Qhot_in_PTES, Qhot_in_sf and Qhot_in_tot are the thermal energy flows in [kJ] 
discharged from the Hot Storage through the PTES cycles, charged to the Hot Storage 
through the PTES cycles, charged to the Hot Storage from the solar field and charged to 
the Hot Storage in total respectively. Similarly, Qcold_out_PTES and Qcold_in_PTES are the thermal 
energy flows discharged from and charged to the cold storage by means of the PTES 
cycles, in [kJ]. Further, (mdot)c, (mdot)d and (mdot)sf are the mass flows of the working fluid 
in the charging PTES cycle, in the discharging PTES cycle and the molten salt mass flow 
charged to the Hot Storage by the Solar Field respectively, in [kg/s]. As for the specific 
enthalpies, the subscript “c” shows that they refer to the charging cycle, the subscript “d” 
is related to the discharging cycle, and subscript “sf” makes a reference to the molten salt 
in the solar field. Finally, Equations 2.46 and 2.47 essentially stem from the 1st Law of 
Thermodynamics (Conservation of Energy). 
In order to avoid determining the properties of the Solar field molten salt and 
consequently reduce the number of unknowns in the system of equations shown above, 
it was assumed that the Solar Field thermal input is a function of the thermal input from 
the PTES charging cycle: 
𝑄ℎ𝑜𝑡_𝑖𝑛_𝑠𝑓 = 𝑥∗ 𝑡𝑐∗(𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡)𝑐∗(ℎ[1] −ℎ[2])𝑐 
2.48 
Where x is a non-negative number, which renders Qhot_in_sf directly analogous to Qhot_in_PTES.

Equations 2.51, 2.52 and 2.53 were added to the charging cycle PTES model (as explained

(enthalpyratio)d was calculated from the discharging cycle and was transferred to the 
charging cycle, adding one extra equation to the EES model of the latter. The rest of the 
linking equations applied in the EES charging cycle model are shown below:

2.57 
 
Where Qtot_cold_charg and Qtot_cold_discharg show the thermal power charged to and discharged 
from the cold storage, in [kW] hoursday is a variable that has the value of 24 and refers to 
the hours in a day, Qsolar_field is the thermal power produced by the solar field, in [kW], mdot 
here is the charging cycle mass flow in [kg/s] and omega is the dimensionless ratio of the 
discharging and the charging time of the storage. It is an optimization parameter in the 
charging cycle model and it is constrained between 2 and 3. The choice of these bounds 
can be attributed to the desired characteristics of the TES system. The two-tank storage 
needs to have enough storage capacity to support full discharge for 16-18 hours, 
assuming daily cycles. These tanks need to be able to accept heat from the solar field and 
the heat pump simultaneously, and this explains why they have to be over-dimensioned. 
More information regarding the TES system design may be found in part 2.2.5 of the same 
chapter.  
==PAGE:44==
40 
 
Figure 2-3 below illustrates all the energy flows that appear in the combined CSP-PTES 
configuration of System 1, and reflects on the modelling approach described above:  
 
Figure 2-3: An illustration of the energy flows in System 1 
At this point it has to be noted that all of the flows shown in Figure 2-3 are energy flows 
measured in [kJ]. A and B are the electricity input in the charging PTES cycle and thermal 
input from the Solar Field respectively. Qprec_charge and Qprec_discharge are the thermal energy 
flows that are wasted through the precooler in the heat pump and in the power cycle. The 
precoolers are the components where most of the inevitable exergy destruction takes 
place. Moreover, it has to be understood that the Round-trip efficiency, ηRT is a 
characteristic indicator of the PTES system, and not of the combined System 1 shown in 
Figure 2-3. The combined system can be regarded as the superposition of the PTES and 
the CSP (Solar Field) systems. This is why the electricity output, ηth*B produced from the 
Solar Field thermal energy, B was not taken into account in the calculation of ηRT. 
Consequently, ηRT is estimated here as the ratio of the PTES electricity discharged over 
the PTES electricity charged, which is equivalent to the product of COP (electrical to 
thermal) and ηth (thermal to electrical). This modelling assumption is crucial for 
understanding of Results of System 1.  
2.2 System 2: Thermodynamic cycles, assumptions, description, 
software and analysis steps 
System 2 is different from System 1, in that the two thermodynamic cycles involved in it 
are not of the same type, as it can be observed from Figure 1-10. For the heat pump, the 
Cold Storage of System 1 is replaced by cooling water, which acts as a heat source at 
(almost) constant temperature, allowing for the decoupling of the energy flows shown in 
Figure 2-3 regarding the Cold Storage. This adds more degrees of freedom to the system. 
It can be assumed that the same cooling water is used by the Cooling tower in the 
condenser of the Rankine cycle. 
A different approach was followed in modelling System 2 and each component was 
analyzed based on several assumptions. A schematic of system 2 and all of its components 
is presented in Figure 2-4 below: 
==PAGE:45==
41 
 
 
Figure 2-4: A schematic of System 2 and its components. 
Figure 2-4 shows the five main components of System 2, which are described in more 
detail in parts 2.2.1 to 2.2.5. These are: 
• 
The reverse Brayton Heat Pump charging the TES with Renewable electricity. 
• 
The Rankine power block that accepts thermal power either directly from the 
Solar Field or from the TES Hot Tank and produces electricity. 
• 
The Solar Field that uses thermal radiation from the sun to heat up the molten salt 
Storage medium from 290 oC (Cold Storage) to 550 oC (Hot Storage). 
• 
 A Solar PV farm that provides electricity to the grid and to the Heat Pump.  
• 
The two-tank sensible Hot Storage TES system used to store the heat from the 
Solar Field and from the Heat Pump. 
==PAGE:46==
42 
 
The Solar PV farm is assumed to be the sole source of Renewable electricity that charges 
the TES in this MSc thesis, and its capacity is assumed to be large enough to support the 
needs of the grid and the heat pump.  
The final parts of the Methods Chapter are devoted to the Control Strategy that dictates 
the operation of System 2 under part-load conditions throughout the year (2.2.6), and to 
the Economic analysis performed for the calculation of the LCOE of the System (2.2.7). 
The multi-parameter optimization scheme implemented for System 2 is also the subject 
of part 2.2.7.  
2.2.1 Heat Pump – Design and Off-design analysis in EES 
2.2.1.1 Heat Pump – the Design Model 
The reverse Brayton heat pump in System 2 needs to exchange heat with cooling water at 
35 oC in the cold side. It is assumed that the same water is utilized by the cooling tower in 
the Rankine power system. Water is preferred over air here owing to its more attractive 
specific heat capacity (Cp).  
The fact that the heat source temperature is higher when compared with the 
corresponding Cold Storage temperature of System 1, renders the usage of interheating 
more promising. This means that in this case the working fluid is expanded in two stages 
and interheating takes place in between. Therefore, three distinct pressure levels are 
created in the heat pump cycle. Furthermore, the Precooler was abandoned in this case, 
as the temperature at the outlet of the Regenerator was considered suitable for direct 
expansion afterwards. An illustration of this modified heat pump cycle is given in Figure 
2-5 below: 
 
Figure 2-5: The reverse Brayton cycle, water-cooled heat pump used in System 2. 
Most of the entries (assumptions) mentioned in Table 2 were still relevant in the EES 
design model created for the heat pump in System 2. However, in this case, T[1] was not 
defined, but rather optimized, water at 35 oC replaced air at 25 oC (and the precooler was 
entirely replaced by two interheaters) and only Nitrogen was used as working fluid. The 
motor efficiency was the same as the generator efficiency mentioned in Table 2.   
The State points depicted in Figure 2-5 are revisited below: 
• 
1: Compressor outlet - CSP/main heat exchanger inlet 
• 
2: CSP/main heat exchanger outlet - Regenerator inlet 
• 
3: Regenerator outlet – Turbine 1 inlet 
• 
4: Turbine 1 outlet – Interheater 1 inlet 
• 
5: Interheater 1 outlet – Turbine 2 inlet 
• 
6: Turbine 2 outlet – Interheater 2 inlet 
==PAGE:47==
43 
 
• 
7: Interheater 2 outlet - Regenerator inlet 
• 
8: Regenerator outlet - Compressor inlet 
Regarding the equations that were applied to describe the processes mentioned above, 
these share many similarities to Equations 2.21 - 2.37 that were used for System 1 above. 
The same approach that was followed for the Precooler (equations 2.23, 2.24) was also 
adopted for the two interheaters. The sole difference was that the temperature at the 
outlet of Interheater 2 (T[7]) was assumed to the equal to the temperature at the outlet 
of Interheater 1 (T[5]), as this maximizes the cycle efficiency of the ideal gas cycle. 
Moreover, only specific properties (enthalpies, work, heat) were defined in this model, as 
mass flows and absolute values were introduced later, in the Off-design model 
formulation. Like in the case of the Discharging and Charging cycles of System 1, the EES 
code for the Design Model of System 1 may be found in Appendix 6.2.1, while the 
constrained variables and the bounds applied may be found in Appendix 6.2.1 too.  
Only the equations that define the specific heat and work values in the cycle are listed 
below: 
𝑞𝑖𝑛 _𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔= 𝑞𝑖𝑛𝑡𝑒𝑟ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡𝑒𝑟1_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔+ 𝑞𝑖𝑛𝑡𝑒𝑟ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡𝑒𝑟2_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔 
2.58 
 
𝑞𝑜𝑢𝑡_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔= 𝑞𝐶𝑆𝑃_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔 
2.59 
 
𝐶𝑂𝑃 = 𝑞𝐶𝑆𝑃𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔/(𝑤𝑐𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔 − 𝑤𝑡𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔1  − 𝑤𝑡_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔_2) 
2.60 
 
𝑞𝐶𝑆𝑃_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔=  ℎ[1] − ℎ[2]

𝑞𝑖𝑛𝑡𝑒𝑟ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡𝑒𝑟1_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔=  ℎ[5] − ℎ[4] 
2.62 
 
𝑞𝑖𝑛𝑡𝑒𝑟ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡𝑒𝑟2_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔=  ℎ[7] − ℎ[6] 
2.63 
Where qin_charg, qout_charg, qinterheater1_charg, qinterheater2_charg and qCSP_charg denote the specific heat 
that is added to the cycle, is dissipated from the cycle, enters the cycle from Interheaters 
1 and 2 and is extracted from the cycle through the CSP/main heat exchanger, in [kJ/kg]. 
Additionally, wc_charg, wt_charg_1 and wt_charg_2 indicate the specific work, in [kJ/kg] of the 
compressor, turbine 1 and turbine 2. The COPel_to_thermal was calculated from Equation 2.34 
also in this heat pump cycle. 
Furthermore, it is important to mention here that the optimization parameters in the 
Design model of the heat pump were those that were changed when the load of the cycle 
changed (off-design) and thus they had to be chosen carefully. Consequently, the main 
purpose of the design model in this case was to identify the optimal values for these 
parameters and use them for the estimation of component characteristics such as UA 
==PAGE:48==
44 
 
values for heat exchangers and turbine/compressor constants for the turbomachines. The 
Off-design model is further analyzed in part 2.2.1.2 that follows. 
2.2.1.2 Heat Pump – the Off-design Model 
The basic principle of Off-design analysis stems from the fact that a realistic cycle has fixed 
components, each of which is characterized by a unique variable. These variables are 
functions of thermodynamic properties and mass flows from the cycle analysis. This is 
why they are defined based on the optimized (nominal) values of thermodynamic 
parameters from the Design cycle.  
Starting from the turbomachines, the turbine/compressor constant was chosen to be the 
characteristic variable. The expression, taken from [36] is shown below: 
𝐶𝑇/𝐶𝐶 = 𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡∗√𝑇[𝑖]
𝑝[𝑖]  
2.64 
Where CT and CC denote the Turbine and Compressor Constant respectively, mdot is the 
mass flow in [kg/s], T[i] is the temperature in the inlet of the machine and p[i] is the 
pressure at the inlet. From Equation 2.64 it can be seen that CT and CC are not 
dimensionless, but rather have units, and more specifically [(kg/s) *(oC)0.5/(bar)] in this 
case. For the compressor, the indicator “i” refers to state point 8, while for the two 
turbines it refers to state points 3 and 5 respectively. The temperatures and pressures 
were optimized from the Design model, nonetheless, the mass flow of the working fluid, 
mdot was not estimated from it. For its calculation the following relation was used: 
𝑄𝑡𝑜𝑡_𝐶𝑆𝑃_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔= 𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡∗(ℎ[1] −ℎ[2]) 
2.65 
Where Qtot_CSP_charg, in [kW], is the thermal load delivered from the heat pump to the Hot 
TES. This was decided to be equal to the nominal thermal load of the Rankine cycle, 
namely 134844 kWth. Subsequently, the mass flow at design conditions, mdot was 
estimated from Equation 2.65. The procedure that was taken for the calculation of this 
thermal load is analyzed in part 2.2.2. For the three other heat exchangers, the thermal 
loads are estimated from the equations below: 
𝑄𝑡𝑜𝑡_𝑝𝑟𝑒ℎ_1_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔= 𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡∗𝑞𝑖𝑛𝑡𝑒𝑟ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡𝑒𝑟1_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔 
2.66 
 
𝑄𝑡𝑜𝑡_𝑝𝑟𝑒ℎ_2_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔= 𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡∗𝑞𝑖𝑛𝑡𝑒𝑟ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡𝑒𝑟2_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔 
2.67 
 
𝑄𝑡𝑜𝑡_𝑟𝑒𝑔_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔= 𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡∗(ℎ[2] − ℎ[3]) 
2.68 
Where Qtot_preh_1_charg, Qtot_preh_2_charg, and Qtot_reg_charg are the thermal duties of Interheater 1, 
Interheater 2 and Regenerator respectively, in [kW]. 
The electrical load charged into the heat pump was then calculated from the equation 
below: 
𝑊𝑒𝑙_𝑛𝑒𝑡_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔∗𝜂𝑚𝑜𝑡𝑜𝑟 = 𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡∗(𝑤𝑐𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔− 𝑤𝑡𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔1  − 𝑤𝑡_𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑔_2) 
2.69 
==PAGE:49==
45 
 
Where Wel_net_charg is the electricity charged to the heat pump, in [kW] and ηmotor is the 
efficiency of the motor (dimensionless). The CT and CC parameters were fixed to their 
design model values and kept constant when the machines operated under part load, in 
spite of the fact that the mass flow and thermodynamic operational parameters like 
temperature and pressure varied.  
As for the heat exchangers, their surface area, A in [m2] is the parameter that characterizes 
them. The equation that links the Area, A with the heat, Qtot (in [kW]) transferred through 
a heat exchanger is the following: 
𝑄𝑡𝑜𝑡= 𝑈∗𝐴∗𝛥𝛵𝑙𝑚 
2.70 
Where U is the overall heat transfer coefficient, in [W/(m2*oC)] and ΔΤlm is the logarithmic 
mean temperature difference, in [oC]. It shows the magnitude of the actual driving force 
in the process of heat exchange [37] and it is defined based on the following formula: 
𝛥𝛵𝑙𝑚=
(𝛥𝛵1 −𝛥𝛵2)
𝑙𝑛(𝛥𝛵1
𝛥𝛵2)
 
2.71 
Where ΔΤ1 and ΔΤ2 are the temperature gradients, in [oC], between the two streams that 
exchange heat in the two sides of the heat exchanger.  
The Qtot and ΔΤlm variables were already known for all heat exchangers, from equations      
2.65 - 2.68. Thereafter, the U values had to be found with the aim of calculating their areas 
from Equation 2.70. For a tube-tube heat exchanger without fins, the U*A product 
represents the resistance to heat transfer between the fluids [38] and is given from the 
following expression [38]: 
1
