2.72 
Where hi and ho are the convective heat transfer coefficients, in [W/(m2*oC)], for the fluids 
on the two sides of the heat exchanger, while Ai and Ao are the corresponding surface 
areas. R’’f,i and R’’f,o are the fouling factors per unit area, in [m2*K/W], Di and Do are 
diameters of the tubes in [m], L is the length of the heat exchanger, in [m] and k is the 
thermal conductivity of the wall material, in [W/m*K].  
Equation 2.72 exhibits that the estimation of the U value is a very challenging and time-
consuming task, which requires lots of data. In order to simplify the modelling of the 
performance of heat exchangers under part-load, the author of [38] makes a series of 
assumptions related to flow and heat transfer characteristics to create a formula that 
replaces the area, A with the UA value for all heat exchangers. This formula is shown 
below: 
𝑈𝐴
𝑈𝐴𝑟𝑒𝑓
= ( 𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡
𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡𝑟𝑒𝑓
)
0.8
 
2.73 
Where UA and UAref are the overall heat transfer coefficients under part-load and at 
design conditions respectively, in [W/(m2*oC)], and mdot and mdotref are the mass flows of 
the working fluid, under part-load and design conditions respectively, in [kg/s].  
Equation 2.73 was the basis for modelling the performance of all heat exchangers at part-
load (Off-design).  
==PAGE:50==
46 
 
In total, two Off-design EES models were prepared for the heat pump cycle. The first had 
the purpose of calculating all the Component characteristic variables (CC, CT and UA) 
under design conditions using Equations 2.64 - 2.71 and having the optimized values for 
all the design variables (from the Design model) as inputs. The second Off-design model 
then used the calculated Component characteristics at design conditions from the first 
model to estimate the new values of the optimization parameters under part-load 
conditions. The components in the heat pump cycle, as shown in Figure 2-5, are seven. 
This number is identical to the number of Optimization parameters from the design 
model. None of these parameters were mass flows however, and Equations 2.64 and 2.73 
show that mass flows are necessary in the estimation of the Component characteristics at 
Off-design conditions. Rather than defining the mass flows at different part-load 
conditions, the electrical load charged into the system was used as input instead, and then 
the mass flows were calculated indirectly from Equation 2.69. Finally, the UA and CT/CC 
values at each part-load level were calculated, together with all of the thermodynamic 
cycle operational parameters (temperature, pressure, enthalpy) at each state point and 
COP.  
The parametric table feature of EES was utilized here. At first, a column containing 
electricity (Wel_net_charg) values in descending order was created, starting from the design 
(maximum) load and ending at 15% of this nominal load. This assumption was based on 
the fact that the pumps, being turbomachines, cannot operate below a mass flow 
threshold. Here this threshold was chosen to be 15% of the design load. Moreover, the 
heat pump system was assumed to operate until its maximum load and not beyond it. 
Therefore, the operational range of the heat pump was between 15 and 100% of the 
design load level. The design electrical load was taken to be equal to 98.35 MWel for the 
heat pump. The reason for picking this design point load is explained in part 2.2.2, as it 
involves parameters from the Rankine power block as well.  
Ultimately, a parametric table with 60 rows, each corresponding to a different electrical 
load was the output of the EES Off-design model. The two Off-design codes and the 
constrained variables in them are given in Appendix 6.2.2.1 and 6.2.2.2. In order to 
simplify the total system (Figure 2-4) Off-design model, it was desired to summarize the 
heat pump Off-design model performance in a single equation. To extract this equation, 
COP and Wel_net_charg data from EES were fitted on a curve. The resulting diagram, created 
in Microsoft excel is given below: 
 
Figure 2-6: The COPel_to_thermal vs the Wel_net for the heat pump of System 2. 
==PAGE:51==
47 
 
As for the variation of ηmotor with respect to the load, Wel_net_charg, [39] shows that the motor 
efficiency for large motors is not significantly reduced below its nominal value (here 
98%), even at part-load conditions. Consequently, it was decided to keep ηmotor constant 
throughout the operational range of the heat pump in this MSc thesis.  
The final relation between the COPel_to_thermal (y) and Wel_net_charg (x), which was the result of 
the data fitting process shown in Figure 2-6 is the following: 
𝑦= −3𝐸−17 ∗𝑥3 + 8𝐸−12 ∗𝑥2 −9𝐸−07 ∗𝑥+ 1,4107 
2.74 
Where y is the COPel_to_thermal and x is the electricity input, Wel_net_charg.  
Equation 2.74 is characteristic for a heat pump with a nominal capacity, Pmax of 98.35 
MWel. For smaller heat pump systems (with smaller nominal capacities), like those tested 
when performing Sensitivity analysis, the final form of the relation between the 
COPel_to_thermal and Wel_net_charg was different. These equations are included in Appendix 
6.2.3. 
2.2.2 Rankine cycle – Approach and Off-design analysis 
Regarding the Rankine cycle, the goal was to extract an Off-design equation, similar to 
Equation 2.74, that describes the relation between the thermal to electrical efficiency (ηth) 
and the thermal input to the cycle, Qhot. Nevertheless, rather than creating separate Design 
and Off-design models, the use of a Power Cycle library was preferred.  
Steam Rankine cycles have been studied extensively, both for fossil-based power systems, 
and for Concentrated Solar Power applications. As a result, several libraries that include 
useful parameters for predicting the performance of these cycles are available in 
Literature.  
The System Advisor Model (SAM) software, which is a tool developed by the National 
Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL) of the United States of America, and is suitable for 
the analysis of CSP systems among others, has built-in libraries for several different 
Rankine power blocks. Each of these reference blocks has some specifications, such as its 
approximate operating temperature (temperature at the steam turbine inlet), its net and 
gross power output, its thermal input and its compatibility with different Heat Transfer 
Fluids (HTF) in the heat source side (Solar field and/or TES) [40]. The reference system 
Nexant 500 oC HTF was found to be the most relevant among the ones included in the 
library, as it has an operating temperature of 500 oC, which is reasonable considering that 
the Hot Molten salt that delivers heat to the cycle is at 550 oC in System 2. Furthermore, 
Nexant 500C HTF is compatible with molten salt HTF [40]. Other available parameters for 
this specific reference power block from [40] are tabulated in Table 3 below: 
Table 3: Nexant 500 oC HTF reference power block operating parameters. Source: [40]

Table 3 shows that the power block is rated for an electrical output 100 MW, yet in this 
thesis a net output of 50 MW was assumed. This means that the extracted part-load 
equation needed to account for this mismatch between the two cycles. Additionally, the 
turbine of the reference system is capable of performing at over-design conditions (above 
its nominal load), which is not a desired characteristic of System 2. As explained above for 
the heat pump, the operational range for the turbomachines was assumed to be from 15 
to 100%, because over-design operation typically harms the attained efficiency. However, 
this last difference was not taken into account for the final equation. 
The terms F0 and F1 that occupy the last rows in Table 3 are the two constants that appear 
in the part-load performance equation of the power block. In its original form from [40],

134.84 MWth.

2.2.3 The Solar Field/ CSP system model in SAM 
The Solar Field of System 2 is a Solar Power Tower. Along with the heat pump, the Solar 
Field is the component responsible for charging the TES system with thermal energy. 
Simultaneously, the solar field needs to have sufficient capacity to provide the nominal 
thermal input to the Rankine cycle for as many hours as possible (depending on the 
average DNI in the plant location), in order to maximize the power output of the plant. 
This means that the capacity of the Solar field must exceed that of the power block. In 
order to quantify this capacity mismatch, a dimensionless parameter called Solar Multiple 
(SM) is defined [41]: 
𝑆𝑀=
𝑄𝑆𝐹,𝑑𝑒𝑠𝑖𝑔𝑛
𝑄𝑝𝑏,𝑑𝑒𝑠𝑖𝑔𝑛
 
2.81 
Where 𝑄SF,design is the thermal load provided by the solar field at the design conditions 
[kW].  
In order to optimize the whole system, the Solar Field component size needs to be 
optimized too. The SM parameter is the most characteristic value for the Solar Field and 
it has to be large enough (higher than 1) to allow the Rankine power block to work at 
nominal load as much as possible. Nevertheless, particularly high SM values often lead to 
uneconomical systems, and thermal energy curtailment, and are thus not desired. The SM 
is also linked to the Solar Field Area (Heliostats and Receiver Area), which defines the 
total cost of the Field. 
As mentioned earlier, the hot salt exiting the Solar Field receiver and the cold salt entering 
it were assumed to be at 550 oC and 290 oC. These temperatures coincide with the Storage 
temperatures in the Hot and Cold tank respectively.  
Another crucial variable that defines the potential of the Solar Field and is dependent on 
the plant location is the Direct Normal Irradiance (DNI). The DNI variable determines the 
==PAGE:54==
50 
 
amount of usable heat produced by the Solar Field, as shown in [42]. When designing a 
Solar Field, the value of DNI at design conditions has to be determined. A high value of the 
design point DNI implies that less heliostats (and thus a smaller receiver) are needed to 
produce the rated power, whereas a lower design DNI value has the opposite effect. The 
SAM software that was used for the analysis of the Solar Field, offers a convenient way to 
calculate a meaningful design DNI value, based on weather data for a specific location. 
SAM is a free software tool that is able to perform techno-economic analysis for a wide 
variety of Renewable Energy Projects including Solar PV and Concentrated Solar Power 
systems. It accepts weather data for a specific location, and some system specifications 
and cost parameters to perform a technical (design and off-design) and economic 
analysis. The location of interest in this MSc thesis is Cape Town, in South Africa. Cape 
Town has a remarkable solar potential, which is favorable for the development of Solar 
PV and CSP installations.  
The developers of SAM [43] recommend that a value corresponding to approximately 
95% of the CDF (cumulative density function) of the annual DNI data should be used. This 
way, the system only exceeds its design thermal rating only during 5% of the sunlight 
hours throughout a year. Figure 2-7 below shows the distribution of DNI in year 2019 for 
Cape Town: 
 
Figure 2-7: The distribution of DNI values in a year for Cape Town, South Africa. Taken from SAM 
Here, it has to be noted that the zero DNI hours of the year are not depicted in the dataset 
in Figure 2-7. Only those DNI values that correspond to sunlight hours are portrayed. 
From Figure 2-7 it can be observed that DNI values around 900 W/m2 are the most likely 
ones, and this explains why this value was elected as the Design point DNI.   
As for the Solar Multiple, the base value used in SAM for the base case model was equal to 
4. Research [44] has indicated that for systems that have large Storage capacities, like 
System 2, it makes economic sense to over-design the Solar Field as well. A large TES 
system is a requirement for System 2, owing to the fact that both the Solar Field and the 
heat pump provide heat that has to be stored. The Storage subsystem is discussed further 
in part 2.2.5. At this point it is only mentioned that the capacity of the TES system was 
assumed to be equal to 17 hours of full discharge. This number was used in the CSP SAM 
model as well.   
The configuration of the heliostat mirrors around the Power Tower is dependent on the 
location of the CSP system. SAM features a built-in optimizer that decides the optimal 
==PAGE:55==
51 
 
position and number of mirrors. It also optimizes the Tower height [m] and the receiver 
height and diameter [m]. For the heliostat field, the SAM model uses location data 
(latitude and longtitude) and the design DNI value as inputs, together with geometrical 
characteristics of heliostats, atmospheric attenuation data and land area data. For the 
receiver, SAM calculates the thermal power based on the SM value and the nominal 
thermal load of the power block. SAM requires data regarding the Heat Transfer Fluid, 
which is Solar Salt (60% NaNO3 – 40% KNO3) for System 2, other materials and their Heat 
transfer properties and receiver flux related parameters. Lastly, the Power cycle and 
Thermal Storage are also modelled through SAM. Apart from inputs that concern 
technical data, SAM allows the user to select many Cost and Financial parameters that 
have an impact on the estimation of useful indicators, such as the Levelized Cost of 
Electricity (LCOE). For the majority of the parameters used in the SAM CSP model, default 
values suggested by the program were adopted. A detailed description of these 
parameters may be found in [40]. The parameters that were changed, along with the 
assumptions used for their estimation are listed in Appendix 6.2.4.  
At this point one has to bear in mind that in the present MSc thesis, the SAM software was 
solely utilized for modelling the Solar Field component and not the whole CSP system. The 
output of SAM is composed of a wide variety of hourly data, which are the output of the 
Off-design analysis for one year. Nonetheless, only three of them were actually taken from 
the program: the Receiver HTF inlet and outlet temperatures, in [oC] and the receiver 
mass flow rate, in [kg/s]. In the sensitivity analysis phase, the SM input was varied and 
different output files were produced from SAM. The three aforementioned output 
variables were utilized with the aim of identifying the Solar Field thermal energy 
produced during every hour of the year. The calculation was based on the following 
relation: 
𝑄𝑆𝐹= (𝑚𝑑𝑜𝑡)𝑠𝑎𝑙𝑡∗𝐶𝑝𝑠𝑎𝑙𝑡∗(𝑇𝑠𝑎𝑙𝑡,𝑜𝑢𝑡−𝑇𝑠𝑎𝑙𝑡,𝑖𝑛) 
2.82 
Where, QSF denotes the Solar Field receiver heat duty, in [kW], Cpsalt is the average specific 
heat capacity of the Solar Salt HTF, in [kJ/(kg*K)], (mdot)salt is the mass flow of the HTF in 
[kg/s], and Tsalt,out and Tsalt,in are the temperatures at the outlet and inlet of the receiver 
respectively, in [oC]. Cpsalt was defined in the average temperature between Tsalt,out  and 
Tsalt,in.  
The hourly QSF  data were subsequently utilized for the characterization of the Solar Field 
component in the Off-design model of the whole System 2. However, in the System 2 Off-
design model, it was assumed that Tsalt,out and Tsalt,in are fixed to 550 oC and 290 oC 
respectively. Consequently, all changes in the QSF load occur only as a result of variations 
in (mdot)salt. The operational range of the pumps that are used for the circulation of the 
HTF was decided to be from 15% to 100% of their design load. The design load here was 
assumed to be equal to 𝑄𝑝𝑏,design. However, since the base case SM value is 4, the pumps 
need to cover the load range from 4*𝑄𝑝𝑏,design down to 0.15*Q̇ pb,design. The issue could 
be tackled by connecting pumps of different nominal capacity in parallel. This way, a 
broader operational range could be covered and less heat would be curtailed. 
2.2.4 Solar PV system modelling in the System Advisor Model (SAM) 
The Solar PV subsystem of System 2 was modelled as a separate component. SAM, and 
more specifically the PVWatts package was used for modelling the Solar PV farm. The 
same weather input file (for Cape Town) that was added to the CSP model, was used in 
PVWatts as well. Fixed PV panels were picked instead of tracking PV panels, due to their 
lower cost. Default values from [40] were taken for all of the inputs, with the exception of 
the system nameplate capacity, which was estimated as follows: 
==PAGE:56==
52 
 
Firstly, the rated inverter size was calculated. The inverter is a piece of equipment 
responsible for converting the direct current (DC) produced by the PV arrays to 
alternating current (AC), which is delivered to the grid. Keeping in mind that the PV 
system needs to deliver power to the grid and charge the heat pump at the same time, the 
PV capacity was assumed to be equal to 98.35 (heat pump nominal load) plus 50 MWel 
(system and Rankine cycle nominal electrical load), or 148.35 MWel in total. This is the 
inverter capacity however, and the system nameplate capacity was then found by 
adopting a DC to AC ratio equal to 1.3. Using this ratio, the final nameplate capacity was 
estimated to be as high as 192.86 MWdc and this is the value that was used in SAM.  
The program generates hourly results for the produced electricity (in kWel) in a year. 
These hourly, Off-design power data were then fed to the overall system Off-design model. 
The Cost and Financial parameters from PVWatts, were subsequently used in the Cost 
analysis for the estimation of the PV system to Total Investment Cost.  
 
2.2.5 Two-tank molten salt storage Direct TES system – assumptions and 
modelling 
The two-tank molten salt direct TES option was considered to be the most attractive for 
System 2. The TES system is the component that links the Solar Field, the heat pump and 
the power block as it can be seen from Figure 2-4. It stores the excess energy from the 
Solar Field that is not directly dispatched to the power block, as well as the thermal energy 
that is charged through the heat pump. Three important assumptions that were made for 
the TES system are listed here: 
• 
The two tanks are charged and discharged simultaneously. This means that at 
every moment the mass flow exiting one tank must be the same as the respective 
mass flow entering the second. 
• 
The temperature gradients that are the direct consequence of inevitable heat 
losses that occur in the tanks were not taken into account in the Off-design system 
model. Therefore, the temperature in the Hot tank was taken to be equal to 550 
oC constantly, while the temperature in the Cold tank was fixed at 290 oC. The 
constant temperature assumption was then counterbalanced by taking a 2% 
penalty in the produced electrical load from the power block to account for the 
heat losses. Taking into account the conversion efficiency of thermal to electrical 
energy in the power block (0.3708 at design point), this assumption was actually 
found to be quite conservative according to [45] and [46]. 
• 
The Effective Volume (Veff) in each tank was taken to be equal to 70% of the total 
geometrical Volume. The term Veff here is the dimensionless ratio the of the 
volume of HTF that is inside the tank and the total geometrical tank volume. It has 
a value of 0.7. 
Prior to listing the equations that were used for the calculation of the Volumes of tanks, 
and the Salt Mass in them, another useful parameter, named Hours of Storage (H), has to 
be introduced here. The H parameter is crucial for the Storage subsystem, as it affects its 
size and determines its capacity. It also influences the operation of the Solar Field and the 
CSP module. This parameter shows the number of hours that a TES tank is able to provide 
the power block with its design thermal load. It is assumed that the level in the Hot 
Storage tank is initially at the highest point allowed and that the tank is the only source of 
thermal energy. H was chosen to have a value of 17 h in this system. The authors of [44] 
suggested that high values of H, up to 16 h, combined with high Solar Multiples (SM) 
render a CSP system economically competitive in terms of Cost per unit energy produced, 
[$/kWh].  
==PAGE:57==
53 
 
Based on the H parameter, the total thermal energy to be stored in the tanks, Qst in [kWh] 
was determined from the following equation: 
𝑄𝑠𝑡= 𝑄𝑝𝑏,𝑑𝑒𝑠𝑖𝑔𝑛∗𝐻 
2.83 
Qst shows the TES system thermal rating. The salt mass in each tank, Msalt in [kg] was then 
estimated from the relation given below: 
𝑀𝑠𝑎𝑙𝑡= 𝑄𝑠𝑡∗3600/[𝐶𝑝𝑠𝑎𝑙𝑡∗(𝑇𝑠𝑎𝑙𝑡,𝑜𝑢𝑡−𝑇𝑠𝑎𝑙𝑡,𝑖𝑛)] 
2.84 
Where 3600 is the conversion factor from kWh to kJ. 
The Hot and Cold molten salt densities, ρHot and ρCold in [kg/m3] were calculated based on 
the corresponding temperatures, Tsalt,out and Tsalt,in. The total geometrical Volumes, in 
[m3] of the Hot and the Cold tanks, VH and VC, were calculated from the following 
equations: 
   
𝑉𝐻= 𝑀𝑠𝑎𝑙𝑡/(𝜌𝐻𝑜𝑡∗𝑉𝑒𝑓𝑓) 
2.85 
 
𝑉𝐶= 𝑀𝑠𝑎𝑙𝑡/(𝜌𝐶𝑜𝑙𝑑∗𝑉𝑒𝑓𝑓) 
2.86 
The VH, VC, Msalt, ρHot, ρCold, Tsalt,out, Tsalt,in and Veff  TES parameters were all utilized in the 
final Off-design model of System 2. Figure 2-4 shows that there are three streams either 
entering or exiting the Hot and the Cold Tank at any given moment. The Hot and Cold Tank 
energy balances are shown below: 
𝑑𝑈𝐻𝑜𝑡

54 
 
𝐿𝐶𝑜𝑙𝑑= (𝑚𝐶𝑜𝑙𝑑/𝜌𝐶𝑜𝑙𝑑)/(𝑉𝑒𝑓𝑓∗𝑉𝐶) 
2.92 
 
Where UHot  and UCold are the internal energies in the Hot and the Cold Tank, in [kJ], mhot 
and mCold are the masses of Salt in the Hot and in the Cold Tank at any given moment, in 
[kg] and LHot and LCold are dimensionless parameters than vary from 0 to 1 and show the 
ratio of the volume occupied by the Salt at any given moment over the maximum allowed 
volume that can be occupied by salt (which is equal to the product Veff ∗VH for the Hot 
tank and Veff ∗VC for the Cold tank). Further, (mdot)in,H,SF, (mdot)in,H,HP, (mdot)out,H 
denote salt mass flows that enter or exit the Hot tank, in [kg/s]. The first is the mass flow 
that enters the tank from the Solar Field, the second is the mass flow that enters the tank 
from the Heat Pump and the last one is the mass flow that exits the tank to the power 
block. Similarly, (mdot)in,C,(mdot)out,C,SF, (mdot)out,C,HP denote salt mass flows that enter 
or exit the Cold tank, in [kg/s]. The first of them is the sole flow that enters the cold tank 
from the power block, whereas the other two are the flows exiting the tank to the Solar 
Field and to the Heat Pump.   
In order to further simplify the total system Off-design model, the infinitesimal time span, 
dt was assumed to be equal to 5 minutes. Following this assumption, a tolerance (tol) was 
established and the maximum tank levels were set to (1 – tol), while the minimum tank 
levels were set to (0 + tol). The tol parameter is effectively the Level change that occurs 
within 5 minutes in the tanks. It was found to be equal to 0.003431. Equations 2.87 and 
2.90 were also reformulated in the final Off-design model for System 2 to account for the 
assumed dt. The TES system design EES model may be found in Appendix 6.2.5. 
2.2.6 Control Strategy for the operation of the combined system 
So far, the discussion has been focused on each component of System 2 individually. 
Nonetheless, the goal in this MSc thesis is to examine the behavior of the whole System 
under Design and Off-design conditions. To do so, an overall Off-design model that reflects 
all the component interactions, as shown in Figure 2-4, needs to be created. As mentioned 
above, the inputs to this final Off-design model were chosen to be the Solar Field thermal 
output, in [kWth] and the Solar PV system electrical output, in [kWel]. These outputs were 
extracted from SAM for every hour in a year. The time span dt was chosen to be equal to 
5 minutes (300 seconds) though, and thus each Solar Field and Electricity output value 
from SAM was repeated 12 times (in other words it was kept constant) and the size of 
each of the two arrays carrying the SAM output data was increased 12 times. 
Subsequently, for each 5-minute step, the values of these two inputs determined the mode 
of operation of the system. The plant was designed to act as a baseload plant, which means 
that its electricity production had to be maximized. Baseload plants are typically 
characterized by high Capacity Factors (CF). The Capacity factor is a dimensionless 
indicator that varies from 0 to 1 and shows the fraction of time that the plant operates at 
nominal power [44]. The following relation is used for its estimation: 
𝐶𝐹= 𝑊𝑜𝑢𝑡,𝑛𝑒𝑡
𝑃𝑛𝑜𝑚∗𝜏 
2.93 
Where Wout,net is the net electricity output in a year (including the 2% penalty), in 
[kW*min], Pnom is the nominal installed capacity, namely 50000 kWel and τ in this case in 
the total number of 5-minute periods in a year, or 365*12*24 min. Note that in order to 
convert Wout,net to [kWh] one needs to divide it with 12, due to the fact that each value 
refers to the energy output in 5 minutes and not in 1 hour.  
==PAGE:59==
55 
 
In the base case, it was desired to maximize the CF factor irrespective of the Cost. In the 
sensitivity analysis/optimization a trade-off that provides a reasonable CF and the 
minimum cost at the same time was sought.  
The overall Off-design model was based on the following operational principles: 
• 
The PV system is over-dimensioned (with a nominal capacity of 148.35 MWel), 
which means that in a typical sunny day, it should be capable of providing the 
nominal electrical load of 50 MWel to the grid by itself, without the support of the 
Rankine power block. The excess PV energy should then be transferred to the TES 
tanks by means of the heat pump. Consequently, the heat pump is charged only at 
those times when the PV electricity exceeds the threshold of 50 MWel. 
• 
The Rankine power block has the purpose of producing electricity at times when 
the PV system does not, most notably at night. The power block is powered both 
by the TES and the Solar Field, nonetheless at night time, only the first option is 
available.  
• 
The fact that the CSP plant is combined with a heat pump and a Solar PV plant 
suggests that in the majority of the hours of the year, the role of the Solar Field is 
to charge the TES system. This happens since the production hours of the Solar 
Field and the PV system typically coincide during a day, and thus the former would 
not need to dispatch thermal power to the Rankine cycle if the desired power is 
already produced from the PV plant.  
• 
Before any thermal power is transferred from the Solar Field and the heat pump 
to the TES in the charging phase, it has to be ensured that the Level in the Hot tank 
is not at maximum (1-tol) and the Level in the Cold tank is not at minimum (0+tol). 
Similarly, before the tanks are discharged to provide energy to the Rankine block, 
it has to be checked that the Level in the Hot tank is above the lowest limit (0+tol) 
and that the Level in the Cold tank is below the highest limit (1-tol). Note that the 
tanks are charged and discharged simultaneously and therefore in every time step 
their dimensionless Levels need to add up to exactly 1.  
• 
The Solar Field and the heat pump thermal (QSF) and electrical (Wel_net) load 
respectively were allowed to vary from 15 to 100% of their nominal values, due 
to the pump limitations as explained above. At those times when the loads were 
not within this range, curtailment had to occur. In the case of the Solar Field this 
is achieved by defocusing the heliostats. Load curtailment had to be enforced in 
the Solar Field, at times when the TES system did not have the ability to store more 
energy (if the Hot tank is full).  
• 
With regards to the heat pump and the Rankine cycle, these were represented in 
the overall Off-design model by the two Off-design equations given above. Their 
output was indirectly dependent on the QSF and W_el_net input variables.  
Additionally, it has to be clarified that all the variables (mass flow, salt mass in the tanks, 
level) in the Off-design code were either directly or indirectly related to the values of QSF 
and W_el_net in every time step.  
During a normal day (with adequate sunlight) the qualitative profiles of the PV power 
produced (WPV), power block power produced (Wpb) and Solar Field thermal power (QSF) 
are portrayed in Figure 2-8 below: 
==PAGE:60==
56 
 
 
Figure 2-8: Qualitative daily profiles for the PV power (W_PV), power block power (W_pb) and Solar Field 
thermal power (Q_SF) according to the proposed operational plan of System 2 
Figure 2-8 shows the expected output from the Off-design model. Based on the discussion 
above, the final Off-design operational Strategy scheme for System 2, which provides the 
guidelines for the operation of the system under all possible conditions throughout the 
year is presented next in Figure 2-9: 
==PAGE:61==
57 
 
 
Figure 2-9: The Operational strategy scheme/ control loop for System 2 
Legend: 
 Q_hot : Input to the power cycle, [kWth], Q_S.F. : the solar field output thermal load, [kWth], Q_charg : thermal load charged to the tanks, [kWth], Pmax : the maximum (design load) 
electricity charged into the heat pump, [kWel],  L_h_t : the level of salt in the hot tank, [-], L_c_t : the level of salt in the cold tank, [-],  L_h_t_min/L_c_t_min : the minimum level of salt in 
the hot/cold tank, [-], L_h_t_max/L_c_t_max : the maximum level of salt in the hot/cold tank, 70% of the tank capacity [-], W_out : the power cycle electrical output, [kWel], TES: thermal 
energy storage system, W_el_net: electricity produced by the PV system and can go either directly to the grid (<50 MW) or both to the grid and to the heat pump block (>50 MW), [kWel]

==PAGE:62==
58 
 
From Figure 2-9 it is observed that the potential outcomes (scenarios) from all possible 
combinations of QSF and W_el_net input are 14. The numbers that are shown correspond to 
different combinations of active subsystems (Solar Field, Heat Pump, TES, power block, 
Solar PV) and different modes of operation (charging, discharging, curtailment etc.). A list 
that includes all numbers (1-26) that are shown in Figure 2-9 and provides detailed 
explanations for the actions that are taken and subsystems/components that are involved   
in each case can be found in Appendix 6.2.6. 
The Operational Strategy scheme given in Figure 2-9 provided the starting point for the 
development of the overall Off-design model of System 2. The model was created using 
the Python programming language and the original code is given in Appendix 6.2.7. For 
each of the 14 possible final cases shown in Figure 2-9, and for every 5-minute time step, 
the Python code calculated 14 output variables. These output variables were: 
• 
The Levels in the Hot and Cold tank, LHot and LCold 
• 
The salt mass in the Hot and Cold tank, mHot and mCold 
• 
The total electricity delivered to the grid, Wout 
• 
The thermal input to the power block, Qhot 
• 
The total thermal load charged (with + sign) and discharged (with – sign) from 
the TES, Qcharg 
• 
The electricity produced from the Rankine power block, Wpb 
• 
The thermal duty delivered by the heat pump, Qhp 
• 
The curtailed Solar Field thermal load, QSF, which was different from the raw QSF 
that was used as input due to the fact that it did include the curtailed thermal load. 
• 
The curtailed Solar PV output, W_el_net, which was different from the W_el_netraw 
that was used as input due to the fact that the latter did include the electricity that 
was finally not used (curtailed). 
• 
The internal energies in the Hot and Cold tank, Uhot and Ucold , and 
• 
The Case number (CaseNo.) from Figure 2-9 that gives information about the 
operational status of the system during each time step. 
2.2.7 Economic Model, Levelized Cost of electricity (LCOE) calculation and 
optimization/sensitivity analysis 
Following the estimation of the total work produced annually from the Off-design model 
of System 2 and the corresponding CF from equation 2.93,  a Cost analysis had to be 
carried out in order to estimate a parameter known as Levelized Cost of Electricity 
(LCOE). LCOE shows the costs that are incurred over the whole lifetime of the investment 
normalized by the electrical energy output of the plant [47]. It also gives an insight into 
the electricity price that the plant has to sell energy at, in order to balance out the costs 
[47]. It offers a convenient way to compare the attractiveness of different energy projects 
or plant configurations.  
The two cost categories that are relevant for the calculation of LCOE are the Capital or 
Investment Costs (CAPEX) and the Operation and Maintenance costs (O&M Costs). The 
CAPEX, in [$] is the up-front cost paid for the construction of the plant and it can be found 
by summing up the CAPEX estimates for all of the components/subsystems of System 2. 
On the other hand, the O&M Cost is usually composed of two parts, namely the Fixed 
[$/yr] and Variable [$/kWh] O&M. The former typically shows the constant maintenance, 
labor and insurance expenditure, while the latter includes those costs that change over 
the lifetime of the project, such as electricity prices or fuel costs (if applicable) [47].  
Regarding the CAPEX, estimates were found for the following parts of System 2: 
• 
The CSP plant 
• 
The Solar PV plant 
==PAGE:63==
59 
 
• 
The Heat Transfer Fluid used in the Solar Field and in the TES system 
• 
The reverse Brayton Heat Pump 
For the CSP plant, the Installation costs provided by SAM were used. The software 
accounts for the Heliostat Field, Tower, Receiver, Power Cycle and Contingency Costs in 
the calculation of the CSP CAPEX. More on the default cost parameters used in SAM can 
be found in [40]. Furthermore, SAM gives a specific cost in [$/kWh] for the Thermal 
Energy Storage system (in this case equal to 22 $/kWh), which can then be multiplied 
with the stored thermal energy, Qst from equation 2.83 to give the final TES CAPEX.  Apart 
from these Direct costs, SAM gives estimations for the Indirect cost component of the total 
CSP CAPEX. The Indirect Costs accounted for in SAM include EPC owner costs, land area 
costs and Sales Tax costs.  The CSP CAPEX is then found by summing these Direct and 
Indirect Costs. 
As for the PV plant, the Installation costs were also found directly from SAM. The Direct 
costs include the Module and Inverter costs, Balance of Plant (BoP), labor and overhead 
costs, as well as the Contingency Costs. The Indirect costs, like land costs, environmental 
permission costs, Engineering, Grid interconnection costs and Sales Taxes are also 
included in the calculation of the final PV CAPEX.  
The HTF molten salt cost was not part of the specific storage cost provided by SAM and 
thus had to be determined separately. It was chosen to use the average value of the 
specific cost, in [$/kg] that was given in the Supplementary material of [12] for Nitrate 
Molten salts. The data were obtained from various vendors and the average value was 
equal to 0.9 $/kg.  This specific cost was then multiplied with the Total Required Salt Mass 
(Msalt,req), in [kg] calculated from the TES system design EES model in order to estimate 
the final HTF CAPEX. 
Finally, with respect to the heat pump, the CAPEX was estimated by means of two 
different paths and the obtained results were compared. In the  first approach, the  
Purchase Equipment Costs (PEC) were found for all of the components of the heat pump 
(Compressor, Turbine 1, Turbine 2, CSP/main heat exchanger, Regenerator/Recuperator, 
Interheater 1 and 2  and Motor) based on Cost relations from the Supplementary Material 
of  [12] and from [48].  Subsequently, it was assumed that the total PEC (Purchase 
Equipment Cost) for all components accounts for 80% of the final TCI, which lead to the 
estimation of the CAPEX (TCI) of the reverse Brayton heat pump.  The detailed cost 
calculations from this approach can be seen in Appendix 6.2.8. To account for inflation, 
Chemical Engineering Cost Indices (CEPCI) from the Supplementary material of [12] and 
from [49] were utilized as well. In the second approach, an inflation and capacity adjusted 
specific cost in [$/kWel] was directly used for the whole heat pump system. The deviation 
in capacity between the heat pump of system 2 and the cycle of [48] was resolved by 
adopting a 0.6 scaling exponent. The adjusted specific cost was then multiplied with the 
nominal heat pump capacity (98350 kWel) to give the heat pump CAPEX in [$]. Despite 
the fact that the original specific costs from [48] were for a s-CO2 Brayton power cycle, 
they were found to be very close with those obtained from the first approach. Therefore, 
it was chosen to use the specific cost from the second approach for the estimation of the 
heat pump CAPEX, both for the base scenario and for the sensitivity analysis. It is 
reminded here that in the Base scenario, the highest value of SM and the highest possible 
Solar PV plant and heat pump capacities were used in order to maximize the Capacity 
Factor of the plant. 
With all the CAPEX values defined, the Fixed O&M cost of System was assumed to be 2% 
of the total system CAPEX. According to the information provided in the Supplementary 
material of [12], the total O&M typically varies from 1-5% of the total CAPEX. Then, to 
calculate the LCOE, the following formula from [42] was utilized: 
==PAGE:64==
60 
 
𝐿𝐶𝑂𝐸= 𝐶𝐴𝑃𝐸𝑋𝑡𝑜𝑡∗𝐶𝑅𝐹+ 𝑂&𝑀𝑡𝑜𝑡 
(𝑊𝑜𝑢𝑡,𝑛𝑒𝑡/12)
 
2.94 
Where CAPEXtot is the total CAPEX for all of the components, in [$], CRF is the Capital 
Recovery Factor, in [1/yr] that practically converts the total CAPEX into an annuity [$/yr], 
O&Mtot includes the Fixed O&M in [$/yr] and the Variable O&M in [$/kWh], multiplied by 
the kWh of electricity produced annually. The term (Wout,net/12) in the denominator refers 
to the total electrical energy produced in a year, in [kWh,el] as discussed above in equation 
2.93. This electricity output was estimated from the output of the Off-design model of 
System 2. 
The CRF is given by the equation below according to [42]: 
𝐶𝑅𝐹= 𝑑∗(1 + 𝑑)𝑙𝑓𝑡
((1 + 𝑑)𝑙𝑓𝑡) −1 
2.95 
Where d is the discount rate of the energy project [%] and lft is the project lifetime, in [yr]. 
The lifetime of System 2 was decided to be 30 yr, as this was the mean value from the 
range 25-35 proposed in the Supplementary material of [12]. Further, as suggested in 
[50], the expected discount rate for 2022 in South Africa is 4%.  
With the CRF now defined, the Base case scenario LCOE can also be determined from 
equation 2.94. 
Finally, a sensitivity analysis was carried out for the PV capacity, the Solar Multiple (SM) 
and the Heat Pump Capacity. The goal was to identify the optimal set of these three 
parameters that minimize the LCOE. The SM values tested were 4 (Base), 3.5, 3, 2.5, 2, 1.5 
and 1. Each of these 7 SM values produced a different QSF output profile in SAM (which 
was used as input to the overall Off-design model). Subsequently, the Heat pump 
capacities (Pmax) evaluated were 98.35 (Base), 85, 70, 55, 40, 25 and 10 MWel. For each 
capacity, a different form of equation 2.74 was produced (which was added in the Python 
Off-design code). Lastly, 5 different PV capacities were examined, namely 148.35, 135, 
120, 75 and 60 MWel. For each one of them, a separate W_el_net SAM output was created 
(which was used as input to the overall Off-design model). In total 5 output tables were 
made, one for each PV capacity. In those sensitivity analysis Off-design models, an extra 
if-condition was added to the ones shown in Figure 2-9. More specifically, the PV electrical 
output, W_el_net was reduced down to (50 + Pmax) in those cases when the excess electricity 
from the PV subsystem was higher than the nominal Heat Pump capacity. This if-condition 
was added to ensure that the maximum operating point of the heat pump never surpasses 
its nominal capacity. In other words, no production outside of the 15-100% range was 
allowed. The resulting diagrams are given in the Results section that follows, while the 
original tables are given in Appendix 6.2.9.    
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:65==
61 
 
3 Results and Discussion 
The Results presented in this section encompass the most important outputs (Figures and 
Tables) from the analysis that was presented in the Methods section above. For System 1, 
the goal was to investigate the charging and discharging cycles and to determine whether 
a Solar Field-integrated PTES configuration based entirely on the Brayton cycle (and 
reverse-Brayton for the Heat Pump) can be attractive from a technical perspective. In the 
case of System 2, the technical feasibility of the system was guaranteed, since the charging 
(reverse Brayton) and discharging (Rankine) cycles were not linked through energy 
balances, like those described in part 2.1.3. Results of note from the models of individual 
components are given for System 2, along with other important Off-design and Economic 
model outputs, and sensitivity analysis results.   
3.1 System 1 Results 
In system 1, the target was to examine the charging and discharging cycles of the PTES 
system and the effect that the addition of extra heat from the Solar Field had on the 
performance of the integrated System. For this reason, the dimensionless x number 
defined in equation 2.48 was varied from 0 to 1. An x value equal to 0 indicates that no 
heat is coming from the Solar Field (thus a PTES-only System), while an x value of 1 
suggests that the Solar Field thermal input is as high as the charging cycle input.  
In the base case, at x=0, the T-s diagrams of the charging (heat pump) and discharging 
(heat engine) one-stage PTES cycles are depicted below. Figure 3-1 and Figure 3-2 refer 
to Argon-based and Nitrogen-based cycles respectively: 
 
 
Figure 3-1: Charging and Discharging cycle T-s diagrams for System 1, with Argon as working fluid and zero 
Solar Field contribution (x=0) 
In Figure 3-1 one may observe the Storage temperatures in each of the four tanks as well. 
These temperatures are 550 and 290 oC for the Hot Storage and 32 and -45 oC for the Cold 
Storage tanks. Nitrate molten salts, such as solar salt could be the storage media of choice 
in the Hot TES system, while a 60-40 wt% MEG-H2O could be used in the Cold TES tanks.   
In each case the Storage temperature lies between the working fluid temperatures of the 
Charging and Discharging cycles. More specifically, the highest (T[1] and T[2]) and the 
lowest (T[5] and T[6]) temperatures in the charging cycle are constantly higher than the 
==PAGE:66==
62 
 
Hot Storage and lower than the Cold Storage temperatures respectively, whereas the 
opposite is observed for the highest (T[1] and T[7]) and the lowest (T[4] and T[5]) 
temperatures in the discharging cycle.  
The compression (7→1 in the Charging cycle and 5→6 in the Discharging cycle) and 
expansion (4→5 in the Charging cycle and 1→2 in the Discharging cycle) processes are 
not isentropic, as a result of the irreversibilities that occur in the turbomachines. 
Additionally, Figure 3-1 illustrates the importance of regeneration/recuperation in the 
System. Regeneration occurs between state points 2-3 and 6-7 in the charging cycle and 
2-3 and 6-7 in the Discharging cycles and it really augments the temperature lift. In the 
charging cycle the recovered heat accounts for 95.6% of the total heat transferred to the 
Hot Storage. Simultaneously, the regeneration process reduces the pressure ratio in the 
cycle. In the discharging cycle, the optimized pressure ratio was 2.7, while in the charging 
cycle it was 2.52. Regeneration is indispensable, especially in reverse Brayton heat pump 
systems to maximize their temperature lift. 
 
 
Figure 3-2: Charging and Discharging cycle T-s diagrams for System 1, with Nitrogen as working fluid and zero 
Solar Field contribution (x=0) 
Figure 3-2 shows that in Nitrogen cycles, the same Hot TES tank temperatures used in the 
Argon PTES system may be used. However, in the Cold TES the range here is unalike the 
one shown in Figure 3-1 for the Argon cycles. It appears that the Cold TES the range 
between the Hotter and the Colder tank is broader here. More specifically, the lowest 
temperature was found to be as low as -55 oC, whereas the Hotter Cold TES tank 
temperature was equal to 33.6 oC in the base case for x=0. In the charging cycle. This can 
be attributed to the specific heat ratio, γ of Nitrogen being lower compared to that of 
Argon (1.667 for Argon versus 1.357 for Nitrogen). The temperature of -55 oC does not 
allow the use of the MEG-H2O mixture in the Nitrogen cycles, as it is not stable below -50 
oC [17]. Alternatives for the Cold TES storage medium should be sought in this case. 
The pressure ratios were 4.3 and 4.787 for the Discharging and Charging Nitrogen cycles, 
reflecting the dissimilar γ values between the two cycles. The operation of turbomachines 
was not isentropic here as well. The Nitrogen cycles were also regenerated, nevertheless 
the effect of adding internal heat exchange in Nitrogen cycles was not as profound as in 
the case of Argon cycles. In Figure 3-2 the temperature gradient between the points 3 and 
==PAGE:67==
63 
 
4 (in the precooler) of the Charging cycle appears to be more significant compared to the 
one shown in Figure 3-1. This implies that in the Nitrogen charging cycle more heat is 
dissipated in the precooler in order to heat up the air compared to the Argon charging 
cycle. It is always preferred to use the regenerator as much as possible over the precooler 
due to the fact that in the former heat is exchanged for free (it is recovered) between two 
streams of the same medium (working fluid).  
The following Tables, Table 4 and Table 5, show the variation of some characteristic 
indicators in the Charging cycle of System 1, under different x values. At this point it has 
to be noted that it was only the charging cycle that changed when x changed. The 
discharging cycle was only optimized once, given the desired Net Work output of 50 MWel. 
Moreover, the addition of extra Solar Field thermal energy affected only the operation of 
the charging cycle, as shown in Figure 2-2, since the discharging cycle merely receives 
heat from the tanks. Whether these tanks had been charged by means of the charging 
PTES cycle or from the Solar Field does not matter for the discharging cycle. Nonetheless, 
as discussed in Part 2.1.3, information from the Discharging cycle was transferred to the 
Charging cycle, rendering the two individual cycles a combined system.

The first important indicator presented in Table 4 is the (enthalpy_ratio)c which was 
defined in equation 2.52. Equation 2.53 shows that an increment in the value of x brings 
about an increment in the (enthalpy_ratio)c , which was verified by the model results given 
in Table 4. The next two variables in Table 4, namely (T[1]-T[2])c and (T[6]-T[5])c are 
directly linked to the denominator and numerator of equation 2.52, since specific enthalpy 
and temperature in ideal gas cycles are interrelated properties. (T[1]-T[2])c  is the 
temperature difference of the working fluid in the CSP/main heat exchanger and is 
==PAGE:68==
64 
 
therefore indicative of the amount of  heat charged to the Hot TES. In general, it seems to 
feature a decreasing trend under increasing x values. This is translated to less heat added 
to the Hot TES. At the same time the qprec_charg/qout_charg indicator that illustrates the ratio 
of heat that is transferred through the precooler over the total heat that exits the Charging 
cycle seems to be increasing at high x values. This means that more heat is wasted in the 
precooler, rather than charging the Hot TES. Then, the (T[6]-T[5])c  difference, which is 
proportional to the Cold thermal energy charged in the Cold Storage, is initially increased, 
nevertheless it then drops quickly, as the minimum bound for T[5] (the lowest 
temperature in the cycle) is reached. The (T[1]-T[2])c indicator then starts to decrease 
rapidly (after x=0.6) to account for the increase in the (enthalpy_ratio)c. The parameter echarg 
is defined in equation 2.22. It determines how well the regenerator performs compared 
to its ideal operating case (reversible heat transfer process). At low x values, internal heat 
exchange appears to be particularly effective and a significant portion of heat is 
recovered. Nevertheless, at high x values less heat is recovered, and more heat is 
exchanged in the precooler, which is undesired, as explained above. The final column of 
Table 4 gives the attained COP_el_to_thermal. This is the key performance indicator for heat 
pumps and it is defined in equations 2.33 and 2.34. As expected, it shows a decreasing 
trend at increasing x values. This happens due to the fact that the actual heat transferred 
to the Hot TES is reduced as the Solar Field contribution becomes more important (higher 
x). For x values higher than 0.4 the heat pump even becomes thermodynamically 
unfavorable, since the electrical energy charged in it outweighs the thermal load that is 
pumped through it. It is clear that under the assumptions considered in this MSc thesis 
for System 1, the lower the Solar Field input is, the more attractive the operation of the 
combined system becomes.

The trends that are depicted in Table 5 for the Nitrogen cycles are similar to those shown 
in Table 4 above for the Argon cycles. The most notable differences are spotted in the 
indicators that occupy the last three columns of the Table. The qprec_charg/qout_charg ratio 
seems to take higher values in the Nitrogen cycles than in the Argon cycles. This shows 
that in Nitrogen cycles, more heat is exchanged through the precooler than in Argon 
cycles. The difference is more prominent in low x values. In the base case, at x=0 the ratio 
in the Nitrogen cycles is three times higher compared to the respective one from Table 4. 
This is the first sign that Nitrogen cycles in System 1 are not as efficient as Argon cycles. 
The regenerator efficiency, echarg was also smaller for Nitrogen cycles, which shows that a 
larger portion of heat is not recovered, and that the temperature gradients that appear in 
the heat exchanger are more significant (thus the irreversibilities are also higher). All of 
these are reflected in the final COP value, which is lower in Table 5 compared to Table 4. 
The COP_el_to_thermal drops below the threshold of 1 even at very low Solar Field inputs. 
Despite the temperature and pressure lift being higher in the Nitrogen charging cycles, 
the achieved COP is lower, when compared to the Argon ones.   
Finally, the Round-trip efficiency (ηRT) of System 1, which was introduced in Figure 2-3 as 
the product of ηth_el (discharging cycle) and COP_el_to_thermal (charging cycle), under different 
x values is depicted in Figure 3-3 below. This indicator is considered to be the most 
decisive in determining the technical feasibility of a PTES system. As suggested by 
equation 2.80, a low ηRT value corresponds to a poor electrical output from the discharging 
cycle, rendering the system an unattractive thermal battery. In the present work, a 
threshold of 0.5 was applied in order to determine whether a PTES system makes sense 
from a technical point of view.  
 
Figure 3-3: The attained Round-trip efficiency (ηRT) for the Argon and Nitrogen cycles of System 1 under 
increasing Solar Field input (x from 0 to 1) 
The trendlines in Figure 3-3 indicate that there exists an almost linear correlation 
between the Solar Field contribution (x) and ηRT. Figure 3-3 also shows that for both 
working fluids the initially proposed configuration of System 1 is not promising, even for 
low x values. This finding contradicts the claims made by the authors of [17], regarding 
the potential of a Brayton-reverse Brayton PTES system integrated with a Solar Field as 
given in Figure 1-11.  Argon shows a slightly better behavior than Nitrogen as working 
fluid, which can be attributed to its superior COP as shown in Table 4 and Table 5 above. 
The ηth  indicator was higher in the Nitrogen discharging cycle (0.439) when compared to 
the Argon discharging cycle (0.412) and this explains why the mismatch in the attained 
==PAGE:70==
66 
 
ηRT from the two working fluids is not significant. It becomes more significant at high x 
values though, as the COP in Nitrogen cycles is decreased swiftly for high Solar Field 
inputs.  
One way to solve the problem with the low Round-trip efficiency would be to use an 
additional refrigeration cycle that accepts heat from the Cold Storage. The thermal load of 
this cycle would need to match the one from the Solar Field in order not to violate the 
energy balances. By adding this component the ηRT could be kept at its optimal value (i.e. 
the one shown for x=0 in Figure 3-3. However, an extra cost would have to be incurred in 
this case. 
3.2 System 2 Results 
The attained results from the analysis of System 2 can be divided in three categories. The 
first category incorporates the results from the individual components/subsystems 
models. A T-s diagram from the reverse Brayton heat pump cycle is given, together with 
some notable output from the heat pump and two-tank EES models. In the second 
category one can find the attained results from Off-design model of System 2, and more 
specifically for the base case (PV capacity 148.35 MW, Heat pump capacity 98.35 MW, SM 
of 4). It is reminded here that in the base case most of the components were over-
dimensioned as the goal was the maximization of the Capacity Factor (CF), irrespective of 
the LCOE. The electrical output from the whole system (Wout), from the Rankine cycle 
(Wpb) and from the PV (W_el_net,raw or WPV) for four characteristic days of the year, each 
representing one of the four seasons of the year, are also provided. Further, profiles of the 
thermal input to the Rankine cycle (Qhot), the thermal power charged to or discharged 
from the tanks (Qcharg), the thermal power produced by the Solar Field (QSF,raw) and the 
thermal output of the heat pump (Qhp) are given for the same four days. Lastly, the 
evolution of the level in each of the tanks (LHot and LCold) during a daily operating cycle for 
the same four days is shown as well. In the third and final results category for System 2, 
diagrams that illustrate the variation of the CF and LCOE indicators for different 
combinations of SM, Heat Pump and PV capacities can be found. A comparison between 
the performance of a bare CSP plant (modelled in SAM) and the suggested combined plant 
of System 2 (modelled in this MSc thesis) in terms of CF and LCOE is also established in 
this final part.  
3.2.1 Individual Components/subsystems 
The majority of the results presented in this part concern the heat pump component. The 
heat pump was modelled in EES and a step-by-step analysis was followed for the 
formulation of the final part-load equation of the component (equation 2.74).  
The first step in the analysis that is described in part 2.2.1 was the extraction of the 
optimized design for heat pump cycle. The heat pump of System 2 features two-stages of 
interheated expansion, uses the same Storage temperatures as System 1 for the Hot TES, 
and water at 35 oC in both interheaters. The T-s diagram for the design heat pump cycle 
of System 2 is depicted in Figure 3-4 below: 
 
==PAGE:71==
67 
 
 
Figure 3-4: The T-s diagram of the reverse Brayton heat pump of System 2 with Nitrogen as working fluid. 
In System 2 Nitrogen was preferred over Argon as the working fluid of the reverse-
Brayton heat pump, as it demonstrated a satisfactory behavior in the cycle. It is also less 
costly compared to Argon, and the manufacturers that provide turbomachines and heat 
exchangers that are compatible with Nitrogen are more numerous. 
Regarding the Hot TES tank storage temperatures, Figure 3-4 shows that the 
temperatures of the working fluid that charges the Hot Storage through the CSP heat 
exchanger (T[1] and T[2]) have a fair difference (gradient) from the Hot TES 
temperatures. More specifically, a lower bound of 15 oC was set for the temperature 
gradients in the CSP heat exchanger in the design cycle. The purpose of this bound was to 
ensure a substantial driving force for heat transfer in the heat exchanger and to guarantee 
that the temperature of the working fluid is always higher than that of the Storage 
medium, even under part-load conditions.  
In the heat pump cycle of Figure 3-4, the temperature lift from the cold to the hot side 
appears to be lower (since the heat source is at a higher temperature) compared to the 
respective lift in the charging cycle of System 1 (Figure 3-2) and thus one has to expect a 
smaller pressure ratio (Prat) in the design heat pump cycle of System 2.  
As for the temperature levels of the auxiliary water (that provides the low-grade heat in 
this cycle), namely T_water_in and T_water out, Figure 3-4 shows that only a minor gradient of 
10 oC exists between them. And despite the fact that the gradient between T[5] and T[7] 
of the working fluid (see also Figure 2-5)  and T_water_in (35 oC) seems reasonable, a much 
higher gradient appears to exist at design conditions between T[4] and T[6] of the 
working fluid and T_water out (25 oC) on the other side of the interheaters. This gradient 
secures an adequate driving force between the two streams in the interheaters at off-
design conditions. In part-load, the temperature of the working fluid is increased slightly 
and approaches that of the water, while T_water_in and T_water out remain unchanged. 
Lastly, the trivial differences between T[2] and T[8], and T[3] and T[7] imply that the 
regeneration process in the heat pump cycle of System 2 is particularly efficient and 
almost reversible. 
Other results of notice that were produced from the Design and Off-design analysis of the 
heat pump and Rankine cycle and from the Design EES model of the two-tank Hot TES 
subsystem are given in Table 6 that follows:

68

Table 6 shows that the achieved ηRT at design conditions is higher than the 0.5 threshold, 
which suggests a technically feasible system. Although the efficiency of the Rankine cycle, 
ηth_el turns out to be lower compared to that of the Brayton discharging cycle of System 1, 
the higher COP in the heat pump partially counterbalances this effect. The pressure ratio, 
Prat is lower in the heat pump of System 2, which is positive.  
Q_tot_preh_charg here is the total thermal power that is exchanged through the interheaters in 
the heat pump. Equation 2.58 shows that the interheaters provide the low-grade thermal 
power input, Qin to the cycle and it is in these components that most irreversibilities occur. 
These irreversibilities are mainly caused by the substantial thermal gradients in the 
interheater heat exchangers, that are clearly visible in Figure 3-4. Additionally, the 
thermal power recovered by means of the regenerator, Q_tot_reg_charg looks particularly high, 
even higher than the heat added to the Hot Storage, Q_tot_CSP_charg, which is crucial for 
achieving a high cycle efficiency.  
The COP values in Table 6 are actually COP_el_to_thermal. The lowest one is attained in the 
Design cycle and the highest at part-load conditions, since the irreversibilities that stem 
from the high temperature gradients tend to be reduced at Off-design. Still. The COP range 
in Table 6 seems to be superior to that of Table 5 and this clearly illustrates the positive 
effect of having interheated, multiple-stage expansion compared to single-stage 
expansion. As the authors of [17] point out, the addition of more stages of interheated 
expansion leads to a subsequent increase in the turbine work, which reduces the 
compression work (and the net electrical work, W_el_net) required by the heat pump. 
Ultimately, this brings about an increase in the COP.  
Moving to the two-tank subsystem design parameters that are given in Table 6, 17 Full 
load hours of Storage (H) were chosen for the design of the tanks. As suggested by 
equation 2.83, this effectively means that the tanks need to be able to accommodate 17 
times more thermal power than the Rankine power block input. This leads to particularly 
bulky tanks, with substantial volumes, VH and VC. Furthermore, the Hot tank was assumed 
to be initially empty (LHot,initial =0), whereas the Cold tank was assumed to be full with 
==PAGE:73==
69 
 
molten salt (LCold,initial =1), during the first time step of the Off-design simulation. This 
means that during the first night of the year, the Hot tank could not be discharged and 
thus electricity could not be produced from the Rankine power block. This assumption 
was used in order to account for the worst-case-scenario CF value. Lastly, the Msalt variable 
shows the tank mass (same for both tanks) that is able to move in and out of them. This 
mass fills up 70% of the geometrical volume of each tank, as explained in 2.2.5. On the 
other hand, Msalt,req is the total mass that corresponds to the total geometrical volume of 
the tank. It was assumed that Msalt,req represents the total salt mass that has to be 
purchased for the Solar Field-TES system.  
3.2.2 Off-design model base case operational scenario 
In the present thesis, it was chosen to pursue the highest possible Capacity Factor (CF) in 
the base case scenario. Despite the fact that a maximized CF was the target though, the 
variations in the intensity of sunlight, which is the energy source of both the Solar field 
and the Solar PV system across the year brings about different profiles for the Electricity, 
thermal energy and tank levels. Starting from a typical Winter Day, which is a Summer 
Day in South Africa (location of the plant), the produced electrical work output from the 
Rankine cycle (Wpb), the total electricity output (Wout) and the total power produced by 
the Solar PV system, before curtailment (W_el_net,raw) are shown over a 24-hour period in 
Figure 3-5 below:  
 
Figure 3-5: The Rankine power block output (Wpb), the total electrical output (Wout) and the raw PV electricity 
output (Wel_net_raw) for a typical Winter Day (daily cycle) 
From Figure 3-5 it can be observed that the attained Wpb profile for the Winter Day is in 
agreement with the qualitative one from Figure 2-8. As expected, during a day with 
abundant sunlight, the main purpose of the Rankine power cycle is to produce at night, 
when there is no sunlight available. Subsequently, as the Solar PV output starts to rise, the 
Rankine power output gradually drops to zero. As soon as the PV energy reaches the 
threshold of 50 MWel, the charging of the TES begins. It can be seen that during this 
specific day, the nominal power production from the PV system was achieved (148.35 
MWel). Moreover, the total output is kept constant at 50 MWel, which indicates that the 
plant is able to deliver power at its nominal conditions throughout the whole day. 
Consequently, the profiles of the thermal input to the Rankine cycle (Qhot), the thermal 
power charged to and discharged from the TES (Qcharg), the Solar Field output before 
curtailment (QSF,raw), and the heat charged through the heat pump (Qcharg) for the same 
Winter (Summer) Day are given in Figure 3-6: 
==PAGE:74==
70 
 
 
 
Figure 3-6: The Rankine cycle thermal input (Qhot), the thermal power charged to the Hot TES (Qcharg), the raw 
Solar Field output profile (QSF_raw) and the thermal power charged to the TES by the heat pump for a typical 
Winter Day (daily cycle) 
From Figure 3-6 it can be seen that the thermal power flows are far superior than 
electrical power flows, and this is a result of the particularly high SM value that was 
adopted in the base case scenario. The Q_SF_raw profile is again in agreement with the 
expected one from Figure 2-8,  nonetheless it appears that the Solar Field produces during 
less hours compared to the PV. This is due to the fact that the latter is able to use both the 
Direct and Diffuse sun radiation, as opposed to the former, which relies exclusively on the 
Direct Normal Irradiation (DNI). The Qhot profile is in agreement with the Wpb profile from 
Figure 3-5 regarding the time frame.  
With regards to Qhp it seems to start increasing from zero values during the morning hours 
and as the Wel_net,raw profile from Figure 3-5 surpasses the threshold of 50 MWel. However, 
after only a few hours in operation the heat pump appears to cease its heat production. 
The same phenomenon seems to occur for the thermal energy charged to the tanks, Qcharg. 
This sudden drop in the two profiles can be attributed to the Level in the Hot tank (LHot) 
reaching its maximum. After this point, the charging process cannot continue and thus the 
thermal profiles in Figure 3-6 swiftly drop to zero.  
Lastly, Figure 3-6 illustrates that the load discharged from the Hot TES during night, Qcharg 
(here it is negative by convention to show that energy is dispatched from the Storage) 
matches exactly the thermal input to the Rankine power block, Qhot. This implies that all 
of the energy required to run the Rankine cycle in the absence of sunlight is provided 
exclusively by the Storage, as expected.  
The daily profiles of the salt Levels in the Hot (LHot) and Cold (LCold) tank for the same 
Winter Day are presented in Figure 3-7 that follows: 
==PAGE:75==
71 
 
 
Figure 3-7: The Levels in the Hot and Cold tank for a daily cycle during a typical Winter Day 
Figure 3-7 shows that the Level in the Hot tank appears to be equal to 1 minus the Level 
in the Cold tank at all times, which was expected. Initially, at night hours the Level in the 
Hot tank drops linearly, while the respective Level in the Cold tank is increased linearly 
at the same rate. Subsequently, once the Wel_net,raw PV profile from Figure 3-5 exceeds 50 
MWel, a gradual increment in the Hot tank level is observed. The increment rate becomes 
even greater at around 9 am, which coincides with the time that the heat pump starts 
working in Figure 3-6. When the maximum level is reached, the addition of heat to the 
TES is ceased at around 11.45 am. From this point the tank levels are not altered until 6 
pm. At 6 pm the PV output drops below 50 MWel as it can be seen from Figure 3-5. Finally, 
the Hot tank level starts decreasing again, as thermal energy is pumped to the Rankine 
power block.  
The respective Work, Thermal power and Tank Level profiles for a typical Autumn 
(Spring in South Africa) and Spring (Autumn in South Africa) Day are depicted in Figure 
3-8, Figure 3-9 and Figure 3-10 respectvely: 
 
 
Figure 3-8: The Rankine power block output (W_pb), the total electrical output (Wout) and the raw PV electricity 
output (Wel_net_raw) for a typical Autumn and Spring Day (daily cycles) 
 
==PAGE:76==
72 
 
 
Figure 3-9: The Rankine cycle thermal input (Qhot), the thermal power charged to the Hot TES (Qcharg), the raw 
Solar Field output profile (QSF_raw) and the thermal power charged to the TES by the heat pump for a typical 
Autumn and Spring Day (daily cycles) 
 
 
Figure 3-10: The Levels in the Hot and Cold tank for a daily cycle during a typical Autumn and Spring Day 
Figure 3-9 shows that the Solar Field remains out of operation during the Spring Day, as 
the DNI is not sufficient for it to produce thermal power. Simultaneously, the PV only 
exceeds the thereshold of 0.15*Pmax (Pmax in this case is 98.35 MWel for the heat pump) 
that is needed for the heat pump to operate during one hour of the day, namely from 13.00 
to 14.00 in the afternoon. During this hour the TES is charged from the heat pump and a 
minuscule variation in the tank levels is observed in Figure 3-10. Subsequently, at 16.00 
the PV electrical production is reduced below 50 MWel and the stored thermal energy is 
dispatched to the Rankine power block, which produces power. The production is 
stopped though, when the Level in the Hot tank drops to zero again. During this day the 
system only produces its nominal power output for approximately 4.5 hours.  
As for the Autumn day, the conditions (sun thermal radiation) appear to be more 
favorable for the operation of System 2. Initially, the Hot tank is empty and thus the 
Rankine power block is not operational, limiting the power output to zero. Then, when 
the PV system starts producing power (Wel_net,raw), the total electricity output, Wout seems 
to follow its profile, until it reaches the desired output of 50 MWel. At 10.00 the 15% 
threshold that is required for the operation of the heat pump is surpassed and electricity 
is stored to the Hot TES through the heat pump. At 12.00 the Solar Field starts supplying 
heat to the Hot TES as well, and the rate of increment of the Hot tank Level is greatly 
enhanced, as shown in Figure 3-10. This rate is then again slowed down at 14.00 when 
the Solar Field ceases production. Finally, at around 17.00, the PV electricity is reduced 
below 50 MWel and LHot begins decreasing, as the TES subsystem starts to provide power 
==PAGE:77==
73 
 
to the Rankine cycle. Around midnight, the Level in the Hot tank has been decreased to 
zero and the power production from the Rankine cycle is terminated.  
Finally, the Summer Day profiles (Winter in South Africa) are shown in Figure 3-11, Figure 
3-12 and Figure 3-13 below: 
 
 
Figure 3-11: The Rankine power block output (Wpb), the total electrical output (Wout) and the raw PV 
electricity output (Wel_net_raw) for a typical Summer Day (daily cycle) 
 
Figure 3-12: The Rankine cycle thermal input (Qhot), the thermal power charged to the Hot TES (Qcharg), the raw 
Solar Field output profile (QSF_raw) and the thermal power charged to the TES by the heat pump for a typical 
Summer Day (daily cycle) 
==PAGE:78==
74 
 
 
Figure 3-13: The Levels in the Hot and Cold tank for a daily cycle during a typical Summer Day 
By observing Figure 3-12 and Figure 3-13, it becomes clear that this Summer (Winter 
Day) has so little sun thermal potential that the Solar Field does not produce at all. At the 
same time the Solar PV subsystem appears to have an output of only 25 MWel (out of the 
148.35 that are installed). With this output, the desired electrical load of 50 MWel is not 
achieved, therefore no energy is available for the heat pump and Qhp never rises above 
zero-levels. With the Heat pump and the Solar Field offline, there is no way to charge the 
TES (Qcharg is also zero) and the level in the Hot tank stays at its minimum, while the level 
in the Cold tank is constantly at the maximum level allowed, as it can be seen in Figure 
3-13. Under these circumstances, the Rankine cycle is not operational as well, and neither 
Qhot, nor Wpb get values higher than zero. The system works constantly under the 
undesired Case/Mode 16 shown in Figure 2-9 throughout this day. This day (June 14) was 
in fact the coldest day of the year (2019) according to available weather data and it was 
chosen on purpose as a representative Cold day with low sun thermal radiation.   
Moreover, some interesting facts regarding the utilization of certain components of 
System 2 throughout the year are given in bullet points below. The percentages were 
calculated from the Off-design output for the whole year.  
• 
21.53% of the Initial Solar PV electricity (Wel_net,raw) is curtailed (Wel_net). This can 
be attributed to the Hot tank being full (or the Cold tank being empty) and also to 
the PV load being higher than 50 MW, but lower than the 15% threshold that is 
needed to turn on the heat pump at this time step. 
• 
The curtailed electricity (Wel_net) corresponds to 18,3% of the total annual power 
production (Wout). This significant electricity curtailment could be reduced by 
adopting an even higher value in the Full load hours of Storage, H for the TES 
subsystem. As Figure 3-5 and Figure 3-6 show, electricity is often curtailed during 
sunny days. Thus, using bulkier tanks could help alleviate this undesired 
curtailment issue. 
• 
39.75% of the thermal power output (QSF,raw) of the Solar Field is curtailed (QSF). 
This may happen because during several time steps, the tank Levels do not allow 
for charging. Another cause could be that during some time steps the thermal 
output is less than the 15% threshold that is required for the pumps to operate. 
Further, thermal output originating from the Solar Field may be wasted because 
the Rankine power block does not need to produce during this time step (the PV 
cover the grid demand). The substantial curtailment ratio in the Solar Field output 
in the Base-case scenario is also driven by the high Solar Multiple value. 
==PAGE:79==
75 
 
• 
35.07% of the PV electricity that is not curtailed is used to charge the heat pump. 
This shows that the primary role of the PV system is to produce electricity despite 
the fact that it is over-dimensioned here. However, since the Rankine cycle is the 
main electricity producer at night and during days with less sunlight, the 
contribution of Wpb in the total annual electrical output, Wout is as high as 56.8%.  
• 
The maximum and minimum Level in both tanks throughout the year is 1 and 0 
respectively. This means that the active volume of the tanks is fully used in the 
Base case scenario. Moreover, it is estimated that in the base-case scenario the 
Level in the Hot tank was at its highest point during 11,46% of the year. This 
provides an additional argument in support of the observation that was made 
above regarding the need for larger Storage tanks.  
Finally, the most important economic indicators that were extracted from the economic 
model of the base-case scenario (see also part 2.2.7) are included in Table 7 below:
