From Table 7 it is observed that the Heat pump and the Solar PV subsystems have similar 
contributions to the Total System 2 CAPEX. The CSP, which includes the Rankine power 
block and the Hot TES tanks has by far the highest CAPEX. This is expected since this is 
the most complex part of System 2. As for the Molten Salt HTF, its CAPEX seems to 
constitute only a small fraction of the total System cost, despite its mass being substantial 
as Table 6 shows. In fact, the Molten Salt CAPEX appears to be comparable with the Fixed 
annual O&M cost, which is also quite significant. The total System 2 CAPEX features a 
particularly high value as well, which shows why the base-case-scenario System 2 would 
not be a very attractive investment choice, despite the fact that it features a remarkable 
CF value. Lastly, the attained LCOE value is quite high too, nonetheless LCOE values of 
around 150 $/MWh,el are not unheard of for combined CSP plants [42].  
That said, the optimization (minimization) of LCOE with respect to three key parameters 
is discussed in part 3.2.3 that follows. The target of the sensitivity analysis is to investigate 
whether high electricity outputs, in [MWh, el] can still be achieved if the component 
nominal capacities (and therefore costs) are reduced.  
3.2.3 Sensitivity analysis/Optimization  
In 3.2.2 above, the results that were produced by the analysis of the base-case-scenario 
System 2 were presented. In this system, the Capacity Factor takes the highest value 
possible. Nevertheless, over-dimensioning the components to achieve this high CF causes 
==PAGE:80==
76 
 
an increase in the total investment cost of the system (CAPEX), which may not be 
compensated by the high electrical energy output. Since the LCOE parameter is defined as 
the total cost that is incurred over the lifetime of an investment, it should be capable of 
indicating the optimal component capacity that minimizes the cost and maximizes the 
electrical output.   In other words, the LCOE can help one to identify until which point they 
ought to over-dimension the components of the System in order to get the most cost-
effective output from them. This suggests that the LCOE may well be utilized as a guide 
for the optimal system. 
In this section the results that were acquired from the Off-design models for all 
combinations of 7 SM values (4,3.5,3,2.5,2,1.5 and 1), 7 heat pump nominal capacities 
(98.35, 85, 70, 55, 40, 25 and 10 MWel) and 5 PV capacities (148.35, 135, 120, 75 and 60 
MWel) are given. More specifically, the attained CF and LCOE values for the 7 SM, and 7 
Heat pump capacities are given in two distinct diagrams, for each of the 5 tested PV 
capacities. The Full Load Hours of Storage, H parameter was not varied in the present 
sensitivity analysis and the therefore the tank capacity was taken as constant. The 
ultimate target is to spot the optimal set of PV capacity – Heat pump capacity – SM for 
System 2. The performance of this cost optimal System 2 is finally compared in terms of 
its curtailment characteristics with the base-case-scenario performance.  
Furthermore, in order to establish a comparison between the proposed configuration of 
System 2 and a commercial, bare CSP plant (without Heat pump and PV support) the 
obtained results of CF and LCOE from SAM (under the same 7 SM values) are also shown 
with dotted lines. Note here that while the CF was taken directly from SAM, the LCOE was 
calculated based on the same CRF, and using the assumption that the Fixed O&M cost is 
equal to 2% of the total CAPEX, which was also used in System 2. The same CSP and molten 
salt HTF CAPEX costs that were used for system 2 were also adopted for the Bare CSP 
plant. With these assumptions, the Bare CSP plant and System 2 were compared on equal 
terms. The Bare CSP CF and LCOE were only affected by changes in the SM. Since a bare 
CSP does not include a PV and Heat pump, varying the capacities of these components 
cannot change the CF and the LCOE of the Bare CSP. 
The CF and LCOE diagrams that correspond to a PV Capacity of 148.35 MWel (the same as 
in the base-case-scenario) are given in Figure 3-14 below: 
 
 
Figure 3-14: The variation in the CF and LCOE parameters under 7 different SM and 7 different heat pump 
capacities for a PV size of 148.35 MWel in System 2 
==PAGE:81==
77 
 
From Figure 3-14 it can be seen that irrespective of the Heat pump capacity, the attained 
CF at high SM values (from 2.5 to 4) are all very close to each other. The CF is generally 
decreased at low Solar Multiples, as the thermal energy provided by the Solar Field tends 
to become equal with the nominal thermal input of the Rankine cycle (which happens for 
SM=1). At lower SM, the discrepancy in the achieved Capacity Factors between the 
different heat pump capacities starts to become more obvious. This behavior can be 
explained by the fact that at higher SM values the thermal energy provided by the Solar 
Field is far superior to the thermal energy provided by the heat pump, and thus even for 
small heat pump capacities the CF does not change significantly. In general, the CF drops 
as the heat pump size is decreased for all different SM values, which is expected, since less 
thermal energy is pumped in the TES if the heat pump capacity is smaller. The advantage 
of the combined System 2 over a classic Bare CSP plant in terms of the CF that they can 
achieve is clear. Even at a SM of 4 (which means that the nominal thermal output of the 
Solar Field is 4 times larger than that of the Rankine power block), the Bare CSP is not 
able to surpass the 65% Capacity Factor threshold. On the contrary, for the combined 
System 2, the CF only drops below 65% at the smallest heat pump capacities of 25 MWel 
and 10 MWel.  
As it can be observed from the LCOE variation diagram, combining a particularly large PV 
subsystem with a much smaller heat pump results in significant electricity curtailment, 
since the heat pump is not able to utilize the majority of the produced PV electricity above 
its nominal capacity. Consequently, the total electricity output is greatly reduced for SM 
values below 2 for the 25 MW and 10 MW heat pumps and the resulting LCOE rises swiftly. 
It is seen that for heat pumps with capacities larger than 55 MW, the reduction in the LCOE 
values that are attained for the same SM is almost constant as the heat pump sizes are 
diminished. Since the obtained CF is quite similar for these higher heat pump capacities, 
the respective LCOE value is only reduced with the heat pump investment cost. This 
causes a uniform trend in the LCOE profiles for heat pumps that are larger than 55 MW. 
Conversely, having a smaller heat pump than 55 MW, starts to affect the CF more as the 
curtailment becomes increasingly important. This causes the LCOE to decrease at a 
smaller rate for capacities below 40 MW. For the 25 and 10 MW heat pumps and for SM 
lower than 2, the drop in the CF (and thus the electrical output of the plant) becomes more 
crucial than the drop in investment costs, which results in very unfavorable LCOE values. 
As for the Bare CSP, it only becomes competitive in LCOE terms at large SM values, namely 
higher than 3, due to the fact that it does not include the extra heat pump and PV costs. 
Nevertheless, after this point the lower investment cost of the Bare CSP gets less 
important compared to the significant electrical output gains (that are reflected in the 
Capacity Factors) of the Combined system 2.  
The minimum LCOE is met for a SM of 1.5 and a Heat pump Capacity of 25 MW and it is 
equal to 134 $/MWh. This value is lower than the optimal LCOE value obtained from the 
Bare CSP, which is around 140 $/MWh. This illustrates that even with an over-
dimensioned PV system (which features a substantial investment cost) the proposed 
combined configuration of System 2 is promising. 
The respective diagrams for a PV Capacity of 135 MWel are depicted in Figure 3-15: 
==PAGE:82==
78 
 
 
Figure 3-15: The variation in the CF and LCOE parameters under 7 different SM and 7 different heat pump 
capacities for a PV size of 135 MWel in System 2 
Figure 3-15 shows that the LCOE profiles follow a similar trend as those in Figure 3-14. 
The minimum LCOE is only slightly lower compared to the one in Figure 3-14, which 
indicates that a reduction of around 15 MW in the PV system does not provoke a 
significant LCOE reduction. All of the attained LCOE shown in Figure 3-15 are only slightly 
lower than those from Figure 3-14 and most of the comments that were made for the 
Figure above are the same for this one as well. For the larger heat pumps (above 55 MW), 
the minimum LCOE appears for a SM of 1.5. As opposed to Figure 3-14 though, the LCOE 
values do not tend to flatten out (remain constant) beyond this point, but they seem to 
increase. This shows that at very low Solar Field thermal inputs, the difference of 15 MW 
in the PV size can make a difference in the electricity output of the plant, which is reflected 
on the LCOE.  
With regards to the Capacity Factor diagram, the trendlines that correspond to heat pump 
capacities of 98.35 and 85 MW appear to overlap in Figure 3-15, due to the fact that the 
largest heat pump (98.35 MW) cannot operate at its nominal output point for a PV input 
that is less than 148.35 MW (98.35 + 50). This effectively means that the two 
aforementioned heat pumps produce almost exactly the same thermal load, and therefore 
have almost identical Capacity Factors, in the absence of other differences. Moreover, the 
CF values seem to remain unchanged or only slightly changed in the high SM region, since 
the abundant thermal power provided by the Solar Field counterbalances the thermal 
energy that is lost (not charged to the TES system) from the heat pump. However, the 
discrepancies tend to become more intense for the low SM values, as the Solar Field 
thermal power is reduced significantly in this region. The System tends to rely more on 
the heat pump for TES charging, and as the capacity is reduced, so does the thermal load 
that is charged in the tanks and the Capacity Factor. It is likely that at low SM values and 
heat pump capacities, the Hot tank is never fully charged (and the Cold tank is never fully 
discharged) and some of its thermal capacity is not utilized. This is an undesired scenario 
that leads to sub-optimal component usage and high LCOE.  
From Figure 3-14 and Figure 3-15 it is also observed that at very small heat pump 
capacities (10 and 25 MW) the LCOE is optimized for larger SM values (at SM=2). In all 
other heat pump sizes, the optimal LCOE is obtained at SM=1.5. This suggests that a 
combined system with a smaller heat pump, requires additional energy from the Solar 
Field in order to operate optimally. Since the PV capacity is still high in Figure 3-14 and 
Figure 3-15, and a significant fraction of the total electrical output, Wout is produced by 
==PAGE:83==
79 
 
the Photovoltaics, the lowest LCOE for the majority of the examined heat pump capacities 
is found for at a particularly small SM value, and more specifically at 1.5. 
The optimal LCOE in Figure 3-15 is equal to 131.36 $/MWh and it is achieved for a heat 
pump of 10 MW and a SM of 2. However, this combination is still considered to be sub-
optimal, due to the significant electricity curtailment that occurs when a large PV and a 
much smaller heat pump are used.  
The Tables that include all of the CF and LCOE values that are plotted in Figure 3-14, 
Figure 3-15, Figure 3-16, Figure 3-17 and Figure 3-18 can be seen in Appendix 6.2.9. 
The attained CF and LCOE diagrams that were extracted from the sensitivity analysis 
output for a 120 MWel PV system are shown in Figure 3-16 below:  
 
Figure 3-16: The variation in the CF and LCOE parameters under 7 different SM and 7 different heat pump 
capacities for a PV size of 120 MWel in System 2 
The trend that was observed in Figure 3-14 and Figure 3-15 regarding the CF values 
getting closer to each other regardless of the SM, at lower heat pump capacities is also 
seen in Figure 3-16. This happens because as the PV size is reduced, the available energy 
for the heat pumps is reduced too, rendering the system more dependent on the Solar 
Field for charging. This reduces the importance of heat pump capacity as an optimization 
parameter, and brings the Capacity Factor lines closer to each other. Furthermore, it 
seems that in any case, choosing a PV system that has a capacity equal to 50 MW plus the 
nominal Heat pump capacity maximizes the Capacity Factor of the combined system. This 
is shown more clearly in Figure 3-17 and Figure 3-18, which concern smaller PV 
subsystems. The CF trendlines for Heat pump capacities greater or equal to 70 MW appear 
to overlap completely, since all of these heat pumps cannot use more than 70 MW from 
the PV electrical output to charge the TES. The PV size here is equal to 120 MW (50+70), 
which implies that it is not possible for it to provide more than 70 MW to the heat pump 
at any time.  
As for the LCOE, the trendlines are similar to those from Figure 3-15 and the variations 
are only minor. The optimal LCOE is still lower compared to the one from Figure 3-15, 
which suggests that 120 MWel is still not the optimal PV size. The LCOE becomes optimal 
at SM=1.5 and 2 and is substantially increased for the lowest SM. As the PV capacity keeps 
decreasing, the system gravitates towards larger SM values in order to maximize its 
output (for which the lowest LCOE is found). This effect becomes more and more 
profound as the PV capacity is decreased. Figure 3-16 also shows that the lowest LCOE 
values achieved for System 2 are comparable with those for the Bare CSP plant. That again 
==PAGE:84==
80 
 
shows the potential of combined systems, such as the one proposed in this MSc thesis 
(System 2).  
The lowest LCOE is found for SM=2 and a heat pump size of 10 MWel and it is equal to 128 
$/MWh. So far, it has been observed that a drop of 15 MW in heat pump capacity causes 
the LCOE to go down by 3 $/MWh. This suggests that an almost linear relation exists 
between the LCOE and the PV capacity, at high PV system sizes. 
In Figure 3-17 and Figure 3-18 that are presented next, the CF and LCOE trendlines that 
correspond to smaller PV systems are shown. In the diagrams of Figure 3-17, the PV 
system has a capacity of 75 MW (just 25 more than the nominal System output). Finally, 
the CF and LCOE values that are shown in the diagrams of Figure 3-18 correspond to a PV 
system with a nominal capacity of 60 MW: 
 
Figure 3-17: The variation in the CF and LCOE parameters under 7 different SM and 7 different heat pump 
capacities for a PV size of 75 MWel in System 2 
 
 
Figure 3-18: The variation in the CF and LCOE parameters under 7 different SM and 7 different heat pump 
capacities for a PV size of 60 MWel in System 2 
To begin with, the first conclusion that can be extracted from Figure 3-17 and Figure 3-18 
is that the Capacity Factors are lower when compared to those presented in Figure 3-14, 
==PAGE:85==
81 
 
Figure 3-15 and Figure 3-16. This trend is expected since having a smaller PV system 
means that its electricity output exceeds the nominal load of 50 MW less often. Therefore, 
not only is the electricity dispatched to the grid reduced, but also less energy is charged 
to the heat pump. A direct consequence is a reduction in the attained Capacity Factor. In 
Figure 3-18, the CF value drops below 50% for the first time, which is in contradiction 
with the expected baseload plant operation. Even so, the Capacity Factor of the combined 
system is substantially higher when compared to that of a Bare CSP plant. This signifies 
that the addition of a relatively small PV system and a Heat pump can greatly enhance the 
performance of a TES-integrated CSP plant. In general, a combined system that features a 
Capacity Factor higher than that of the Bare CSP plant and an LCOE lower than that of the 
Bare CSP plant is deemed as acceptable.  
In Figure 3-17 the CF appears to independent from the Heat pump capacity. As discussed 
above, using a heat pump that is larger than the maximum excess electrical PV load does 
not bring any benefits to the obtained Capacity Factor. Furthermore, if the maximum 
excess PV load matches exactly the Heat pump capacity, the optimum performance is 
observed. This is verified by the CF trendlines that are shown in Figure 3-17 and Figure 
3-18. In the former, the CF that corresponds to the 25 MW heat pump is the highest, 
whereas in the latter, the CF that is obtained by the 10 MW heat pump seems to be the 
optimal. The Capacity Factor appears to flatten out, approaching its highest value at a 
Solar Multiple of 4. This happens because if the Solar Field size is increased too much, the 
Hot tank is filled quickly during days with abundant sunlight, and subsequently thermal 
energy curtailment has to occur. This is verified in Figure 3-7, which shows the variation 
in the Hot and Cold tank levels during a sunny day. On the other hand, low Solar Multiple 
values deprive the Hot tanks from their main thermal energy source. In case a small heat 
pump is utilized as well, the maximum level in the Hot tank is never reached, which also 
results in a sub-optimal use of the TES system. To sum up, both very low and very high 
SM values do not allow certain components of the system to operate optimally, and 
therefore, the lowest LCOE is always achieved for intermediate SM values.  
Moving to the LCOE, the profiles shown in Figure 3-17 and Figure 3-18 look unlike those 
from Figure 3-14, Figure 3-15 and Figure 3-16, which suggests that  noticeable differences 
exist between the profiles that correspond to high PV capacities and those that 
correspond to lower PV capacities. For higher PV capacities, the optimal LCOE tends to be 
achieved at low SM values, namely 1.5 and in some cases 2. On the contrary, for lower PV 
sizes, SM values of 2 and 2.5 produces the lowest LCOEs. Moreover, Figure 3-17 and Figure 
3-18 show that the LCOE rises steeply for Solar Multiples that are lower than 2 for all heat 
pump sizes. The System 2 LCOE profile tends to resemble the Bare CSP profile more. This 
is attributed to the fact that electricity production is heavily penalized under the 
combined effect of low Solar Multiples and small PV capacities. Thus, the LCOE is 
increased significantly. The lowest LCOEs are driven towards higher SM values, as the 
available PV electricity is diminished. Furthermore, Figure 3-17 and Figure 3-18 both 
illustrate that the LCOE clearly drops with the Heat pump capacity and the discrepancies 
between the neighboring trendlines are almost the same in all cases (with the exception 
of the 25 and 10 MW Heat pump sizes where the differences in the obtained LCOE are 
smaller). Since the attained Capacity Factors are almost identical for all Heat Pump 
capacities, the LCOE values are affected solely by the cost differences. The Heat Pump 
CAPEX is decreased at smaller Heat pump sizes, and this brings the Total CAPEX, as well 
as the final LCOE down.  
Finally, the lowest LCOE out of all seems to be attained for the PV Capacity of 75 MW. At 
60 MW, the LCOE values start to rise again. Furthermore, this LCOE is observed for a Heat 
pump capacity of 10 MW and a SM of 2. Although the 25 MW Heat pump is the one that 
maximizes the electricity output and the Capacity Factor of the plant, the lower CAPEX of 
the 10 MW Heat pump appears to give the best LCOE in this case. The final optimal LCOE 
==PAGE:86==
82 
 
is only barely above 120 $/MWh, a 36% decrease compared to the base-case-scenario 
LCOE.  The CF that corresponds to the optimal component capacities (PV 75 MW- Heat 
Pump 10 MW-SM 2) is equal to 69.4%, which is still higher compared to the optimal Bare 
CSP CF of 63.9%. This indicates that by adding a relatively small PV plant and a Heat pump 
to a classic Bare CSP (with a TES) can be really beneficial.  
Finally, some interesting curtailment statistics from the Off-design model output of the 
optimized (lowest LCOE) System 2 are presented in bullet-point form below: 
• 
Only 7.72% of the Initial Solar PV electricity (Wel_netraw) is curtailed (Wel_net). In the 
base case, system the curtailed electricity accounts for 21.3% (or 1/5) of the PV 
output. 
• 
The curtailed electricity (Wel_net) accounts for 4.11% of annual net electrical output 
(Wout) of the system. In the base case, system, the respective percentage is 18.3%. 
• 
Owing to the fact that the Solar Multiple of the optimized System 2 is half that of 
the base-case-scenario System 2, the curtailment in the Solar Field is only 3.47% 
for the optimized system. For comparison, the Solar Field curtailment in the base-
case-scenario system is 39.75%, or 10 times higher.  
• 
The electrical load charged to the heat pump is 9.43% of the total, uncurtailed PV 
output in the optimized system. In the base-case-scenario system this percentage 
is 35.07%.  
• 
The maximum and minimum Level in both tanks throughout the year is 1 and 0 
respectively. This means that the active volume of the tanks is fully used in the 
optimized system. However, the Hot tank is at its highest level during only 0.71% 
of the year. In the base-case scenario the respective percentage is 11,46%. 
Despite the fact that an optimization process was carried out, it is not known whether the 
Optimized Heat Pump, PV and Solar Field capacities truly correspond to the ideal system 
as the attained CF is relatively low. In the base-case-scenario, the Solar Field integrated-
PTES system can store electrical energy that is three times higher (148.35 MW) than its 
nominal output (50 MW) and this is something positive.  In any case, the Capacity Factor 
of the optimized System 2 plant is still fine, even for a baseload plant [51], especially 
considering that System 2 is an entirely Renewable System, capable of storing and 
dispatching green energy to the grid. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:87==
83 
 
4 Conclusions and suggestions for Future Work 
4.1 Conclusions 
In this MSc thesis an analysis of two different system configurations for Concentrated 
Solar power plants (CSP) integrated with Pumped Thermal Energy Storage (PTES) 
systems was conducted. The first system (System 1) was based on Brayton/reverse 
Brayton, recuperated, one-stage, discharging and charging PTES cycles and a TES system 
consisting of four tanks (two Hot and two Cold) with two separate storage media in them. 
Further, two different working fluids were tested for the PTES thermodynamic cycles, 
namely Argon and Nitrogen. The discharging cycle was optimized only once since the 
design electrical output was fixed at 50 MWel, while the charging cycle was optimized 
several times, depending on the Solar Field input. The charging and discharging cycles 
were tied through energy balances, which also involved the variable Solar Field thermal 
input. The results showed that the stand-alone PTES (with zero Solar Field input) can 
achieve Round-trip efficiencies, ηRT of 54.5%(Argon) and 53% (Nitrogen), which are 
acceptable. Furthermore, in these stand-alone PTES cycles, a significant amount of heat 
was recovered in the regenerator and the irreversibilities produced by the finite 
temperature differences in heat exchangers were minimized. However, even the addition 
of small thermal inputs from the Solar Field, not greater than 20% of the charging PTES 
thermal load, deteriorated the performance of the combined system. The temperature 
gradients in the heat exchangers were driven towards particularly high (and thus 
unfavorable) values, and a substantial fraction of heat that should have been charged to 
the Hot TES was wasted in the precooler, in order to preheat the air there. The COP of the 
heat pump dropped below 1, rendering its operation impossible. As a result, ηRT was 
reduced below the value of 0.5 that was chosen as the deciding threshold, even at 
particularly low Solar Field thermal inputs. Consequently, System 1 was found to be 
technically non-feasible system, according to the assumptions that were taken.  
The second system (System 2) featured a Rankine power cycle for discharge, a reverse 
Brayton heat pump for charge, two Hot tanks, water (assumed abundant) acting as the 
heat source, replacing the Cold Storage and a PV subsystem. The PV electrical output was 
sent directly to the grid if it was lower or equal to the nominal load of 50 MWel. If it was 
higher than 50 MWel, the excess electricity was charged into the heat pump and into the 
TES as thermal energy. The Solar Field and PV subsystems were both modelled in SAM 
(System Advisor Model) and the yearly output was used as input, representative of these 
components, in the overall System 2 Off-design model. The Rankine power cycle was not 
modelled in detail, and its part-load performance was given by an equation found in 
Literature. The interactions between all components were described in detail in a Control 
diagram, denoted as System operational strategy scheme. Lastly, an economic model was 
set up, the Levelized Cost of Electricity (LCOE) for the whole system was estimated, and a 
sensitivity analysis was performed with the aim of identifying the optimal PV size, Heat 
pump capacity and Solar Field size (Solar Multiple or SM). The attained Off-design model 
results showed that a Capacity Factor as high as 86.3% can be achieved with over-
dimensioned components, nonetheless the corresponding LCOE is equal to 188.29 
$/MWh, which is really high. Since the Off-design output was for one year, the variations 
in the thermal, electricity and tank level profiles under different sunlight conditions (each 
represented by a different season) were investigated. The results indicated that during 
days with abundant sunlight the tanks are quickly filled and undesired curtailment takes 
place. On the other hand, during cold days the sun potential does not allow for TES 
charging and thus the levels in the tanks are not varied. The sensitivity analysis results 
indicated that System 2 can compete with a classic CSP plant, both in terms of Capacity 
Factor and LCOE.  
==PAGE:88==
84 
 
From the sensitivity analysis results it was observed that lower PV and Heat pump 
capacities and intermediate Solar Multiples give the optimal LCOE and vice versa. Both 
very high and very low Solar Multiples appeared to be sub-optimal, since in the first case 
the load curtailment was significant, while in the second case the thermal capacity of the 
tanks was not fully utilized, rendering their use uneconomical. The combination of 
particularly high heat pump capacities with low PV sizes also resulted in sub-optimal 
LCOE. At smaller PV capacities, the Heat pump size does not affect the attained capacity 
factor, since the available electricity for charging is limited. Moreover, the LCOE is 
generally decreased with the capacity of the heat pump for (almost) all Solar Multiples, 
which suggests that the reduction in total cost (provoked by the reduction in the size of 
the heat pump) is more important than the corresponding reduction in the thermal 
(charged to the TES) and electrical energy output of the plant. The lowest LCOE was 
attained for a heat pump capacity of 10 MW, a PV capacity of 75 MW and a SM of 2. The 
fact that the optimal capacities almost coincide with the smallest ones (among those 
tested) suggests that the total cost of the system is so high that its reduction (caused by 
the reduction in component sizes) influences the obtained LCOE to a great extent. So much 
so that the decrease in the electrical output of the system, which is observed at low 
component capacities, cannot counterbalance its effect.    
4.2 Future Work 
Based on the work conducted in the present MSc thesis, some interesting 
recommendations for the future could be the following: 
For System 2: 
• 
To investigate whether an even higher capacity factor can be achieved by 
incorporating wind energy apart from Solar PV in the charging process. 
• 
To create a detailed model for heat losses in the tanks and include the temperature 
variations in the energy balances. By doing that, the validity of the 2% penalty 
assumption that was adopted in this thesis could be checked. 
• 
To add the Full Load Hours of Storage of the tanks, H in the optimization 
parameters. The ideal size needs to ensure that thermal power from the Solar 
Field and the Heat pump can be stored at all times, and simultaneously that the 
whole tank thermal capacity is utilized. Further, it would be nice to formulate a 
proper Objective Function for LCOE minimization and run a multi-parametric 
optimization algorithm in a suitable software such as MATLAB or GAMS. 
• 
To Create detailed thermodynamic models for the Rankine power cycle and the 
Solar Field and investigate the effect that the inevitable variations in temperature 
in these components, which occur in real systems, have on the performance of the 
System (together with the heat loss tank models). 
• 
To investigate whether it is viable and cost-competitive to co-generate electricity 
and heat from the plant (e.g., by means of using a backpressure turbine). The heat 
could be used for district heating in winter months, or for water desalination. 
• 
To account for thermal inertia phenomena and other relevant transients (e.g., 
start-up times in turbomachines) in the Off-design models. 
For System 1: 
• 
To examine the obtained ηRT trendline for s-CO2 cycles (supercritical cycles) in the 
PTES (only) system.

6 Appendix 
6.1 System 1 
6.1.1 Discharging cycle EES model 
EES Code (The cycle with Argon as working fluid is given here): 
"Thermodynamic Brayton cycle - discharge" 
  
"-----------------------------------------Inputs---------------------
--------------------------" 
  
  
T_amb = 25 {Assume 25 C for a typical day - dependent on the location 
of the plant} 
p_amb = 1 {1 bar = atmospheric pressure} 
  
P_rat = P_H/P_L {P1/P2--> turbine inlet/turbine outlet, BUT due to 
pressure losses, the highest pressure is in point 6 and the lowest in 
point 5 (after and before the compressor)} 
{P_rat = 3,5} 
P_rat_c = P_H_c/P_L_c 
P_H_c = p[6] 
p_L_c = p[5] 
  
Trat = abs((T_H_K)/(T_L_K)) {T1/T5 --> turbine inlet/compressor 
inlet, SOS!T_rat is in Kelvin} 
  
T_H_K = converttemp('C'; 'K'; T_H) 
T_L_K = converttemp('C'; 'K'; T_L) 
  
"-----Component inefficiencies-----" 
  
{1} 
  
"-------------Polytropic efficiencies------------" 
"Compressor" 
eta_sp_c = 0,9 "Polytropic" 
"Polytropic to isentropic" 
eta_s_c = (((P_rat_c)^((gamma-1)/gamma))-1)/((P_rat_c)^(((gamma-
1)/gamma)*(1/eta_sp_c))-1) 
"Define gamma for the specific working fluid, gamma = Cp/Cv" 
"Turbine" 
eta_sp_t = 0,9 "Polytropic" 
==PAGE:94==
90 
 
"Polytropic to isentropic" 
eta_s_t = (1 - ((1/P_rat)^(((gamma-1)/gamma)*eta_sp_t)))/ (1 - 
((1/P_rat)^(((gamma-1)/gamma)))) 
  
  
{2} 
  
"Heat exchanger pressure loss" 
"Defined separately for each heat exchanger --> use 1% as starting 
point" 
  
prloss_precooler = 0,01 
prloss_intercooler = 0,01 
{prloss_preheater = 0.01} 
prloss_regenerator = 0,01 
prloss_main = 0,01 
  
{3} 
"Turbine & Compressor are ssumed to be adiabatic --> No heat loss" 
  
{4} 
"Effectiveness is defined for the recuperator regenerator" 
"Pinch Point DT & Outlet Air temperatures are defined for the air 
cooler + heater" 
  
"-------------------------------------------------State Point 
Calculations---------------------------------------------------------
-" 
  
"Working fluid" 
"Air/N2/Argon-->start with Argon"  
wf$ = 'Ar' 
cp_wf = cp(wf$;T=T[1]) {The Cp of Argon as an ideal gas is 
practically independent of temperature} 
Cv_wf = cv(wf$;T=T[1]) {same} 
gamma = Cp_wf/Cv_wf 
  
"Coolant in precooler/Heating medium in preheater" 
"Air -->Assumption" 
prec$ = 'Air_ha' 
preh$ = 'Air_ha' 
"Air is also an ideal gas here" 
==PAGE:95==
91 
 
  
  
"!State 1" 
"Gas Turbine Inlet"  
  
T[1] = 540 "Assumption" 
T[1] = T_H 
p[1] = 100 "Assumption" 
p[1]=p[7]-prloss_main*p[7] "p[8] is fixed from here" 
p[1]=p_H 
s[1] = entropy(wf$;h=h[1];P=p[1]) 
h[1] = enthalpy(wf$;T=T[1]) 
  
  
"!State 2" 
"Turbine outlet - Regenerator inlet" 
p[2]=p_L 
s2_s = s[1] 
h2_s = enthalpy(wf$;P=p[2];s=s2_s) 
T2_s = temperature(wf$;s=s2_s;P=P[2]) 
   
s[2] = entropy(wf$;h=h[2];P=p[2]) 
T[2] = temperature(wf$;h=h[2]) 
  
{Define eta_s_t & fq_t - turbine relations} 
h[1] - h[2] =  w_t 
w_t_ideal = h[1]-h2_s 
eta_s_t = w_t/w_t_ideal 
  
  
"Regenerator/Recuperator effectiveness" 
  
{Relation between h[7] and h[8]} 
h[7] - h[6] = h[2] - h[3]  {also equal to q_regenerator - energy 
balance} 
{T[7] - T[6] = T[2] - T[3]}  {because cp is the same and constant for 
an ideal gas and mass flow is the same} 
  
{e = 0,93} {effectiveness - assumption} 
e = (h[7]-h[6])/(h[2]-h[6]) {relation between h[6] and h[7]} 
  
==PAGE:96==
92 
 
"!State 3" 
"Regenerator Outlet - Precooler/Air-cooler inlet" 
p[3]=p[2]-prloss_regenerator*p[2] 
  
{precooler} 
  
m_dot*(h[3] - h[4]) = m_cold_air*(h_cold_air_out - h_cold_air_in) 
{also equal to Q_total_precooler - energy balance} 
  
T_cold_air_in = T_amb 
h_cold_air_in = enthalpy(prec$;T=T_cold_air_in;P=P_cold_air_in) 
P_cold_air_in = P_amb 
  
  
P_cold_air_out = P_cold_air_in  
h_cold_air_out =enthalpy(prec$;T=T_cold_air_out;P=P_cold_air_out) 
{!either define Temperature of air out or T[5] as the lowest 
temperature of the cycle} 
  
DT_pinch_prec = T[4] - T_cold_air_in 
   
h[3] = enthalpy(wf$;T=T[3]) 
  
s[3] = entropy(wf$;h=h[3];P=P[3]) 
  
"!State 4" 
"Precooler/Air-cooler outlet - Intercooler inlet" 
p[4]=p[3]-prloss_precooler*p[3] 
  
h[4] = enthalpy(wf$;T=T[4]) 
  
s[4] = entropy(wf$;h=h[4];P=P[4]) 
  
"!State 5" 
"Intercooler outlet - compressor inlet" 
p[5]=p[4]-prloss_intercooler*p[4] 
  
s[5] = entropy(wf$;T=T[5];P=P[5]) 
  
T[5] = T_L 
{T[5] = -30} {could be left as an optimization variable} 
==PAGE:97==
93 
 
  
h[5] = enthalpy(wf$;T=T[5]) 
  
  
"!State 6" 
"compressor outlet - regenerator inlet" 
p[6]=p_H/((1-prloss_regenerator)*(1-prloss_main)) 
  
s6_s = s[5] 
h6_s = enthalpy(wf$;P=p[6];s=s6_s) 
T6_s = temperature(wf$;s=s6_s;P=P[6]) 
  
s[6] = entropy(wf$;h=h[6];P=p[6]) 
{h[6] = enthalpy(wf$,T=T[6])} 
T[6] = temperature(wf$;h=h[6]) 
  
{Define eta_s_c & fq_c - compressor relations} 
w_c = h[6] - h[5] 
w_c_ideal = h6_s - h[5] 
eta_s_c = w_c_ideal/w_c 
  
"Do not use an air preheater here (as the outlet of the compressor is 
at high T) + Assume T[2] = T[7] + 5" 
  
"!State 7" 
"regenerator outlet - main CSP hex inlet"   
  
T[7] = temperature(wf$;h=h[7]) 
  
s[7] = entropy(wf$;T=T[7];P=P[7]) 
   
  
"-------------------------------efficiency-eta calculation power 
cycle-----------------------------------" 
  
q_in = q_hot 
q_out = q_prec + q_cold {Q_dot_cold is the load delivered to the cold 
storage tanks} 
eta = (w_t - w_c)/q_in 
bwr = w_c/w_t {back work ratio} 
q_in = (w_t - w_c) + q_out 
==PAGE:98==
94 
 
  
q_hot = h[1] - h[7]  
q_prec = h[3] - h[4] 
  
W_net = (W_turb - W_comp) "in kW" 
W_el_net = eta_gener*W_net "in kW" 
eta_gener = 0,98 {Generator efficiency - Assumption} 
W_turb = m_dot*w_t 
W_comp = m_dot*w_c 
W_el_net = 50*1000 {Plant Design specification, in kW} 
  
{Q_CSP = 80*1000} {insert this parameter to the CSP Design - SAM or 
MATLAB, in kW}  
Q_tot_hot = m_dot*q_hot 
Q_tot_in = m_dot*q_in 
Q_tot_out = m_dot*q_out 
Q_tot_cold = m_dot*q_cold 
Q_tot_prec = m_dot*q_prec 
  
  
"----------------------------Energy balance in storages satisfaction 
- discharging/charging cycle----------" 
{Without CSP - Only PTES} 
  
enth_dis_rat = (h[4] - h[5])/(h[1] - h[7]) "discharging cycle"

6.1.2 Charging cycle EES model 
EES Code (The cycle with Argon as working fluid and x=0 is given here): 
"Thermodynamic Brayton cycle - charge" 
  
"-----------------------------------------Inputs---------------------
--------------------------" 
  
  
T_amb = 25 {Assume 25 C for a typical day - dependent on the location 
of the plant} 
p_amb = 1 {1 bar = atmospheric pressure} 
  
  
enth_dis_rat = 0,2949 "discharging cycle" 
enth_charg_rat = (h[6] - h[5])/(h[1] - h[2])  "charging cycle" 
enth_dis_rat*(1+x) = enth_charg_rat "x>0 is the extra load from the 
CSP and has to be optimized" 
x=0,0 
  
"-----------------------Thermodynamic Brayton cycle - charge---------
------------------------------------" 
  
"-------------------------------Inputs-------------------------------
---------------------------" 
  
"Heat exchanger pressure loss" 
"Defined separately for each heat exchanger --> use 1% as starting 
point" 
  
prloss_precooler = 0,01 
prloss_intercooler = 0,01 
{prloss_preheater = 0.01} 
prloss_regenerator = 0,01 
prloss_main = 0,01 
  
{3} 
"Turbine & Compressor are ssumed to be adiabatic --> No heat loss" 
  
{4} 
"Effectiveness is defined for the recuperator regenerator" 
"Pinch Point DT & Outlet Air temperatures are defined for the air 
cooler + heater" 
==PAGE:100==
96 
 
  
"-------------------------------------------------State Point 
Calculations---------------------------------------------------------
-" 
  
"Working fluid" 
"Air/N2/Argon-->start with Argon"  
wf$ = 'Ar' 
cp_wf = cp(wf$;T=T[1]) {The Cp of Argon as an ideal gas is 
practically independent of temperature} 
Cv_wf = cv(wf$;T=T[1]) {same} 
gamma = Cp_wf/Cv_wf 
  
"Coolant in precooler/Heating medium in preheater" 
"Air -->Assumption" 
prec$ = 'Air_ha' 
preh$ = 'Air_ha' 
"Air is also an ideal gas here" 
  
P_rat_charg = P_H_charg/P_L_charg "defined in the compressor" 
P_rat_t_charg = P_H_t_charg/P_L_t_charg "defined in the turbine" 
  
"!define P_H_charg and P_L_charg in the cycle state points" 
  
{ 
Trat_charg = abs((T_H_K_charg)/(T_L_K_charg)) {SOS!T_rat is in 
Kelvin} 
  
T_H_K_charg = converttemp('C'; 'K'; T_H_charg) "!define T_H_charg and 
T_L_charg in the cycle state points" 
T_L_K_charg = converttemp('C'; 'K'; T_L_charg) 
} 
  
{P_rat_charg = 3,5} "Optimize if possible" 
P_H_t_charg = p[4] "turbine inlet" 
p_L_t_charg = p[5] "turbine outlet" 
  
"Turbomachines set 2 - define polytropic efficiencies" 
  
"Compressor" 
{eta_sp_c_charg = 0,9} "Polytropic" 
"Polytropic to isentropic" 
==PAGE:101==
97 
 
eta_s_c_charg = (((P_rat_charg)^((gamma_charg-1)/gamma_charg))-
1)/((P_rat_charg)^(((gamma_charg-1)/gamma_charg)*(1/eta_sp_c_charg))-
1) 
"Define gamma for the specific working fluid and temperature, gamma = 
Cp/Cv" 
  
"Turbine" 
eta_sp_t_charg = 0,9 "Polytropic" 
"Polytropic to isentropic" 
{eta_s_t_charg = (1 - ((1/P_rat_t_charg)^(((gamma_charg-
1)/gamma_charg)*eta_sp_t_charg)))/ (1 - 
((1/P_rat_t_charg)^(((gamma_charg-1)/gamma_charg))))}  
  
term_1 = (1/P_rat_t_charg) 
term_2 = ((gamma_charg-1)/gamma_charg) 
term_3 = term_2*eta_sp_t_charg 
eta_s_t_charg = (1-(term_1^term_3))/(1-(term_1^term_2)) 
  
cp_wf_charg = cp(wf$;T=T[1]) {The Cp of Argon as an ideal gas is 
practically independent of temperature} 
Cv_wf_charg = cv(wf$;T=T[1]) {same} 
gamma_charg = Cp_wf_charg/Cv_wf_charg "gamma defined for the charging 
cycle" 
  
{!Assume same pressure drop in the heat exchangers? - Keep them the 
same for now} 
   
"!State 1" 
"Compressor outlet - Main/CSP heat exchanger inlet" 
  
p[1] = 100 {same high pressure} 
  
p[1]=p_H_charg 
  
s1_s = s[7]  
h1_s = enthalpy(wf$;P=p[1];s=s1_s)  
T1_s = temperature(wf$;s=s1_s;P=P[1])  
  
  
T_discharge = 540 [C] "from discharge cycle" 
{T[] = temperature(wf$;h=h[1])} 
T[1] = T_discharge + 2*DT_pinch_CSP {*2 to account for the hot HTF in 
between the discharging and charging lines according to Simone's 
sketch} 
==PAGE:102==
98 
 
{DT_pinch_CSP = 10} "Assumption --> !to be optimized from 5 to 35 C" 
  
h[1] = enthalpy(wf$;T=T[1]) 
{T[1] = T_H_charg} 
  
s[1] = entropy(wf$;h=h[1];P=p[1]) 
  
{Define eta_s_c_charg - compressor relations} 
h[1] - h[7] =  w_c_charg 
w_c_ideal_charg = h1_s - h[7]  
eta_s_c_charg = w_c_ideal_charg/w_c_charg  
  
"!State 2" 
"Main/CSP heat exchanger Outlet - Regenerator inlet" 
  
p[2]=p[1]-prloss_main*p[1] 
  
{T_7_discharge = 284,6} 
{T[2] = temperature(wf$;h=h[2])} 
h[2] = enthalpy(wf$;T=T[2]) 
{T[2] = T_7_discharge + 2*DT_pinch_CSP} 
{DT_pinch_CSP = 10} 
  
s[2] = entropy(wf$;h=h[2];P=P[2]) 
  
"!State 3" 
"Regenerator outlet - Precooler/Air-cooler inlet" 
  
p[3]=p[2]-prloss_regenerator*p[2]  
  
e_charg = (h[7]-h[6])/(h[2]-h[6]) 
{e_charg = 0,95} {!optimize} 
  
h[2] - h[3] = h[7] - h[6] 
  
T[3] = temperature(wf$;h=h[3]) 
  
{h[3] = enthalpy(wf$;T=T[3])} 
  
s[3] = entropy(wf$;h=h[3];P=P[3]) 
==PAGE:103==
99 
 
  
"!State 4" 
"Precooler/Air-cooler outlet - turbine inlet" 
  
p[4]=p[3]-prloss_precooler*p[3] 
{p[4] =  P_H_t_charg} 
{m_dot_charg*(h[10] - h[11]) = m_cold_air_charg*(h_cold_air_out_charg 
- h_cold_air_in_charg)} {Energy balance - equal to Q_tot_prec_charg} 
  
{or define the ratio m_dot_charg/m_cold_air_charg = y and optimize it 
to achieve the highest round-trip efficiency} 
(h_cold_air_out_charg - h_cold_air_in_charg)/(h[3] - h[4]) = y 
  
T_cold_air_in_charg = T_amb  
h_cold_air_in_charg = 
enthalpy(prec$;T=T_cold_air_in_charg;P=P_cold_air_in_charg) 
P_cold_air_in_charg = P_amb 
  
{T_cold_air_out_charg = 75} "!Assumption - to be optimized" 
  
P_cold_air_out_charg = P_cold_air_in_charg  
h_cold_air_out_charg 
=enthalpy(prec$;T=T_cold_air_out_charg;P=P_cold_air_out_charg) 
  
DT_pinch_prec_charg = T[4] - T_cold_air_in_charg {most likely the 
pinch is on this side of the heat exchanger} 
{DT_pinch_prec_charg = 10} {!Optimize this} 
  
h[4] = enthalpy(wf$;T=T[4]) 
  
s[4] = entropy(wf$;h=h[4];P=P[4]) 
  
"!State 5" 
"turbine outlet - intercooler/cold heat exchanger inlet" 
  
p[5] = p_L_charg/((1-prloss_intercooler)*(1-prloss_regenerator)) 
{p[5] =  P_L_t_charg} 
  
s5_s = s[4] 
h5_s = enthalpy(wf$;P=p[5];s=s5_s) 
T5_s = temperature(wf$;s=s5_s;P=P[5]) 
    
==PAGE:104==
100 
 
{T[5] = temperature(wf$;h=h[5])} 
{T[5] = T_L_charg} {!Optimize T[5]} 
{T[5] = - 49,85 - 2*DT} 
{DT = 5} 
  
{Define eta_s_t_charg - turbine relations} 
w_t_charg = h[4] - h[5] 
w_t_ideal_charg = h[4] - h5_s 
eta_s_t_charg = w_t_charg/w_t_ideal_charg 
  
T[5] = temperature(wf$;h=h[5]) 
  
s[5] = entropy(wf$;T=T[5];P=p[5]) 
  
  
"!State 6" 
"intercooler/cold heat exchanger outlet - regenerator inlet"  
  
"P[6] is defined in P[7]" 
  
T[6] = temperature(wf$;h=h[6]) 
{h[6] = enthalpy(wf$;T=T[6])} 
  
s[6] = entropy(wf$;T=T[6];P=P[6]) 
  
"!State 7" 
"regenerator outlet - compressor inlet" 
  
p[7] = p[6]-prloss_regenerator*p[6] "p[6] is fixed from here" 
p[7] = p_L_charg 
  
s[7] = entropy(wf$;T=T[7];P=p[7]) 
h[7] = enthalpy(wf$;T=T[7]) 
  
{T[7] = 290} 
  
  
"-------------------------------efficiency-COP calculation heat pump 
cycle-----------------------------------" 
  
==PAGE:105==
101 
 
q_in_charg = q_cold_charg  
q_out_charg = q_prec_charg + q_CSP_charg  
  
  
COP = q_CSP_charg/(w_c_charg - w_t_charg) 
COP_el_to_thermal = COP*eta_motor 
bwr = w_c_charg/w_t_charg {back work ratio} 
eta_motor = 0,98 
q_CSP_charg = h[1] - h[2]  
q_prec_charg = h[3] - h[4] 
q_cold_charg = h[6] - h[5] 
  
eta = 0,4202 "from discharging cycle" 
  
eta_RT = COP*eta {!To be maximized} 
  
{W_el_net = 50*1000} "50 MW is a spcecification, equal to the 
discharing cycle load" 
W_net = eta_motor*W_el_net 
W_net = (W_comp - W_turb) 
W_turb = m_dot*w_t_charg 
W_comp = m_dot*w_c_charg 
  
Q_tot_cold_charg*t_c = Q_tot_cold_discharg*t_d "Energy balance cold 
storage - in joules" 
Q_tot_cold_discharg = 35802 
t_d = hours_day - t_c 
hours_day = 24*3600 [s] 
t_d/t_c = omega "Optimize omega" 
  
Q_tot_CSP_charg = m_dot*q_CSP_charg 
Q_tot_in_charg = m_dot*q_in_charg 
Q_tot_out_charg = m_dot*q_out_charg 
Q_tot_cold_charg = m_dot*q_cold_charg 
Q_tot_prec_charg = m_dot*q_prec_charg 
  
Q_solar_field = x*m_dot*q_CSP_charg

6.2 System 2 
6.2.1 Heat pump – Design EES model 
EES Code:  
"Thermodynamic reverse Brayton cycle - charge - Interheating" 
  
"-----------------------------------------Inputs---------------------
--------------------------" 
  
  
T_amb = 25 {Assume 25 C for a typical day - dependent on the location 
of the plant} 
p_amb = 1 {1 bar = atmospheric pressure} 
   
"-----------------------Thermodynamic Brayton cycle - charge---------
------------------------------------" 
  
"-------------------------------Inputs-------------------------------
---------------------------" 
  
"Heat exchanger pressure loss" 
==PAGE:107==
103 
 
"Defined separately for each heat exchanger --> use 1% as starting 
point" 
  
prloss_precooler = 0,01 
prloss_interheater1 = 0,01 
prloss_interheater2 = 0,01 
prloss_regenerator = 0,01 
prloss_main = 0,01 
  
{3} 
"Turbine & Compressor are assumed to be adiabatic --> No heat loss" 
  
{4} 
"Effectiveness is defined for the recuperator regenerator" 
"Pinch Point DT & Outlet Air temperatures are defined for the air 
cooler + heater" 
  
"-------------------------------------------------State Point 
Calculations---------------------------------------------------------
-" 
  
"Working fluid" 
"Air/N2/Argon-->start with Argon"  
wf$ = 'N2' 
cp_wf = cp(wf$;T=T[1]) {The Cp of Argon as an ideal gas is 
practically independent of temperature} 
Cv_wf = cv(wf$;T=T[1]) {same} 
gamma = Cp_wf/Cv_wf 
  
"Coolant in precooler/Heating medium in preheater" 
"Air -->Assumption" 
prec$ = 'Steam_IAPWS' 
preh$ = 'Steam_IAPWS' 
"Use hot cooling water from the condenser to preheat the working 
fluid" 
  
P_rat_charg = P_H_charg/P_L_charg "defined in the compressor" 
{P_rat_charg_2 = P_M_charg/P_L_charg "defined in the low pressure 
turbine - T2"} 
P_rat_t_1_charg = P_H_t_charg/P_M_t_charg "defined in the High 
Pressure turbine - T1" 
P_rat_t_2_charg = P_M_t_charg/P_L_t_charg "defined in the Low 
Pressure turbine - T2" 
==PAGE:108==
104 
 
{P_rat_charg = 3,597} "!Optimal value used as input here"  
  
"!define P_H_charg and P_L_charg in the cycle state points" 
  
{ 
Trat_charg = abs((T_H_K_charg)/(T_L_K_charg)) {SOS!T_rat is in 
Kelvin} 
  
T_H_K_charg = converttemp('C'; 'K'; T_H_charg) "!define T_H_charg and 
T_L_charg in the cycle state points" 
T_L_K_charg = converttemp('C'; 'K'; T_L_charg) 
} 
  
{P_rat_charg = 3,5} "Optimize if possible" 
P_H_t_charg = p[3] "turbine 1 inlet" 
P_M_t_charg = p[4] "turbine 1 outlet" "Pressure at turbine 1 outlet 
is the same as in the turbine 2 inlet-if we do not account for the 
pressure drop in the hex" 
p_L_t_charg = p[6] "turbine 2 outlet" 
  
"Turbomachines set 2 - define polytropic efficiencies" 
  
"Compressor" 
eta_sp_c_charg = 0,9 "Polytropic" 
"Polytropic to isentropic" 
eta_s_c_charg = (((P_rat_charg)^((gamma_charg-1)/gamma_charg))-
1)/((P_rat_charg)^(((gamma_charg-1)/gamma_charg)*(1/eta_sp_c_charg))-
1) 
"Define gamma for the specific working fluid and temperature, gamma = 
Cp/Cv" 
  
"Turbine 1 - High Pressure Turbine - T1" 
eta_sp_t_charg_1 = 0,9 "Polytropic" 
"Polytropic to isentropic"  
term_1 = (1/P_rat_t_1_charg) 
term_2 = ((gamma_charg-1)/gamma_charg) 
term_3 = term_2*eta_sp_t_charg_1 
eta_s_t_charg_1 = (1-(term_1^term_3))/(1-(term_1^term_2)) 
  
"Turbine 2 - Low Pressure Turbine - T2" 
eta_sp_t_charg_2 = 0,9 "Polytropic" 
"Polytropic to isentropic"  
==PAGE:109==
105 
 
term_4 = (1/P_rat_t_2_charg) 
term_5 = ((gamma_charg-1)/gamma_charg) 
term_6 = term_2*eta_sp_t_charg_2 
eta_s_t_charg_2 = (1-(term_4^term_6))/(1-(term_4^term_5)) 
  
cp_wf_charg = cp(wf$;T=T[1]) {The Cp of Argon as an ideal gas is 
practically independent of temperature} 
Cv_wf_charg = cv(wf$;T=T[1]) {same} 
gamma_charg = Cp_wf_charg/Cv_wf_charg "gamma defined for the charging 
cycle" 
  
{!Assume same pressure drop in the heat exchangers? - Keep them the 
same for now} 
   
"!State 1" 
"Compressor outlet - Main/CSP heat exchanger inlet" 
  
p[1] = 100 {same high pressure} 
  
p[1]=p_H_charg 
  
s1_s = s[8]  
h1_s = enthalpy(wf$;P=p[1];s=s1_s)  
T1_s = temperature(wf$;s=s1_s;P=P[1])  
  
{!Should I define T[8](and p[8]) or T[14] (and p[14])?}  
   
{T[1] = 565} 
  
h[1] = enthalpy(wf$;T=T[1]) 
{T[1] = T_H_charg} 
  
s[1] = entropy(wf$;h=h[1];P=p[1]) 
  
{Define eta_s_c_charg - compressor relations} 
h[1] - h[8] =  w_c_charg 
w_c_ideal_charg = h1_s - h[8]  
eta_s_c_charg = w_c_ideal_charg/w_c_charg  
  
"!State 2" 
"Main/CSP heat exchanger Outlet - Regenerator inlet" 
==PAGE:110==
106 
 
  
p[2]=p[1]-prloss_main*p[1] 
  
{T_7_discharge = 284,6}

h[2] = enthalpy(wf$;T=T[2]) 
{T[2] = T_7_discharge + 2*DT_pinch_CSP} 
{DT_pinch_CSP = 10} 
  
s[2] = entropy(wf$;h=h[2];P=P[2]) 
  
"!State 3 - Merged with point 4 since there is no precooler in this 
case"  
"Regenerator outlet - Precooler/Air-cooler inlet" 
  
p[3]=p[2]-prloss_regenerator*p[2]  
  
e_charg = (h[8]-h[7])/(h[2]-h[7]) "To calculate T[2]" 
{e_charg = 0,95} {!optimize} 
  
h[2] - h[3] = h[8] - h[7] "to calculate T[3]" 
  
T[3] = temperature(wf$;h=h[3]) 
  
{h[3] = enthalpy(wf$;T=T[3])} 
  
s[3] = entropy(wf$;h=h[3];P=P[3]) 
   
"!State 4" 
"turbine 1 outlet - interheater 1 heat exchanger inlet" 
  
{p[4] = p_L_charg/((1-prloss_intercooler)*(1-prloss_regenerator))} 
{p[4] =  P_L_t_charg} 
"!Either set p[4] = p_intermediate as an optimization variable or 
define p[4]=(p_H+p_L)/2" 
  
s4_s = s[3]

T4_s = temperature(wf$;s=s4_s;P=P[4]) 
    
{T[5] = temperature(wf$;h=h[5])} 
==PAGE:111==

{T[5] = T_L_charg} {!Optimize T[5]}

{DT = 5}

w_t_charg_1 = h[3] - h[4] 
w_t_ideal_charg_1 = h[3] - h4_s 
eta_s_t_charg_1 = w_t_charg_1/w_t_ideal_charg_1 
  
T[4] = temperature(wf$;h=h[4])

"Heat exchangers" 
DT_lm_preh_1 = ((T_water_out_charg_1 - T[4]) - (T_water_in_charg_1 - 
T[5]))/ln((T_water_out_charg_1 - T[4])/(T_water_in_charg_1 - T[5]))

T[7]))/ln((T_water_out_charg_2 - T[6])/(T_water_in_charg_2 - T[7]))

T[7]))

T_salt_cold + T[2])/(-T_salt_hot + T[1]))

6.2.2.2 Model 2 – Parametric off-design study 
EES Code: 
"Thermodynamic Brayton cycle - charge - Interheating" 
  
"-----------------------------------------Inputs---------------------
--------------------------" 
  
  
T_amb = 25 {Assume 25 C for a typical day - dependent on the location 
of the plant} 
p_amb = 1 {1 bar = atmospheric pressure} 
   
"-----------------------Thermodynamic Brayton cycle - charge---------
------------------------------------" 
  
"-------------------------------Inputs-------------------------------
---------------------------" 
  
"Heat exchanger pressure loss" 
==PAGE:125==
121 
 
"Defined separately for each heat exchanger --> use 1% as starting 
point" 
  
prloss_precooler = 0,01 
prloss_interheater1 = 0,01 
prloss_interheater2 = 0,01 
prloss_regenerator = 0,01 
prloss_main = 0,01 
  
{3} 
"Turbine & Compressor are ssumed to be adiabatic --> No heat loss" 
  
{4} 
"Effectiveness is defined for the recuperator regenerator" 
"Pinch Point DT & Outlet Air temperatures are defined for the air 
cooler + heater" 
  
"-------------------------------------------------State Point 
Calculations---------------------------------------------------------
-" 
  
"Working fluid" 
"Air/N2/Argon-->start with Argon"  
wf$ = 'N2' 
cp_wf = cp(wf$;T=T[1]) {The Cp of Argon as an ideal gas is 
practically independent of temperature} 
Cv_wf = cv(wf$;T=T[1]) {same} 
gamma = Cp_wf/Cv_wf 
  
"Coolant in precooler/Heating medium in preheater" 
"Air -->Assumption" 
prec$ = 'Steam_IAPWS' 
preh$ = 'Steam_IAPWS' 
"Use hot cooling water from the condenser to preheat the working 
fluid" 
  
P_rat_charg = P_H_charg/P_L_charg "defined in the compressor" 
  
{P_rat_charg = 3,602} "!Use it to find turbine/compressor constants 
and design UA values of hexs" 
  
{P_rat_charg_2 = P_M_charg/P_L_charg "defined in the low pressure 
turbine - T2"} 
==PAGE:126==
122 
 
P_rat_t_1_charg = P_H_t_charg/P_M_t_charg "defined in the High 
Pressure turbine - T1" 
P_rat_t_2_charg = P_M_t_charg/P_L_t_charg "defined in the Low 
Pressure turbine - T2" 
  
  
"!define P_H_charg and P_L_charg in the cycle state points" 
  
{ 
Trat_charg = abs((T_H_K_charg)/(T_L_K_charg)) {SOS!T_rat is in 
Kelvin} 
  
T_H_K_charg = converttemp('C'; 'K'; T_H_charg) "!define T_H_charg and 
T_L_charg in the cycle state points" 
T_L_K_charg = converttemp('C'; 'K'; T_L_charg) 
} 
  
{P_rat_charg = 3,5} "Optimize if possible" 
P_H_t_charg = p[3] "turbine 1 inlet" 
P_M_t_charg = p[4] "turbine 1 outlet" "Pressure at turbine 1 outlet 
is the same as in the turbine 2 inlet-if we do not account for the 
pressure drop in the hex" 
p_L_t_charg = p[6] "turbine 2 outlet" 
  
"Turbomachines set 2 - define polytropic efficiencies" 
  
"Compressor" 
eta_sp_c_charg = 0,9 "Polytropic" 
"Polytropic to isentropic" 
eta_s_c_charg = (((P_rat_charg)^((gamma_charg-1)/gamma_charg))-
1)/((P_rat_charg)^(((gamma_charg-1)/gamma_charg)*(1/eta_sp_c_charg))-
1) 
"Define gamma for the specific working fluid and temperature, gamma = 
Cp/Cv" 
  
"Turbine 1 - High Pressure Turbine - T1" 
eta_sp_t_charg_1 = 0,9 "Polytropic" 
"Polytropic to isentropic"  
term_1 = (1/P_rat_t_1_charg) 
term_2 = ((gamma_charg-1)/gamma_charg) 
term_3 = term_2*eta_sp_t_charg_1 
eta_s_t_charg_1 = (1-(term_1^term_3))/(1-(term_1^term_2)) 
  
==PAGE:127==
123 
 
"Turbine 2 - Low Pressure Turbine - T2" 
eta_sp_t_charg_2 = 0,9 "Polytropic" 
"Polytropic to isentropic"  
term_4 = (1/P_rat_t_2_charg) 
term_5 = ((gamma_charg-1)/gamma_charg) 
term_6 = term_2*eta_sp_t_charg_2 
eta_s_t_charg_2 = (1-(term_4^term_6))/(1-(term_4^term_5)) 
  
cp_wf_charg = cp(wf$;T=T[1]) {The Cp of Argon as an ideal gas is 
practically independent of temperature} 
Cv_wf_charg = cv(wf$;T=T[1]) {same}

cycle"

In the following Table, several different parameters and outputs from the SAM Solar Field

The Solar Multiple was the sole parameter that varied and 7 different Solar Field thermal 
profiles were obtained.  
The first four parameters have already been mentioned in the main text. The choice for 
the maximum Receiver flux was based on the work from Liao et al., 2014 and on the high 
HTF temperature of 550 oC. The Receiver Height and Diameter, the Tower Height and the 
number of Heliostats were optimized from SAM. An image of the optimized configuration 
of the Heliostat Field is shown below: 
==PAGE:137==
133 
 
 
The temperature setpoints were the thresholds below which the electric heater was 
activated. They were chosen to be 20 and 10 oC lower compared to the storage 
temperatures to account for heat losses. The electric heater was assumed to be 
sufficiently large in capacity to be able to raise the tank temperatures back to their initial 
levels. The operating principle of the electric heater was assumed to be similar to that of 
a motor, and therefore a similar efficiency was adopted.   
The Boiler operating pressure was chosen to be approximately 123 bar, as the saturation 
temperature of steam in this pressure is equal to 327.3 oC. Considering the fact that typical 
degrees of superheating for steam turbines often vary from 100-200 oC, and that the HTF 
in the Hot tank is available at 550 oC, having the boiler at 123 bar is a reasonable choice. 
As for the Condenser, it was decided to utilize wet-cooling in the Rankine cycle. The water- 
cooled condenser could then use the same water as the heat pump in its Cooling Tower. 
Then, the ambient temperature choice of 17.5 oC was based on weather data for the 
location. The 95% CDF criterion that was applied for picking an appropriate DNI design 
value was also used here for the design ambient temperature. Moreover, by adopting an 
Approach temperature of 5 oC, the water temperature at the condenser inlet is assumed 
to be equal to (17.5 + 5) or 22.5 oC. Further, if a Reference Condenser water dT of 12.5 oC 
is taken for the water in the condenser, the water temperature at its outlet turns out to be 
equal to (22.5+12.5) or 35 oC, which matches exactly the temperature of the auxiliary 
water in the heat pump interheaters (in the heat pump of System 2). The aforementioned 
parameters are not unheard of for real Cooling Towers.  
Lastly, the operating range of 15-100% of design load, which was used for the heat pump 
and for the Solar Field was also adopted for the Rankine power cycle in SAM. Over-
production was not allowed.

6.2.6 Description of all possible operating modes and actions that appear 
in Figure 2-9 
Components – subsystems activation. Explanations & Control 
1) Heat pump is not activated in this case. All PV electricity produced goes directly into 
the grid. The plant operates like a classic CSP – two tank TES system and the power block 
has to be activated to avoid an electricity shortage. 
2) Heat pump is potentially activated in this case. All electricity comes from PV, thus 
power block is not active. TES charging options are examined. 
3) Heat pump needs at least 15% of design load in order to operate the machines that 
constitute it. Pmax is the design load (maximum load of the heat pump). TES charging from 
solar field is examined then. 
4) Heat pump is potentially active in this case. Tank levels have to be examined too. 
5) If the mass flow in the Solar field is not enough (15% of maximum), then both solar 
field and heat pump are deactivated (offline) and thus no charge of TES takes place here. 
6) Here the solar field can potentially be activated, emptying salt from the cold tank and 
transferring it to the hot tank (TES charging process). The levels in the Tanks need to be 
examined. 
7) If the hot tank is full (and cold tank is empty), no charging can occur. Therefore, the 
solar field is turned off (QSF = 0)    
8) If the hot tank is not full, TES is charged only from the solar field in this case. Qcharg = 
Q_s.f. 
9) In this case the heat pump can be activated, whereas the Solar field cannot. Tank levels 
are examined in order to proceed with TES charging from heat pump only. 
10) Similar to case 7 (above). The heat pump in turned off (Qhp = 0). Some electricity from 
the PV system is inevitably wasted in this case. 
11) Heat pump charges the TES system. The input to the heat pump is (Wel_net – 50) and 
the thermal load into the tanks is Qcharg = COP*(Wel_net – 50). Note that COP is thermal to 
electrical. 
12) In this case, TES may be charged both by the solar field and the heat pump. Tank levels 
are examined. 
13) If the Tank levels are not appropriate, no charging takes place. Solar field must be 
defocused and heat pump must be deactivated. 
14) Here, TES is charged both by the heat pump (Wel_net – 50) and the solar field (QSF). 
Thus Qcharg = COP*( Wel_net – 50) + QSF. 
15) In this case, the electrical load from PV is not sufficient to cover the fixed demand of 
50 MW, and the solar field is not active (most likely this profile occurs during night). TES 
discharge is examined. 
==PAGE:141==
137 
 
16) If the 4 conditions for TES discharge are not met, then everything in the system is 
shut off and the electricity comes from the PV (if applicable). Wout = Wel_net (<50 MW). An 
undesired scenario. 
17) If the 4 conditions are met, TES discharge happens and the input to the power cycle 
is Qhot = (50-Wel_net)/ηth,Rankine. 
18)  In this case the solar field is operational. The next step is to check whether its output 
is by itself sufficient to run the power cycle or if TES discharge is also required.   
19) In this case the solar field produces more power than needed to run the power cycle, 
and thus TES charging with the excess thermal load is examined.  
20) If the tanks cannot be charged, the solar field output must be limited (by means of 
defocusing) to the power cycle requirements, namely QSF = Qhot = (50 – Wel_net)/ηth,Rankine.  
21) If the tanks can be charged, then Qhot (input to the power block) is (50 – Wel_net)/ 
ηth,Rankine and the rest of the solar field output is charged in the TES system, Qcharg = QSF – 
Qhot 
22) Here it is checked if the solar field gives exactly the needed load to run the power 
cycle, namely (50- Wel_net)/ ηth,Rankine 
23) In the unlikely event that QSF = (50 – Wel_net)/ ηth,Rankine exactly, then no TES discharge 
is needed and the needed electricity is covered from the PV (partially – if applicable) and 
the power cycle (partially). 
24) If the output of the solar field is less than (50 – Wel_net)/ ηth,Rankine, then TES discharge 
has to be examined in order to cover the needed electrical load. 
25) In this case the Tanks cannot be discharged and therefore the baseload of 50 MW 
cannot be met by the contribution of solar field and the PV production alone. Qhot = QSF 
(part-load operation). Undesired case. 
26) In this case the PV system (electrical load), the Solar field (thermal load) and the 
stored energy in the TES (thermal load) all contribute to the power output of 50 MW. The 
Rankine block is powered both by the Solar Field and by the stored energy in the TES. Qhot 
= (50 - Wel_net)/ ηth,Rankine = QSF + Qdischarg. Qdischarg in [kW] denotes the discharged energy 
from the tanks.

#----------- Rankine and heat pump - eta_el_net and COP_el_to_th calculation -----------    
 
def COP_heat_pump(x): 
    return (-3e-17*x**3  + 8e-12*x**2 -9e-7*x+1.4107) # = COP_el_to_th  
    # This function accepts ONLY W_el_net as input 
 
def Q_out_hp(x): 
 
return COP_heat_pump(x)*W_el_net 
==PAGE:144==
140 
 
    # Gives the output of the heat pump = Q_out_hp 
 
Q_hp = Q_out_hp(W_el_net) # heat pump output 
 
def eta_rankine(x): 
    a = -2.52993997/2 #check if it gives 0.3708 at Qin = 134844 
    b = 0.3802111185 #check if it gives 0.3708 at Qin = 134844 
    return (a*1000/x+b) # = eta_th_to_el *1000 to have the Qhot in kW as it is MW in the original 
equation 
    # Do not forget to return the value of Wrankine = eta_th_to_el*Q_hot when function is called 
    # This function accepts ONLY Q_hot as input 
 
a = -2.52993997/2 # for the loop 
b = 0.3802111185 # for the loop 
    
def Wrankine(x): 
 
return eta_rankine(x)*Qhot 
 
# Gives the output of the Rankine cycle work

# ---------SOS___SOS check these functions again during the code 
 
def update(i): 
 
# QUESTION - SHOULD I ADD ONLY THE PARAMETERS THAT CHANGE EVERY TIME STEP 
IN THE LIST BELOW? 
 
# ANSWER : NO, ALL VARIABLES THAT APPEAR IN THE FOR LOOP MUST BE STATED AS 
GLOBAL VARIABLES 
    global  tol  # this remains constant 
    global  DT   # this remains constant 
    global  Q_SF # is an input 
    global  W_el_net # is an input 
    global  Pmax  # this remains constant 
    global  Q_SF_design # this remains constant 
    global  L_h_t_max # this remains constant 
    global  L_h_t_min # this remains constant 
    global  L_h_t # changes with time - dependent on the previous time step 
    global  L_c_t_min # this remains constant 
    global  L_c_t_max # this remains constant 
    global  L_c_t # changes with time - dependent on the previous time step 
    global  Thot # this remains constant 
    global  Tcold # this remains constant  
    global  Cp # this remains constant 
    global  Cp_hot # this remains constant 
    global  Cp_cold # this remains constant 
    global  mhot # changes with time - dependent on the previous time step 
    global mcold # changes with time - dependent on the previous time step

6.2.8 Heat pump CAPEX calculation – First approach 
The Compressor PEC, in [$] was found from the following relation from A. Agazzani et al 
(doi:10.1115/96-GT-479): 
𝑃𝐸𝐶𝑐𝑜𝑚𝑝= 1.051 ∗39.5𝑚̇ ∗𝑙𝑛𝛽
0,92 −𝜂
 
6.1 
Where 𝑚̇  is the mass flow rate of the working fluid in the cycle, in [kg/s], β is the pressure 
ratio, Prat in the heat pump cycle, and 𝜂 is the isentropic efficiency of the machine. By 
substituting a β of 3.602, an 𝜂 of 0.8823 and an 𝑚̇  of 477.5 kg/s (from the output of the 
design heat pump EES model of System 2) in equation 6.1, the obtained 𝑃𝐸𝐶𝑐𝑜𝑚𝑝 turns 
out to be equal to 2427115.88 $. If adjusted for inflation from 1995 to date, the final 
compressor cost is 5135091.93 $. 
For the turbines the cost relation for the PEC in [$] was also found in A. Agazzani et al 
(doi:10.1115/96-GT-479): 
𝑃𝐸𝐶𝑡𝑢𝑟𝑏= 1.051 ∗266𝑚̇ ∗𝑙𝑛𝛽
0,92 −𝜂
∗(1 + 𝑒𝑥𝑝(0.036𝑇𝑖𝑛−1.207 ∗54.4)) 
6.2 
==PAGE:156==
152 
 
 
 
Where Tin is the turbine inlet temperature in [K].

tabulated below:

The obtained 𝑃𝐸𝐶𝑡𝑢𝑟𝑏  for Turbine 1 was equal to 7580724.35 $. If adjusted for inflation 
from 1995 to date, the cost was increased to 16038672.4 $. For Turbine 2, 𝑃𝐸𝐶𝑡𝑢𝑟𝑏 was 
5436739.357 $ prior to inflation and was increased to 11502605.5 $ after inflation 
adjustments.

Finally, the Cost relation for the motor was taken from M. Morandin et al 
(doi:10.1016/j.energy.2013.05.038): 
 
𝑃𝐸𝐶𝑚𝑜𝑡𝑜𝑟= 5000 + 110 ∗𝑊̇  
6.3 
 
Where 𝑊̇  is power in [kW]. For the Heat pump it was equal to 98350 kWel (nominal load), 
and the resulting PEC was found to be 10823500 $ (before inflation) and 16721571.3 $ 
(after inflation).  
 
The total PEC for the Heat Pump was then obtained by summing the individual PEC of all 
components. The total PEC was estimated to be equal to 145112754 $. The total PEC was 
assumed to be 80% of the total Heat Pump CAPEX, while the remaining 20% was used to 
account for Indirect and other contingency costs. The CAPEX was estimated to be 
181,390,943 $. For comparison, the attained CAPEX from [48] (which was finally used in 
the Economic Model) was 188107602 $.
